THE RED PAGE.
BALLAD OF THE WOMEN OF PARIS
By Francois Villon.
Albeit the Venice girls get praise
For their sweet speech and tender air,
And though the old women have nice way»
Of chaffering for amorous ware,
Yet at my peril dare I swear,
Search Rome, where God’s grace mainly tarries,
Florence and Savoy, everywhere,
There’s no good girl’s lip out of Paris.
The Naples women, as folk prattle,
Are sweetly spoken and subtle enough :
German girls are good at tattle,
And Prussians make their boast thereof}
Take Egypt for the next remove,
Or that waste land the Tartar harries,
Spain or Greece, for the matter of love.
There’s no good girl’s lip out of Paris.
Breton and Swiss know nought cf the matter,
Gascony girls or girls of Toulouse;
Two fishwives there with a half hour's chatter
Would shut them up by threes and twos ;
Calais, Lorraine, and all their crews,
(Names enow the mad song marries)
England and Picardy, search them and choose,
There's no good girl’s lip out of Paris.
Prince, give praise to our French ladies
For the sweet sound their speaking carries ;
’Twixt Rome and Cadiz many a maid is,
But no good girl’s lip out of Paris.
Trans, by A. C. Swinburne.
It seems odd that Stephen Crane, an American,
should receive so little honor in his own country.
Recognition of the value of ‘ The Red Badge of
Courage ’ was instantaneous in England, yet
even now American criticism of it seems hard and
grudging. Some of Crane’s short stories are said
to have been refused by ‘Harper’s,’ ‘The Cen-
tury,’and a score of the best magazines in the
States. Yet it is difficult to understand how any
capable reader could miss seeing their fresh,
strong, indubitable genius. Whitman suggested
that the American public preferred verse which
was ‘either the poetry of an elegantly weak sen-
timentalism, at bottom nothing but maudlin
puerilities or more or less musical verbiage, aris-
ing out of a life of depression and enerva-
tion as their result; or else that class
of poetry, plays, &c., of which the found-
ation is feudalism, with its ideas of lords
and ladies, its imported standard of gen-
tility, and the manners of European high-life
below-stairs in every line and verse.’ This state-
ment has lost little of its essential truth in the
passage of forty years; and the effects of the mental
attitude and capacity which it implies extend to
prose as well as verse. It is singular how bad is
the literary taste of the mass of readers in the
Great Republic ; and how weak are the minds
which topple in thousands at the shock of
ephemeral crazes.
• * * *
The Society of Artists (Sydney) is not too well
satisfied with the results of its experimental
auction of produots. The second day’s sale, in
particular, was a very sad proceeding. There
were a few painters, and a few brokers, and a
very few of the public whom it was desired
to stimulate into patronage of High Art at Low
Prices. While the company was waiting for the
auctioneer a couple of haughty, well-dressed
ladies entered, sniffed at the blanks on the wall,
and remarked audibly, ‘ O dear, everything good
has gone. We’d better go too.’ Then they
Haughtily rustled out, followed by the reproachful
glances of the painters. Then a prosperous person
entered, button-holed a painter friend, assured
himself that one of the friend’s dozen pictures
had been sold, and loudly bewailed his own ill-luck.
‘No! he had set hie heart on the one ; he would
have nothing to do with the eleven.’ Then the
auctioneer came in breezily, and proceeded to
knock down impressionistic masterpieces athalf-a-
guinea and a guinea in jovial fashion- -
‘Gentlemen, I’m not saying anything about the merit
of these works of Art; you probably know more about
that than I do.’
Chorus of painters : ‘Wedo ! We do! ’
Voice of auctioneer: ‘“A Quiet Afternoon.” Only a
guinea and a-half offered for “ A Quiet Afternoon.” I
would give a good deal more myself fora quiet afternoon.’
Chorus of painters : ‘ What disgusting prices! Grand
idea this auction sale for us poor devils ! ’
Voice of auctioneer : ‘A bust! a miniature bust! only
feur shillings I’m offered for a bust. It’s the cheapest
bust I’ve ever had anything to do with.’ (Laughter of
brokers.)
Chorus of public as sale ends: ‘Rubbish, these pictures!
They can’t be any good or they would have brought
bigger prices. Anyhow’ (illogically), ‘we've got some
bargains.’
O Art ! Art ! Art!

Pagan: According to ‘The Saturday Review,’
an English parson brings Northern Nansen to
task for his ‘godless’ book. There is not one
mention of an Almighty in hundreds of pages.
This is the last thing the ordinary reader would
miss, but—the tradesman’s eye ! Nansen should
have taken a hint from H. M. Stanley’s prefatory
Pharisaisms (how well he knew J. Bull \) —
Constrained at the darkest hour to humbly confess
that without God’s help I was helpless, I vowed a vow in
the forest solitudes that I would confess His aid before
men.
Nansen will know better next time. After eaGh
florid piece of soenio description, and after each
account of marvellous escape, he will place a foot-
note at the bottom of his diary’s page: ‘ Here
thank God for his wondrous works and the cer-
tainty of a second edition. ’
H. G. Wells commences in the magazines
what promises to be the most brilliant of the
scientific romances with which his name is asso-
ciated. The idea is a simple one : all the planets
are cooling as the sun’s heat lessens, and Mars,
35,000,000 miles farther from the sun than Earth
is, has grown so cold that the highly-intelligent
inhabitants perceive themselves threatened with
extinction. So they resolve to migrate to Earth,
and fire at our attractive planet hollow shells
filled with warriors, like the Trojan horse. As
the story commences, one shell has hit; and the
Mars-men are commencing to make things lively
with heat-rays which have all the destructive
force of Lytton’s vril. It would be a nice exercise
for the imaginative students at our local Univer-
sities to continue the story from the point where
Wells leaves off.
Katharine Tynan in ‘The Academy ’ —
People engaged in literary work will understand the
difference between a literary reputation and a public one.
Those whom writers, in their own circles, delight to
honour in more or less degree are just those whose names
have never reached the outside public. Yet in those
circles all work of real merit is known and praised, and it
is but the beginning of the wave that will carry a reputa-
tion in ever-widening circles farther than we can see. Of
course, there are always critics who are true tasters,
though the noise of the crowd may temporarily prevent
their being heard. I would say that critics to-day, as a
class, are men and women who honestly and humbly
endeavour to serve literature. Of ‘ log-rolling ’ I have
no knowledge and no experience. Critics are gentler
nowadays than of old. They w : ll not trample the life out
of a Keats, or scarify subjects too robust to die of their
treatment. That many of them go wrong is inevitable.
The noble pleasure of praising intoxicates some out of
discretion, but at least it is a nobler form of failure than
the criticism that lay in wait to bludgeon genius and
garrot young talent.

15 —‘The Railway Commissioner has authorised the car-
riage of fodder for cattle and sheep from Melbourne or

20 per cent, below the ordinary charges,’ &c. Do we

♦
George Meredith, in his recently-published
‘E<say on Comedy,’says, as nearly as he dare,
thu„ the Englishman is an oaf who doesn’t know
the comic spirit from a Cheshire cheese. In the
Saturday Review Bernard Shaw points Meredith’s
parable—
‘The English public,’ says Meredith, ‘have the basis of
the comic in them: an esteem for common sense.’ This
flattering illusion does not dupe Mr. Meredith com-
pletely; for I notice that he adds ‘taking them gener-
ally.’ But if it were to be my last word on earth I must
tell Mr. Meredith to his face that whether you take them
generally or particularly—whether in the lump, or sec-
tionally as playgoers, churchgoers, voters, and what not
—they are everywhere united and made strong by the
bond of their common nonsense, their invincible deter-
mination to tell and be told lies about everything, and
their power of dealing acquisitively and successfully with
facts whilst keeping them, like disaffected slaves, rigidly
in their proper place: that is, outside the moral con-
sciousness. The Englishman is the most successful
man in the world simply because he values success-
meaning money and social precedence—more than any-
thing else, especially more than fine art, his attitude
towards which, culture-affectation apart, is one of half
diffident, half contemptuous curiosity ; and, of course,
more than clear-headedness, spiritual insight, truth,
justice, and so forth. It is precisely this unscrupulous*
ness and singleness of purpose that constitutes the
Englishman’s pre-eminent ‘common sense’; and this
sort of common sense, I submit to Mr. Meredith, is not
only not ‘the basis of the comic,’ but actually makes
comedy impossible, because it would not seem like com-
mon sense at all if it were not self-satisfiedly conscious of
its moral and intellectual bluntness, whereas the function
of comedy is to dispel such unconsciousness by turning
the search-light, of the keenest moral and intellectual
analysis right on to it.
• * • •
Now the Frenchman, the Irishman, the American, the
ancient Greek, is disabled from this true British common
sense by intellectual virtuosity, leading to a love of
accurate and complete consciousness of things—of intel-
lectual mastery of them. This produces a positive enjoy-
ment of disillusion (the most dreaded and hated of cala-
mities in England), and consequently a love of comedy
(the fine art of disillusion) deep enough to make huge
sacrifices of dearly idealised institutions to it. Thus, in
France, Molibre was allowed to destroy the Marquises. In
England he could not have shaken even such titles as the
accidental sheriff's knighthood of the late Sir Augustus
Harris. And yet the Englishman thinks himself much
more independent, level-headed, and genuinely republi-
can than the Frenchman—not without good superficial
reasons; for nations with the genius of comedy often
carry all the snobbish ambitions and idealist enthusiasms
of the Englishman to an extreme which the Englishman
himself laughs at. But they sacrifice them to comedy, to
which the Englishman sacrifices nothing ; so that, in the
upshot, aristocracies, thrones and churches go by the
board at the attack of comedy among our devotedly con-
ventional, loyal and fanatical next-door neighbours,
whilst we, having absolutely no disinterested regard for
such institutions, draw a few of their sharpest teeth, and
then maintain them determinedly as part of the machinery
of worldly success.
Besides, the Englishman is a serious person : that is, he
is firmly' persuaded that his prejudices and stupidities
are the vital material of civilisation, and that it is only
by holding on to their moral prestige with thesliffesfi
resolution that the world is saved from flying back into
savagery and gorilladom, which he always oonceives, in
spite of natural history, as a condition of lawlessness and
promiscuity, instead of, as it actually is, the extremity,
long since’grown unbearable, of his own notions of law
and order, morality and conventional respectability. Thus
he is a moralist, an ascetic, a Christian, a truth-teller,
and a plain dealer by profession and by conviction;
and it is wholly against this conviction that, judged
by his own canons, he finds himself in practice a great
rogue, a liar, an unconscionable pirate, a grinder of
the face of the poor, and a libertine. Mr. Meredith
points out daintily that the cure for this self-treasonable
confusion and darkness is Comedy, whose spirit overhead
will ‘look humanely malign and cast an oblique light on
them, followed by volleys oC silvery laughter.’ Yes, Mr.
Meredith ; but suppose the patients have ‘ commonsense *
enough not to want to be cured! Suppose they realise
the immense commercial advantage of keeping their ideal
life and their practical business life in two separate con-
soienoe-tight compartments, which nothing but ‘the
Comic Spirit ’ can knock into one ! Suppose, therefore,
they dread the Comic Spirit more than anything else in
the world, shrinking from its ‘illumination,’ and con-
sidering its ‘ silvery laughter ’ in execrable taste!
* • • *
Surely in doing so they are only carrying out the
common-sense view, in whicn an encouragement aad en-
joyment of comedy must appear c. 9 silly and suicidal and
‘ unEnglish ’ as the conduct of the man who sets Are to
his own house for the sake of seeing the flying sparks,
the red glow in the sky, the fantastic shadows on the
walls, the excitement of the crowd, the gleaming charge
of the engines, and the dismay of the neighbors.
No doubt the day will come when we shall deliberately
burn a London street every day to keep our city
*up to date in health and handsomeness, with no
more misgivings as to our common sense than we now
have when sending our clothes to the laundry every
week. When that day comes, perhaps comedy will
be popular too; for, after all, the function of comedy,
as Mr. Meredith after twenty years’ further considera-
tion is perhaps by this time ripe to admit, is nothing
less than the destruction of old - established morals.
Unfortunately, to-day such iconoclasm can be tolerated
by our playgoing citizpns only as a counsel of despair and
pessimism. T>iey can find a dreadful jov in it when it is
done seriously, or even grimly and terribly as they under-
stand Ibsen to be doing it; but that it should be done
with levity, with silvery laughter like the crackling of
thorns under a pot, is too scandalously wicked, too
cynical, too heartlessly shacking to be borne. Conse-
quently, our plays must either be exploitations of old-
established morals or tragic challengings of the order of
Nature. Reductions to absurdity, however logical; ban.
terings, however kindly ; irony, however delicate ; merri-
ment, however silvery, are out of the question in matters
of morality, excepting among men with a natural
appetite for comedy which must be satisfied at all cost
and hazards ; that is to say, not among the English play B
going public, which positively dislikes comedy.
• * * *
No doubt it is patriotically indulgent of Mr. Meredith
to say that ‘Our English school has not clearly imagined
society,’ and that ‘ of the mind hovering above congre-
gated men and women it has imagined nothing.’ But is
he quite sure that the audiences of our English school do
not know too much about society and ‘ congregated men
and women ’ to encourage any exposures from ‘ the vigi-
lant Comig,’ with its ‘ thoughtful laughter,’ its ‘ oblique
illumination,’ and the rest? . . Is it clear that the
Comic Spirit would break into silvery laughter if it saw
all that the nineteenth century has to show it beneath the
veneer ? There is Ibsen, for instance : he is not lacking,
one judges, in the Comic Spirit; yet his laughter does
not sound very silvery, does it? No: if this were an age
for comedies, Mr. Meredith would have been asked for
one before this. How would a comedy from him be re-
lished, I wonder, by the people who wanted to have the
revisers of the Authorised Version of the Bible prose-
cuted for blasphemy 'jecause they corrected as many of
its mistranslations as they dared, and who reviled Froude
for not suppressing Carlyle’s diary and writing a fictitious
biography of him, instead of letting out the truth 1
Comedy, indeed !
Writes ‘J. Jingle ’ —
‘ Yellowbush ’ says ‘ we shall not see Australia as it is
until we have separated the immigrant from the native
author.’ How are they to be separated? Must the immi-
grant return to his native soil before versifying his
Australian impressions ? Must he prefix eo his verses
—‘These are only foreign ideas’—so that the native
readers may not be led into thinking the poem Australian,
when the author learned to read and write in Spain or in
the North of Ireland some twei ty years before ? A man
need not necessrrily be born in Egypt to paint a truthful
picture of a portion of the right bank of the Nile. Why,
then, must he be born in Australia in order to truthfully
pourtray, in verse, an action or a scene occurring in
Australia?
I do not claim Gordon as an Australian poet. What I
do claim is that, together with his other work, Gordon
wrote some typioal Australian poetry. As a bush poem
the * Sick Stockrider ’ stands alone. Where is described
so well the glorious rush of joy-in-living that runs
now and then through the hard life of the ca!tie-man?
And if an average man were born forty times over into
this exceptionally horsey country, could he write a de-
scription of a race to equal Gordon’s * Melbourne Cup ’ ?
Similarly, ‘ Geoffrey Hamlyn ’ is none the less a purely
Australian novel, though it is written by an Englishman.
You are born in England, emigiate at 18; at 30 you pro-
duce a novel. In it there are graphio descriptions of
Australian scenery ; the dialogue reeks of the bush, even
to the blankies; and the characters are Australian to a
man, woman, and child. Lastly, the book is a huge suc-
cess. Say, is it an English or an Australian work?
Australian, by St. Blanky !
A COLOR SCHEME.
( Showing the fate that overtakes the average girl artist).
’Twas in the Latin Quarter
That pretty Susan Worth
To her painting class each morning
Along the street tripp’d forth.
Now, there was a jolly artist
Lived in the Rue Sufflot,
Who daily to the Beaux Arts
With pots and paints did go.
So they often passed each other
At the early hour of eight,
But her thoughts they dwelt on colors,
His on technique up to date.
(Interval of two short montht).
When last I saw sweet Susan,
Au cinquierne au droit c,
Smilingly she welcomed me,
And happy seem’d her lot.
* Of course, you keep up painting V
She gently shook her head.
‘ My Jack is a great artist,
He paints for both,’ she said.
‘ Well, then, you draw a little,
Sketch a bit, as sketching goes ?'
‘ No !—dear Jack needs a model,
And so ’ —she said —‘ I pose.’
Kate Adatr
in ‘ The Quartier Latin.'

VELVET. Simple and good is your motto ;
you’re almost a Quaker, though not quite, and
you’d rather see your money in the bank than on
your back, and dividends are better, say you, than
Ribbons and Laces. Well, have your own way.
We provide for all sorts and sizes.
Or perhaps you are of another class. A world-
ling, if you will, floating down the stream of life,
with “youth at the prow and pleasure at the
helm,” you want to dress well and to look well.

win their smiles. You want Clothes and Hats
and Boots, Gloves you must have, and a thousand
other things you crave for. Rings, Lockets,

CIGARS, and TOBACCO of the best. You’d
like them all, wouldn’t you ? You can have them,
sir, at our store for what is, when compared to
what other stores are asking, a mere song.

ROSAMOND BENHAM.
WEST OF THE WORLD.
West of the World ! all red suns sleep
a Hee °y car Pet of crimson cloud,
And the weary winds from the eastward creep
xo their shining goal in the western steep,
in the golden arms of the starry crowd
West of the World.
World ! all true hearts ride
10 a further bourne than the best have trod,
lul they cross the last creek gleaming wide
And wave their hands from the last divide ’
Ere they drop their load at the feet of God-
West of the World.
West of the World ! all dead hopes drift
On the heaving heart of the hiding Day
lo the clinging shadows that show no rift,
With a lingering step that is all too swift
1 1 or the eyes that follow their trackless way—
West of the World.
GLENROWAN.
MY WEIRD.
Oh ! stern accusing Eyes of Hate,
Your terrors on my slumbers wait!
You haunt my days, dread Eyes of Fate !
It was not so, Girl, years agone :
Then the dear lovelight softly shone
In twin stars, lit by love alone.
And if I vowed my love would last,
I knew not then love could be past;
When smiles are gone, tears hold not fast.
You loved ; I wearied; now, you know,
In silence where deep waters flow
Your Hate holds what your Love let go.
The white face with the darkened eyes,
With child’s look, calmly, sadly wise,
They hid it where the rushes rise.
So strangely like you, I might deem,
I saw your face, Girl! in that dream
Of a pale face beside a stream.
But your last 100k —it had not worn
The fury of a woman’s scorn :
’Twas but a dream, of madness born.
A dream ! but yet I surely know
Two dark eyes watch where’er I go,
With dead hate in their smouldering glow.
Will the old love that made Youth sweet
Flash from dark eyes a greeting fleet
When you and I and Death shall meet ?
IAN GLEN.
FOUR FEASTS.
I dug in my garden plot to-day,
And now and again desisting,
I watched the earthworms, brisk and gay,
In the deep black loam of the yellow clay,
Crawling and wriggling and twisting.
And I softly laughed : “Ha ha ! ho ho !
You’ve been having a fine feast down below
In the richest spot
Of my garden plot! ”
And the worms kept wriggling and twisting.
My ducks came out with a “Quack, quack, quack,”
And after me, waddling and wobbling;
They followed closely my spade’s fresh track,
And I saw by a glance behind my back
How the worms they were greedily gobbling.
And again I laughed : “Ha ha ! ho ho !
Eat on, my beauties, and fatter grow ;
For the worms are sweet,
And they’re cheaper than wheat! ”
And the ducks kept gobbling and gobbling.
In fancy I saw, some weeks ahead,
Those ducks grow fatter and fatter,
And I thought of a glorious Christmas “spread,”
When a couple should grace my table’s head
In a gravy-brimming platter !
And I merrily laughed : “ Ha ha ! ho ho !
My time for feasting will come, I know ;
I’ll be fairly in luck
With a fine roast duck ! ”
I was counting my chicks—no matter !
Then a graver thought came over my soul,
And gloom took the place of jesting ;
For a sombre vision before me stole
Of a sexton digging a deep black hole
For my worn-out body’s resting.
And I laughed no longer, but sighed : “ That’s so »
The ducks and myself to the earth must go.
And the ‘ Conqueror Worm ’
Shall wriggle and squirm
And enjoy the final feasting.”
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.

SATURDAY, JUNE 12, 1897.
The Great Ikey Mo Problem.
Something of a rather vague and misty
character has happened to the gambling
industry of New South Wales, and, for the
moment, a bewildered Ikey Mo can’t fully
realise the situation. Hitherto, the letter of
the cornstalk law on the subject of betting
has been very clear and precise, but a quaint
interpretation has been cast upon it by the
courts, which entirely altered its apparent
meaning. According to the statute, it is
illegal, in a general sort of way, to use any
place for betting purposes. A place, in the
popular acceptation of the term, is any region
on the earth, or the sea, or below or above
either of them ; according to the customary
reading, if any man is engaged in betting, or
in anything else for that matter, or if he
merely exists without being engaged in any-
thing, that fact is sufficient evidence that he
is in a place ; the idea of a human being who
is in no place at all is beyond human com-
prehension. Nevertheless, the law decided
that a shop was a place but that a racecourse
was not; therefore shop-betting was illegal
and racecourse-betting was perfectly lawful
and justifiable. This interpretation of the
law lasted for many years, but quite recently
a British court created a new precedent,
which has broken out in New South Wales
with great virulence. It decided that a race-
course was a place after all, therefore race-
course-betting is also illegal. On the strength
of this, the New South Wales law has
descended with a crash of sudden enthusiasm
on bookmakers who bet upon racecourses for
cash, while those who bet on credit are, so
far, left to carry on their business with im-
punity, for some reason not too well defined.
The new reading of the law, so far as The
Bulletin can see, is hardly important
enough to merit all the excitement it has
created. Betting in shops or offices kept for
the purpose has been admittedly illegal for
many years, yet there are about a hundred
well - known betting establishments and
“totes” in and around Sydney. Some of
them have offices in the principal business
streets ; many have their names in large
letters on the brilliantly-lighted window to
let the police know that they are there ; and
quite a number are—or were—wont to regu-
larly advertise their addresses and their un-
lawful trade in The Bulletin and other
papers, so that the police might know where
to go and summon them six times every
week or oftener. Yet nothing happens to
them except that they are fined a small
amount each about once in three years,
as an average. Therefore, it is evident
that, with the kind of police force
and magistracy and Parliament and Minis-
try that New South Wales possesses, it
really matters next to nothing whether betting
is legal or not. On the strength of the new
decision a temporary show is being made of
/suppressing the cash Ikey Mo who plies his
business on the racecourse, but presumably
this burst of energy will also die away, and
the illegal Ikey Mo on the racecourse will
have the same impunity as the illegal Ikey
Mo in the shop. By-and-bye it will pro-
bably be found that the credit Isaac Mo is
as illegal as his cash compatriot, and then
another temporary panic will strike the com-
munity, and for a little while there will be
another rush of legal proceedings and small
fines. Then, presumably, that excitement
will also die away, and the four gay male-
factors—the shop Mo, the small Mo who
keeps a tote, the racecourse cash Mo, and
the credit Mo—will march abreast placid and
unmolested. That is to say, they will be
fined once in three years or so as a small
matter of form, and nothing more.
* ♦ ♦ *
The shop Ihey Mo is not obliterated by
the strong arm of the law, because the police
systematically leave him alone—presumably
in consideration of valuable “tip” received.
The God- fearing Ministry doesn’t inquire
why he is left alone, presumably because it
doesn’t care whether the law is broken and
the police corrupted or not, and also, pre-
sumably, because some members of it are
themselves notoriously of a sporting turn.
The racecourse cash-Hebrew will, presum-
ably, be left alone also, when things simmer
down, provided he also tips the force liberally
and regularly. So will the credit-Hebrew
when his turn comes. Beyond the fact that
there will be more tip and more corruption,
the new departure, so far as The Bulletin
can see, makes no difference whatever. It
only means that the mean and dirty and
venal system which so many N.S.W. Govern-
ments have winked at will continue to be
winked at, only the venality will be more
extensive and the wink consequently larger.
* * * *
If, however, racecourse betting on credit
is, by some accident of the law, really not
illegal, while cash betting is, then the only
thing which any consistent Ministry can do
is to introduce new legislation making both
either lawful or unlawful. If it makes them
both lawful, then it has no earthly excuse
for not legalising the totalisator, which is
simply a bookmaker in another guise—only
one that gives fair odds and never welshes or
uses unpleasant language. And if it makes
all these lawful, then there is no excuse for
not legalising lotteries under proper super-
vision, and making both them and the tota-
lisator pay a proportion of their profits to
the State Treasury. In fact, there is no
excuse for not legalising lotteries, even apart
from the bookmaker question. The N.S.W.
Government shifted “ Tattersall ” out of
the province, but it makes no effort to
prevent his consultations being advertised in
the Sydney and other papers, and though it
stopped letters going through the post to
“Tattersall” in Sydney it doesn't even
ask Parliament for authority to prevent
letters from New South Wales going to the
same individual in Hobart. It allows the
Cornstalk to gamble in lottery-tickets just as
freely after “ Tattersall’s ” expulsion as
before, and it carries the letters and money
of that sinful person in its mails, and is his
partner and agent ; therefore it is itself a
godless, gambling Ministry whose virtuous
professions on the subject are a matter of
ridicule. If, on the other hand, it makes
credit-bookmaking illegal in order to bring
it in line with the same sin when done on a
cash basis, The Bulletin respectfully sug-
gests that the GoD-fearing Ministry should
cease to be a blatant and time-serving hypo-
crite over the matter. Let it take steps to
ensure that the police will institute a
separate prosecution against every book-
maker and betting-shop-keeper and “tote”
man for every day he pursues his business,
and have him fined six times a week regu-
larly so long as he continues in the trade.
Let it get Parliamentary authority to stop
carrying “Tattersall’s” letters and cir-
culars through the mails and cease taking the
wages of sin from that individual in the
shape of postages. Let it shut down on all
gambling advertisements, and then it will at
least be honest, even if it is very stupid
and wholly impracticable.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s view of the matter is that
gambling can’t be repressed, and that it is
very doubtful if it ought to be. Gambling
may be an evil ; so probably are drink
and tobacco, and opium, and horse-racing,
and war, and many other things that waste
time and money to no purpose. But if all
these pleasant vices were abolished the
muddy stagnation that would ensue—the
howling of the mental desolation that is to
come—the placid, cow-like calm of a blame-
less life that would settle down upon every-
body, might prove an evil 20 times more
objectionable. Therefore, this paper believes
that suppression is neither possible nor
advisable, and with that conviction on its
mind it advocates State regulation instead.
Its theory is that the totalisator should be
allowed on every racecourse under proper
supervision and provided the . racing club
pays to the State an annual license fee and a
share of its v percentage on the money in-
vested. It believes that a limited number of
totalisators should be allowed in Sydney and
other large towns under the same conditions
to accommodate gamblers who don’t care to
attend races. Further, it has an idea that
the State should either authorise some
wealthy and responsible person or co.
or syndicate to run lotteries in the province,
on condition that the State gets a percentage,
or else that it should run them itself ; and,
finally, that it should license a certain num-
ber of bookmakers in order to provide an
outlet for that class of betting which the
tote does not accommodate. And, haying
thus provided means for honest gambling,
that it should shut down on all unlicensed
gambles remorselessly.
* * * *
The Bulletin advocates this policy because
it believes that gambling is irrepressible and
not wholly objectionable. But as the
Gon-fearing Ministry of New South Wales
holds that it is wholly objectionable,
and that it can be repressed, this
paper respectfully asks it to act up to
its convictions. As a step towards this end,
it is hereby suggested that it should make
credit bookmaking illegal, should increase
the minimum fine upon the tote-keeper,
betting-shop man, and the Ikey Mo (whether
he bets for cash or otherwise) to £lOO, and
give a third of the amount to the informer in
order to make sure that the law will be en-
forced, and should get Parliamentary autho-
rity to stop carrying “Tattersall’s” cor-
respondence, and also to shut dow T n on every
paper which publishes his advertisements.
This would show that it really believes
gambling should be and can be put down,
and also that it seriously desires to put it
down. If the attempt succeeded, well and
good. If it failed, then some method of
regulation could be tried in its stead.
The Cornstalk Railway Deficit.
Ox no less than 39 lines the earnings fall below actual
working expenses. . . It was by the expenditure of
borrowed money upon works of this kind that the great
Protectionist boom was vamped up, and Victoria turned
into a temporary “ paradise of labor.” . . The money
was simply squandered in creating a state of fictitious
prosperity, of which Protection was given the credit. No
doubt we have similar sins of our own to answer for, but
they are on nothing approaching the Victorian scale. . .
In the other colonies it is the same, more or less, but the
case of Victoria with her 39 railways, all of which would
be dear at a gift, is quite unique. —Sydney Telegraph,
17th May, 1896.
It is one of the standing legends of New South
Wales that it has the best-paying railway
system in the six eastern provinces of Austra-
lasia. This is ascribed partly to the magnifi-
cent wealth of the country, and parlly to the
prosperity naturally created by Freetrade, and
partly to other things. What New South
Wales is especially proud of is that its railway
system is not like that of the other provinces—
especially not like that of Victoria. In Vic-
toria the railway deficit is published every year
or much oftener; also, the list of railways
which don’t pay working expenses and interest,
and the loss upon each ; and the demand that
the railway deficit shall be abolished, or that
somebody shall be sacrificed for not abolishing
it, is loud and incessant. And the Syd-
ney press writes articles like that quoted
above and “ feels good ” over the matter.
Meanwhile the local deficit is never, so far as
The Bulletin is aware, published in any
paper, or alluded to in Parliament, or men-
tioned in the Financial Statement, or set forth
in any of the Statistician’s publications, and
everybody takes it for granted that no such
thing exists, or if it does exist it isn’t worth
mentioning.
* * * *
As the Telegraph truly observes, there
are 39 Victorian railways which don’t pay
expenses; and it might also add 19 more
to that list without deviating from the
truth to any extent worth mentioning. The
total loss on these 58 lines, according to the
latest published account, reached the immense
sum of £334,228 per annum. As an expression
of the other side of the case, the following is
the list of unprofitable New South Wales lines,
as supplied on page 451 of the “ Wealth and
Progress of New South Wales,” and the losses
thereon for the year ended June, 1896 :

There is nothing in these figures to be especially
proud of; in fact, the list is among the most
lamentable things of its kind in Australasia.
And yet the Sydney Foreign-trade organ, for
the sake of boosting up its imported calico
policy, weeps over the Victorian boom rail-
ways and states proudly that the cornstalk
trouble is on “nothing approaching the Vic-
torian scale.”

caused by drought or other accidental causes
Ihe figures supplied go back four years. For
1892 there were thirteen railways on the deficit
list, and the total loss was £311,000. Next
year there were sixteen, and the shortage
expanded to £324,000. Then, in 1894 the
losing lines had increased to nineteen and the
loss to £358,000 ; while for the year ended June,
1896, there were again nineteen, but the loss
had increased to £374,000.
. • • *
Still the general supposition in the province
is that the New South Wales railways are, on
the whole, a paying investment. But the
Auditor-General blights this idea in one act.
On page 197 of his report for 1892 he sets forth
that the total loss on the railway system from
its beginning in 1850 until the end of 1891
(41 years) was £6,6/1,834. At 30th -June,
1896, the loss had increased to £7,735,213, sc
the average loss for the last \\ years wa>
£236,306 per annum. The last year, fron
June, 1895, to June, 1896, was apparently 'he
worst of all, the loss, as shown on page 208 of
the Auditor-General’s latest report, being
£321,690. Between ’92 and ’96 the gross
revenue declined by £338,000, despite the fact
that over 330 miles of new line were opened in
the interval. The moral, apparently, is that,
taking an average of the last few’ years, there
is very little indeed to choose between the
deficits of the two provinces. The principal
difference seems to lie in the varying degree of
publicity given to the shortages.
• » • *
The old, weary, fiscal controversy has pro-
bably a great deal to do with the prevailing
misconception. For some unexplained reason,
Victoria is to the Cornstalk the typical Pro-
tectionist country— the Lord alone knows why,
seeing that both Tasmania and Maoriland are
more distinctly Protectionist than the place of
the exploded land-boom. New .South Wales,
on the other hand, is the typical Freetrade pro-
vince, and the struggle to manufacture proofs
of the extra blessedness of that policy is very
keen. Victoria has practically no Freetrade
party within its own borders, therefore the
Victorian press isn’t under the perpetual neces-
sity to concoct evidence in defence of Protection;
when it does concoct it, w'hich is frequently,it is
mainly from love of controversy. The domi-
nant party in New South Wales, however, has
a strong Protectionist minority to confront in
its own territory, and in the struggle to keep
the hostile faith from spreading it has to be
constantly constructing evidences of the
material advantages of Foreign-trade, and pro-
ducing real or imaginary facts about the pro-
sperity in its own province and the ruin in the
province next door, and about the difference be-
tween New South Wales now and the same pro-
vince in the days of the “ Dibbs deficit.” And
one result of the legend that has been built up
about the surprising prosperity of the New
South Wales railway system and the awful
state (owing to Protection) of the adjacent
systems, is that the cornstalks refused almost
unanimously to hear of any proposition for
federating their lines with those of the other
provinces. There is no visible reason why they
should have refused ; on the Auditor-General’s
showing, the New South Wales deficit can
hold its own reasonably well with those
of its neighbours. But the legend had
somehow taken root, and as a conse-
quence one of the most important advan-
tages of Federation is likely to be thrown away.
The N.S.W. representatives were not wholly,
or anything like wholly, responsible for the
disastrous resolution arrived at in the Conven-
tion ; still they were so far responsible for it
that their vote, if cast in the Federal instead
of the provincial interest, might very probably
have turned the scale. And but for the widely-
accepted legend that the New’ South Wales
railway system was a concern of great profit
which no sane person would dream of casting to
the adjacent swine of the other provinces, the
matter might have been view ed very differently.
That stupid legend has a vast amount to answer
for.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Remedy for Deadlocks.
When the Federal Constitution was under dis-
cussion one matter on which the Progressive
and Conservative elements diverged widely
was the question of deadlocks between
two branches of the Legislature. The Pro-
gressive party held that, when the two
Houses were hopelessly at variance on any
point, there should be an arbiter of some sort to
decide between them ; the Conservatives held
that the matter should be allow’ed to settle
itself by blasphemy or public agitation or foot-
ball scrum, or else to remain permanently
unsettled. The case for the Conservative party
is explained by the Australasian as follows
It should be plain to anyone who takes up the inquiry
without prejudice that deadlocks result from our setting'
out with the proposition that all laws shall require the
assent of two Houses of Legislature, and then proceeding
tc encourage one of these two Houses to consider that it
has the right to demand the passing of any Act it chooses
to set its heart upon. A contradiction in the constitution
gives rise to deadlocks. The people have never yet been
prepared in any country enjoying constitutional govern-
ment to hand themselves over body and soul to a single
chamber, yet every deadlock has arisen out of the claim
of one House to legislate as a single chamber. If the
remedy for deadlocks, under the Federal Constitution, is
to take the shape of machinery for enabling the
House of Representatives to get its own way,
when it thinks that it has the popular vote with
it, what need is there for any Upper House at all?
This is the Tory case put in a nutshell. It is
beautifully and refreshingly illogical, and there-
fore it is all the more Tory. The proposition
that “ All laws require the assent of two
Houses of Legislature ” is absolutely true ; the
rest of the case is bunkum. There are two
matters—not merely one—to be considered ;
the law which it is proposed to enact, and the
old law or the old system which it is pro-
posed to supersede. A deadlock implies that
each House supports a different law. If, as has
happened more than once, the Vic. Assembly
passes a One-Man-One-Vote Bill and the Council
throws it out, that means that one House
assents to the proposed Manhood Suffrage idea
and the other assents to the existing Property
feutirage idea. But neither idea has the assent
of two Houses of Legislature ; one of them
might have had the assent of two previous
Houses of Legislature, but neither has it now.
Therefore the Australasia N.’s logic goes right
down in a heap. When neither idea has the
assent of both Houses then one is just as un-


constitutional as the other, and there is no
more reason why the old policy which one
House assents to should prevail than the new
policy which is assented to by the other House.
When it can be done there is no doubt that it is
advisable to have the assent of both Houses.
But it can’t always be done ; the existence of a
deadlock on any point whatever is proof that
some law is being kept in force unconstitutionally
by the assent of only one House, and, therefore,
the old law which the Tory paper wants to pre-
serve is, by the Tory paper’s own showing, as
utterly indefensible as the new one which the
Democratic parly wants to enforce. And if
neither party changes its sentiments, then, on
this one point, a law must exist by the assent
of one House only. And which one ? The
Tory idea is that the stagnant House should
always get the benefit of the doubt, and that
every law which exists by the assent of only
one House should do so by the assent of the
Tory House. The Democratic idea is to create
some third power to decide between them, so
that the vote of one House and of that third
power, whether it is the people or the two
Houses sitting as one, will decide the question.
The Bulletin believes that some remedy for
deadlocks is absolutely necessary for exactly the
same reason by which the Australasian tries
to prove that it is not necessary—because no
law should be enforced by the authority of one
House only. If having a remedy for deadlocks
means letting the House of Representatives
“ get its own way ” every time—which it
doesn’t— then it necessarily follows that not
having a remedy—the policy which the Tory
paper advocates —means letting the unrepre-
sentative House get its own way every time.
Therefore the Australasian gives itself away
horribly by its own words.
In re Gentleman One.
“ A Layman ” to The Bulletin :
“ Rus. Roberts ” correctly states the case when he
says that Gent. One is a court officer, and, in that capa-
city, subject to a jurisdiction “ to which no other class of
persons is subject." But he then compares that jurisdic-
tion to the jurisdiction possessed by the Public Service
Board, and argues that whereas the board, in its pre-
liminary inquiries into charges against an officer, does
not take the public into its confidence, so neither should
a court in like circumstances, where a solicitor is con-
cerned. That sort of reasoning could go on for ever, but
the public in this matter sees exactly what it wants,
and will get there, not by beating technical and
other windmills, but by asserting its own case thus ;
bet solicitors, if they want a professional tribunal to try
purely professional charges, get one as like the
Public Service Board as they wish. What that board
® a yd° with them in the initial, and generally even in
the final stages, will be to the public a matter of absolute
indifference. But if, as now, Judges who are paid to
administer public justioe occupy open courts and the
public time to point out that one set of defendants are to
be shielded from the publio gaze, because they are
solicitors, while another set, smirched with similar
charges, are to be thrown naked to the world, because
they are not solicitors, common sense cannot but revolt,
the present writer has reason to know that at
least one Judge on the N.S.W. Bench regards his
position in these transactions as anomalous. Many
solicitor-cases involve criminal misdeeds. In ordi-
nary jurisdictions, the Judge who in the course of
» trial becomes aware that perjury has been committed
Bends the supposed-perjurer for trial; and in bankruptcy
or commercial affairs, or in divorce affairs, if the case
unexpectedly discloses crime, he promptly commits or
forwards the papers to the Attorney-General’s office. But
lr \i j case ?>. an d they are numerous, in which the
mi- n solioitor’s affairs show him to have been a false-
afhdavit maker and criminal extortioner or holder-on of
clients moneys, these Judges—the public guardians of
equal-handed justice—suspend their “boy” or strike
, I *L the roll, but never say a word about com-
mitting him for his false affidavits or profes-
sional garrotting. “Rus” says clients have their
tw. 4 a^er the Judges are done with him.
mats just the aggravation. There’s nothing then
w go for. An 1 this most important point is raised by
inis remark of “ Rus” and others : If an aggrieved client
chose afterwards to prosecute an erring solicitor, would
not the question of seeking to find a man twice guilty
come to the front ? Would not the erring solicitor go to
him r : t r. ,al the ve rdict of guilty pronounced on
him in the Full Court hanging about his neck? Think
oack on a few of the cases in which solicitors have been
suspended or disrobed and this will be clear. The de-
mand then is either that the professional conduct of
sohmtors shall be sent to a board, as “ Rus ” suggests, or

Buckley’s Chance!
\\ rites M'Guire, from Melbourne, to The
Bulletin :
Seven weeks have passed since Buckley, the Melb. safe-
roooer, made an assertion from the dock at Melb.
Lnminal Court which east upon Yic. Penal Department a
siur of cruel and barbarous injustice. The allegation
" as one wn, ch Bucklby averred would be borne out by a
teference to the books of the department; but, though
such investigation could be easily made in ten minutes by
a small boy at 10s. a week, so far nothing has been pub-
Ushed either in support or disproof of the prisoner’s
word. Buckley was accused of safe-breaking, and, in
pleading guilty, he said that his whole career of crime,
extending over 12 years, was the outcome of a sentence
for assault and robbery he received when a boy of 17.
Buckley claimed that he was found guilty of this
ciime, and was sentenced to five years and three
oggings of 20 lashes each. He had served two
years and had gone through the torture of
two flagellations when evidence was forthcoming
in proof of his innocence. The Govt, of the day kindly
granted him a free-pardon, magnanimously remitted the
remaining scourging, and told Buckley, the man with the
scored back, he might go. No mention of compensation
was made, and though Buckley made efforts to obtain
s ° me redress for the humiliation he had passed through,
all he could get was an intimation that the Queen could
I™ no wrong and a snuffling injunction to lead a pious
life. That is Buckley’s story. Whether it is a lie told
for purposes of mitigating his sentence, or a solid-slab of
fact, is questionable, but the silence of the Vie. Penal
Dept, on the subject makes it possible that Buckley told
the truth. If so, the law has been the big brute it
usually is, but a bigger ass. If what Buckley says is true,
then the State that scarred Buckley’s back scarred
Buckley’s mind ten times as seriously, and, having trans-
formed the boy into the tiger, refused to give the tiger
the chance of becoming a man. A few issues ago there
was an inquiry on the “Red Page” of The Bulletin,
asking for the origin of the expression “ Buckley’s
chance.” How does this stand in the competition ?

Tasmanian Sweeps.
“ Raspberry Land ” to The Bulletin :
The regulations just issued by Tas. Governor-in-Council
dealing with lotteries under the Gaming Act of last year
seem most inefficient. It is set forth that those
desirous of going into the sweep business must deposit
with the Treasurer £lO,OOO by way of license, money to
be returned should the promoter relinquish said license.
All lotteries to be subject to the provisions of the Audit
Act; Govt, inspector to be present at all drawings ;
names of lucky ticket-holders not to be divulged to out-
siders. License may be revoked for bankruptcy of the
licensee or by his breaking any of the regulations or
the provisions of the Gaming Act. If the promoter be-
comes incapacitated Ministers are to appoint some person
or persons to carry on the business until the expiration of
the license. As no determining date is specified in the
license form gazetted with the regulations the licensee
(or the Govt, substitute for a gone-bung licensee) may, if he
likes, apparently run the show until the resurrection day.
But, apart from the slovenly construction of the regula-
lations and the monopoly given by the high figure de-
manded as deposit, their chief weakness seems to lie in
the arbitrary fixing of the amount totally regardless of
the size of the sweep man or the proportions of the public
risk. Under these beautiful conditions a perfectly
“square” sweep-promoter conducting a small business
would be absolutely crushed out of the field. At no one
time may he hold more than £3OOO of other people’s
money, and his profits may never exceed £2OO per annum,
yet he is asked to put up and lose the interest on £10.000!
Unless, therefore, a sweep-man can make a profit so high
that he won’t miss £3OO a year —interest on £lO,OOO at 3
per cent. —he must stay out of the business altogether.
It will hardly pay to lose a certain t 300 for an uncertain
£2OO. The big man may have £50,000 or more of clients’
money in hand at a stated time, yet he is just as
likely as the small man to button up on
the lot, and calmly refer ticket-holders to the Treasurer
for a four shillings in the pound redress. The whole
arrangement looks like a frantic desire on the part
of Govt, to bolster a monopoly, and suggests the edifying
spectacle of a large, voracious Fysh relentlessly pursuing
the small shrimp of i 300 extra revenue up a particularly
noisome sewer-pipe. Yet the legislators who allow this
sort of thing have hitherto stood aghast at the bare men-
tion of a State Lottery Department.
A Pair of Police-Constables.
At one o’clock on Sunday morning, March 14,
a man named Thomas Clarke —old, sick,
out of work was sleeping with other poor
fellows in Sydney Domain. He had just been
discharged from Camden Convalescent Hospital
—had no money, no food, no home. Up came
two Sydney policemen, Hammond and Cham-
bers, who had been spending a jolly evening at
the Hotel Metropole, and were apparently “ on
for a lark.” They were not on duty; they
were in plain clothes. And they—or one of
them —began pulling off the bags and news-
papers with which the miserables sleeping in
the park had tried to cover themselves.
Great fun, this !—a man never looks so silly as
when he’s woke out of a shivering nap—and to
see how quickly a full-fed policeman’s boot
tames the protests of an empty stomach ! —it
would make a cow laugh. But Clarke pro-
tested—Clarke was a new-comer who had seen
better days ; in spite of his age and his illness,
he still hid somewhere among his rags a spark
of manly spirit. Then Hammond— Senior-
Constable Hammond, sworn to preserve the
peace— Hammond, full of meat and beer,
rushed at Clarke, the weak and un-
fortunate, and struck him brutally on
the head. Clarke, who had doubted
whether his assailants police, knew it
now. He ran away ; Hammond followed ; and
the Technological Museum watchman, standing
as near as he dared, saw the blows and heard
the heavy thud ! thud ! thud ! as Hammond
knocked Clarke down and struck him re-
peatedly with his stick. “Help! help!”
cried the beaten wretch-it makes honest men’s
blood tingle —and Chambers stood and looked
on ; while the watchman and the broken-spirited
outcasts on the ground listened fearfully. They
guessed what they would get if they meddled.
Then Chambers called his comrade off, and he
came off. Clarke got Jp with his face stream-
ing with blood ; but there was grit left in him
yet. He followed Hammond and Chambers,
calling “ Police ! ” Constable M‘Q,uaker came
up, saw Clarke’S condition, and affirmed that
he was quite sober. At the police-court Ham-
mond and Chambers (his brother-in-law) lied
unblushingly. They said Clarke stopped
them and asked for a shilling and a match ;
Hammond refused, and pushed Clarke down,
when he cut his face. Two other witnesses
corroborated Clarke; and Hammond was com-
mitted for trial; while Chambers was dis-
charged—with the public congratulations of the
court officers 1 At a first trial of Hammond
the jury disagreed. At a second trial last week
he was found guilty and sentenced to six
months’ imprisonment. And now, after Chief
Justice Darley has declared that there was
“ gross perjury on one side or the other,” and
the jury has found that the perjury was on the
side of Chambers and Hammond— what is
going to happen to Senior-Sergeant Chambers,
who, though he asked Hammond to come off
his victim, did not take the victim’s part, and
swore point-blank that there was no assault at
all ? Also, what is going to happen to Ham-
mond for his perjury ? George Dean got 14
years for exactly the same offence.
The Stale Premier.
Apropos of John Downer’s election to the
leadership of the S.A. Opposition, it is one of
the quaint'features of Government by Contra-
diction that it is the minority that almost al-
ways elects the Premier. The Opposition is the
minority ; often a very small and discredited
and low-spirited minority indeed. This dis-
consolate fragment gets together and chooses a
new leader, because the old leader has either
thrown up the business in disgust, or has lost
his seat altogether, or is utterly discredited in the
general rout. By-and-bye, the wheel goes round
and the minority becomes a majority, and its
elected leader becomes Premier. But it isn’t
the majority which elects the Premier ; in most
cases at least it simply, through force of cir-
cumstances, gives in its adhesion to the man
already appointed. The Government is made
by the Premier, and the Premier is made
almost every time by the disgraced and rejected
and chucked-out scrag-end of a party which
chooses him for its head, and agrees to follow
him till the loaves and fishes come round again.
Reid and Dibbs were each made head of the
State by the vote of about one-third of the
N.S. W. Assembly. James Munro was made
Premier of Victoria by about a dozen votes or .
less; when the opponents of the Gillies
Government consisted of practically two men
and a dog, and the dog wasn’t feeling at all
well, they nominated Munro as their head
because that ordinary person was the best
of a very small and ordinary gathering.
When the party increased to about fifty
and came into office, Munro wasn’t the
best of that crowd by a very long way, and if
the Premier had been elected by open voting
old Banker James would have had only the
ghost of a chance for the leadership. But he,
became head of the Government as a matter of
course, because the little handful of men who
formed the first beginnings of the party had
appointed him. Lyne will probably, some day,
be Premier of New South Wales in like manner,
because about 20 men in a House of 125 elected
him to the billet in the days when his
was at the bottom of its fortunes. The Bulle-
tin advocates the election of the Premier and
his colleagues, and the old Conservative politi-
cians denounce the idea as revolutionary and,
unconstitoosh’nal, and things like that, and de-
mand the preservation of the good old system
which has been hallowed by age and other
things. But under the fine old constitoosh’nal
system the Premier really is elected as shown
above, and there is nothing at all revolutionary
in the election principle. The chief point of
difference between the two systems is that The
Bulletin and the other advocates of the Swiss
Executive system want the majority to elect
him, while the old constitoosh’nal party wants
him to continue to be elected by the minority.
The other point of difference is that the advo-
cates of the new system want to elect him
fresh, and the supporters of the old arrange-
ment want to have him stale. Sometimes six
years or so elapse between the time when a new
leader is elected by some small handful of sur-
vivors just come back from a disastrous election,
and the time when the same man becomes
Premier. By that time he is often very stale
indeed, especially if it happened that he was
only a makeshift person from the beginning.
The election of a stale Premier by a minority
vote instead of a fresh one by a majority is
about all the discrepancy that separates the two
methods of government.
Q. Lands Commission recommends the Govern-
ment that scourged Tattersall’s sweep out of
the territory, and turns up the whites of its
post-office eyes at all sorts of betting, to con-
tinue the system of ballot for land selections.
Now, the ballot was introduced in substitution
of the auction, or the sealed-tender system, in
order to give Short-purse a fair chance against
Long-purse. Result: Long-purse gets there
all the same, and dummying is rampant in the
land. The key to the whole position is the
rigid enforcement of a five-years’ compulsory
personal residence on the selection or grazing
farm, no transfer to be allowed, anil
the successful applicant to be debarred from
making any new application in any part of the
province, during that period. This would shut
out Long-purse, because he could not, or would
not care to, conform to the conditions. That
being once ensured, why not revert to sealed
tenders, and let the State get the highest rental
that it can for its land ? No one is likely to
offer more than the land, under cast-iron con-
ditions of personal residence (the crux of the
agreement) is worth to him.
Melbourne Argus has now finally abandoned
its time-worn yelp for a Freetrade revolution in
Victoria. Says Fat Man’s organ The
Canadian Liberals are pledged to remove the
40 per cent, wall, but the Laurier Govern-
ment does not propose to blow the wall up in
ruins, but to take it down course by course,
and no other policy is likely to be pursued by
any colony where the protective system has had
a long reign.” Poor old Argus ! The protec-
tive system gazes with equanimity at the pro-
spect of being “ taken down course by course,”
because any course that threatens danger to an
established industry will be carefully avoided.
If Australia should ever return unanimously to
the Freetrade principle it will be when foreign
goods can no longer find a market in competi-
tion with the local article.
One of the Pentridge (Melb.) officials refers to
the competition in “ converting ” prisoners that
is waged among the snuffling chaplains of rival
religious sects, as though it were quite a new
and surprising feature of gaol humbug. Charles
Dickens, in “ David Copperfield,” depicts the
same sort of competition between visiting
magistrates at the Model Prison. One gentle-
man trots out Uriah Heep (No. 27) as his par-
ticular pet lamb; another takes the polite
Littimer (No. 28) under his care :
It appeared to me that the gentleman in spectacles
backed his Twenty Eight against Mr. Creaklb’s Twenty
Seven, for each of them took his own man in hand.
“ What is your state of mind, Twenty Eight?” said the
questioner in spectacles. “ I thank you, sir,” returned
Mr. Littimer, “ I see my follies now, sir. I am a good
deal troubled when I think of the sins of my former com-
panions, sir; but I trust they may find forgiveness.”
And so forth. Each precious convert attached
himself to the patron who offered him the
strongest inducement to play the hypocrite,
whilst the less artful prisoners were passed
over as irredeemable sinners. At Pentridge,
seemingly, an old hand puts himself up to auc-
tion, and the shepherd who undertakes to
procure the largest amount of prison “conces-
sion,” or future assistance, snavels the interest-
ing penitent into his little flock. It is a war of
cants, from which no good result can issue,
hence the combination of chaplains to prevent
the Salvation Harmy emissaries from invading
their “field of labor.” The Harmy fellows
propose to plough the field on a practical
worldly system, and manure it with rough com-
mon-sense talk, and raise respectable crops as
time goes on. Given a fair chance, they
could do a great deal in the reformation way.
The average convict is quite capable of an am-
bition to become much the same sort of per-
son as the red-coated “ captin’ ” who calls him
brother, but no average mortal, in prison or out
of it, can cultivate an enthusiastic desire to
resemble a chaplain. The artificial, repellent
pietist in the long black coat identifies himself,
by profession, with all the virtues, wherefore
the felon, feeling himself hopelessly out of touch
with every form of “ goodness,” doesn’t enter-
tain one genuine thought of reformation as long
as Aminadab Sleek is the object-lesson. But
when the hallelujah.missionary, rolling a bit of
chewing-gum round his teeth, asks, “ Why
carn’t yer try to be like me, seein’ as how I
mighter been like you ? ” the tribulating male-
factor will, in a fair number of cases, promise
to do his best if they give him a blanky show.
There is nothing ridiculous in the idea. It
appeals to the man’s sense of eternal fitness in
things.
A London weekly (the Reeeree) points out
that the waiter-tipping system has long been
diverted from its original purpose. “As
tipping became general the proprietor of the
restaurant, as well as the waiter, saw his chance
of standing in. Presently waiters were found
to be willing to do their Work for nothing; and
eventually they paid for their tables, which is
now the custom at all restaurants and hotels
boasting a generous clientele. The result is
that, with all his tips, the waiter is no better
off than ever he was. If he received <£loo in
tips he would have to return .£9B of it as table-
money to his employer, who, in his turn, under
stress of competition, would have to reduce the
price of his wines and dishes.” In the case of
railway-porters, the tip system amounts to a
discretionary tax on the general public for the
benefit of shareholders. Porters accept nominal
wages, and really live upon tips, which are thus
practically appropriated by the railway co.
towards a reduction of working expenses and
consequent payment of dividends.
The other day the Orange Institute or Victoria
held a half-yearly meeting, whereat “ the chair-
man made an earnest appeal for all Protestants
to join the Orange Order.” This seems a proper
time—in fact, any time is proper—for mention-
ing that the Orange Order is quite disreputable
in the Eye of the Law, as well as hateful to all
reasoning Protestants of the English and Scotch
varieties. Founded by a gang of Ulstermen,
after a faction fight, the first Orange Lodge was
a society for depriving Roman Catholics of their
unlawful blunderbusses and other instruments
of destruction. In its palmiest, goriest days,
Orangeism was discountenanced by the Lord
Lieutenant of Ireland, and since it spread to
England in 1808 the precious dis-Order has
been once nominally dissolved, and once tempo-
rarily suppressed by Act of Parliament. At
the present time it would be wiped out alto-
gether if it were an infectious disease, which it
isn’t. It is merely a hereditary weakness, and
every Orangeman who dies without issue is an-
other nail in the Order’s coffin.
Melbourne Punch is moved by the persistent
emigration from Victoria to make a few remarks
in its old, familiar strain :
The old feeling of want of confidence is arising in our
midst. . . . Any country whose leaders are fonder of ex-
perimental legislation, rather than the practical and the
sound, cannot be expected to make substantial progress.
We are drifting, slowly but surely drifting, and can ex-
pect nothing else until many of our windbag legislators
are replaced by statesmen of eool and calm reflection and
moderate views.
Every time anything goes wrong in Victoria,
Punch feebly throws this same slab of mist at
the Turner Government. But it never by any
chance gets down to particulars, or explains
what are “ moderate views,” or what a states-
man of calm reflection would do that Turner
hasn’t done, or would leave undone that
he has done. It always approves strongly
of “the practical and the sound,” but never
explains what is the practical or the sound, and
it objects to “experimental legislation,” with-
out once mentioning what is experimental.
Punch’s political creed and that of Victorian
Conservatism in general boiled down : “ I am
strongly in favor of—ahem. I believe if—ahem
—-isn’t done this country will go to wreck.
The one great curse of this country is—er—er—
er. If er—er—er —h’m were carefully avoided,
and we went in unanimously for er—er —er
hem, the result would be an immediate restora-
tion of our former prosperity. We are lost for
want of er—ahem —h’m. Nothing, nothin<j can
ever restore our vanished glory and replace our
lost wealth and raise us again to our pinnacle
except—er—ahem. Therefore, let us rise up
gradually as one man and demand—er—h’m—
er—er—h’m.”
In ; 90, -Q. Labor-leader Glassey was an
avowed Socialist; in ’97, he is an avowed “Con-
stitutionalist”—whatever that may really mean.
Bimeby Fat Man ?
'Twas a Glorious Jubilee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The population of the United Kingdom is 40 millions
—mostly fools.”— Carlyle.
Old Casper, one fine evening, sat beside his cottage
door
And smoked his pipe, and sipped his beer, as in
the days of yore ;
And playing near him on the green,
Were Peterkin and Wilhelniine.
Old Casper watched the youngsters play, until his
eyelids closed;
Mayhap the old man’s eyes were weak, mayhap
the old man dozed ;
When suddenly he heard a shout,
Which made the old chap stare about.
Old Casper saw young Peterkin come tearing o'er
the grass
With something glitt’ring in his hand that looked
like gold—or brass —
“Oh ! Grandad, what is this I’ve found
A-lying there upon the ground ? ”
Old Casper took it from the lad, who stood ex-
pectant by,
Then clapped his specs, astride his nose, and this
was his reply—
“Thishere’s a medal —a brass ’un—see,
Twas struck for the Great Jubilee.”
“The Jubilee,” quoth Peterkin, “I’ve heard of
that before :
You told us once of crowds and glare, and shouts,
and dust, and roar.
Now, say what made the people shout,
And what was all the row about ? ”
“ I mind it well,” old Casper said, “the Queen,
for three-score years,
A-sitting on the throne had been —just think of it,
my dears ! ”
“Oh, yes, I see ! ” cried Wilhelmine ;
Was she a very noble Queen ? ”
“Dunnow my lass,” old Casper said, “ I really
bean’t aweer
Of any noble hact of hern ” —here Casper sipped
his beer.
“ But then, you see, my little dears,
Bhe’d been a-sitting sixty years.”
“ Oh, fiddlesticks ! ” said Wilhelmine—she was a
forward chit —
“Surely the Queen did something great. Now,
Grandpa, think a bit.”
“ She sat for sixty year,” said he,
“ And ’twas a famous Jubilee.”
“But, Grandad,” Peterkin demurred, “you told
us that before.
What good is sitting on a throne ? There must be
something more.”
“ I mind o’ nothin’ more,” said he,
“ But ’twas a glorious Jubilee.”
Tan.
J.P.M.
