“The Solicitor” waa put up at Sydney
Criterion on Saturday, and is practically the last
sustained effort of the Brough Co. ; after that
haggard and distracted Gent One makes his exit
the rest of the season is mainly one-night revivals.
“ The Solicitor ” is one of those farcical comedies
that are full of doors and have an erring wife, or a
husband in a dreadful state of mind, or a burglar
concealed in every room, and a distracted lawyer
lurking on the stairs, and an infuriated brother
lying low in the umbrella stand or ambushed in
the hat-rack, and the only place where nobody is
concealed is the tea-caddy. Most of the leading
male characters belong to the Condensed Grena-
diers—at least the very moderate size of some of
them certainly suggests that the Grenadiers are
condensed. Brough, as a sedate, middle-aged
lawyer in a large way of 6s. Bd., is induced by the
least-compressed Grenadier to steal and drive a
hansom for a lark. In the course of the evening
he drives his wife to a strange house, and when he
stands up on his perch, and thus nearly lifts a
half-strangled horse off its feet, he sees her over the
blind kissing a strange soldier. Then he drives two
burglars who are just escaping with the plunder.
He is afterwards retained by the cabman’s
daughter to defend her dissipated parent, who is
charged with having a silver soup-ladle found in
his cab, and of being an ally of house-breakers,
and for three acts a storm of suspicion rages around
the cab and its tired-out horse. Strange, broken-
nosed people hang around and offer in a husky
voice trimmed with gin not to blast the law-
yer’s reputation by saying anything about the
episode, and to return the jewellery for a moderate
sum down. The lawyer suspects the burglar with
the powerful gin-breath of being the person who
embraced his wife. The undermining influence of
the cab brings quite a lot of people around late at
night to see if their husbands are hugging any-
body, or if their wives are being hugged by any-
body, and one injured lady resolves to wear dark-
green in the witness-box while the other decides
to be divorced in black. A gorgeous Grenadier
captain is badly chewed by a dog while trying to
escape through the back garden, and finally things
grow so badly tangled in Act 11. that they never
get properly unravelled in Act 111. after all.
• )* * *
“ The Solicitor ” is an amusing outbreak
with no specially new ideas in it, but with the old
ideas cleverly worked up. There is much glitter-
ing uniform and nice girl and thrill in it. Brough
makes the leading character highly interesting,
and Harry Roberts, Carey, and Ferris support
him very cleverly, The other Roberts —Ralph of
that ilk—is a somewhat mechanical warrior who
vaguely suggests a robust wooden doll. Miss Hill,
as a smudged slavey with a cold—the typical Bary
Jade of a cheap hash-palace —is a marked success.
Four other ladies—Misses Temple, Watt-Tanner,
Hardy, and Titheradge—fill four rather ordinary
parts creditably, the last-named being the most
successful of the four. The present season ends
on June 19, and it is proposed to crowd
“Thoroughbred,” the “Woman of No Import-
ance,” the “Passport,” “Second Mrs. Tanqueray,”
“In Honor Bound,” “A Pair of Spectacles,” and
“Niobe” all into the brief remaining space of
time. Then the co. moves on to Adelaide, Maori-
land, and India. That clever young actor, Harry
Pliminer, joins it on its departure from Sydney,
and follows its fortunes till the final disbandment,
which takes place in the land of the rajah and the
depreciated rupee. The writer trusts that the
Asiatic venture will be a success, but he dreamed
last night that he got up early in the morning and
found Brough in a state of dilapidation, and with
his hair sticking up through his hat, delivering
the chops at the back door, and Harry Roberts
removing the ash-barrel, and that looks somehow
like a bad omen-
* * * *
On Monday, J une 21, the deserted Lyceum
opens for one night only. On that remembersome
occasion Mr. and Mrs. Brough are to be tendered
a benefit by the citizens of Sydney, the perform-
ance being under the patronage of His and Her
Ex. and suite, and of everybody else who is any-
body, or thinks he is, or can get anybody else to
say he is. “ Dandy Dick ” is to be the bill for the
farewell night, and somewhere in between that'
affecting story of the racehorse with the burnt
tail some influential citizen will presumably make
the usual speech. That influential citizen, who-
ever he may be, is hereby advised in advance to
cut the speech as short as possible. Another
benefit (Titheradge’s) is fixed for next Wednesday
afternoon at the Criterion. And as Tith., by no
means a millionaire, has both publicly and pri-
vately deserved as well of his fellow-man as any
actor who ever trod the Australian boards, The
Bulletin hopes to see a packed house.
* * * *
The Sydney Opera Society’s little attempt
at the “Grand Duchess ” at Sydney Royal went
through with a respectable amount of success.
There was very little in the house except the
orchestra and a clear conscience, but the co. got
away alive each night, and that at least was some-
thing. Speaking seriously—if amateur opera is
ever spoken of seriously—the Society deserved
rather better luck than it got. The scenery was
plain, but honest. The chorus was dressed re-
spectably, and though it was nervous and some-
what scared and a little out of step it wasn’t in an
agony of embarrassment about what to do with
its four extremities, and there were no grievous
hitches in the stage management. (These re-
marks apply to the second night; The Bulletin
wasn’t present on Saturday, but the daily papers
described that opening performance as resembling
the stampede of an army of lunatics with its hair
all hanging down its back.) Miss Ivy Gorrick
sang the part of the Grand Duchess moderately
well and played it very well indeed; Frank
Weekes, barring a hideously-improbable mous-
tache, could pass muster as the General; and
Albert Leston played Fritz in a cheerful, larky
fashion. Also, little Claude Swan, as the mite
with the drum, was the best and most tuneful
drummer-mite this paper ever met. A few weak,
inaudible people raged here and there in an uncer-
tain whisper on the outskirts of the show, but out
of regard for the Grand Duchess and the drummer-
mite and one or two other people, the names of
these harmless musical blots are suppressed.
* * # *
Harry Furniss and his mentor, the Much-
Travelled Smythe, are due at Sydney Royal to-
morrow (Friday), and on Saturday of next week
Maggie Moore succeeds him at the same estab-
lishment. The lady who was sung to by Butler
opens her season with a new comedy-drama,
“Johanna.” Butler, it will be remembered, pro-
mised to call round and be an influential dead-
head in Maggie’s show as soon as he is acquitted,
and if he keeps his word there will be a big audi-
ence for one night, anyhow. If he isn’t acquitted,
probably a “ Butler Curse ” will set in after the
manner of the Dean anathema, and then business
at the Royal will go down to nothing. If a
Butler curse does start it will fix on Miss Moore
to a certainty, because that broken-nosed person
sang a song to her about how he wanted mother.
Possibly if Miss Moore got herself exorcised by
the Cardinal, and fasted and mortified the flesh,
and gave a benefit to the Consumptive Hospital,
and had her hair cut, and slept on the bare floor
for a while, it might alleviate the danger, but
even with these precautions the present scribe
wouldn’t be the person who was sung to by
Butler for a good deal.
* # * *
There is very little left to say about the
“ Sign of the Cross,” almost all the reflections
which that good moral spectacle gave rise to
having already been fully expressed. Therefore,
it is only necessary to remark that the pageant is
in good health and condition and going strong,
and so far no change is even spoken of. The cata-
leptic Nero, though his empire looked very un-
certain on the first night, holds on to the purple
manfully, and this week he is specially supported
by a letter signed “Susan Hampden,” in which
Her Ex. approves of the play in general and of
Williamson’s generosity in giving a benefit to the
Consumptive Hospital fund in particular.
* * * *
“ The Bohemian Girl ” finishes a fort-
night’s run at Sydney Opera House this week,
and “Fatinitza” is announced for Saturday.
There is a good deal of nice harem in “Fatinitza ”
and very little else, but if the co. does justice to
this feature of the case it should be good for a
week or two, anyhow.
* * * *
* MacMahon’s cinematographe in Pitt-st.,
Sydney, has laid on a large number of new pic-
tures since the last time when The Bulletin was
sufficiently rested to allude to it in this column,
and most of them are well worth a visit. The
Crown Studio “ cin.” has moved to new quarters
in King-street, and by an improvement in the X-
ray apparatus undertakes to show the visitors’
bones in a more aggressively bony aspect than
heretofore. Also, the Tivoli is doing as well as
usual at the old address, which time-worn remark
has become a weariness and a Jubilee and a dire
monotony. A time will certainly come when this
paper will hire someone to burn the Tivoli merely
to lend variety to the above observation.
* * * *
“A Life of Pleasure,” at Melb. Royal,
strikes one as a better-grown specimen of this
variegated melodrama than was produced at the
Princess’s three years ago. The valor of the
British troops in Burmah is spread over a larger
battlefield, and gallant Colonel Cosgrove has
added two stone at least to his fighting weight
since he first took command of the victorious regi-
ment. Also, the opposite bank of the Thames is
further back from the footlights than it used to be
and the deceived colleen has more space wherein to
curse her cruel fate. Bland Holt’s shows need
all the elbow-room they can get. He describes
this particular effort as “ a drama of love, luck,
and loyalty,” the emblem of loyalty being a Nor-
denfeldt gun, which crashed through a portion of
Burmese precipice on Saturday, and went near to
falling upon Tommy Atkins. With the scenery
built up anew, and several other first-night defects
remedied, “A Life of Pleasure” should prove a
payable Jubilee bill, although next week is likely
to be a sad time for theatrical managements in
general.
* * * •
It is a mixed narrative, especially in the
last act, where the five scenes of yore are boiled
down to one Scene, and sundry characters who
ought to be at divers places are brought together
under the same roof in an unreasonable medley.
Walter Baker, at the outset, promises to be a wiid
and stormy Irish hero. He offers violence to a
process-server, whilst the surrounding tinantry
fire off volleys of encouragement; afterwards he
threatens to commit a first-class assault upon the
wrong man, and then, as the hours go by, Baker
loses his grip of public interest. Bland Holt,
in a beautiful curly wig and an eyeglass,
is cheerfully heroic this time; likewise, Cos-
grove appears brave and noble for a change.
But the chief villain and his lady victim divide
the cream of the acting opportunities. Two or
three times during the evening Miss Watson and
Albert Norman form the centre of a strong situa-
tion, or have the entire situation to themselves.
The results are always satisfactory. Norman
casts Miss Watson off with some artistic force,
and the mental upset of the lady, culminating in
a reckless resolve to drown her feelings in a
vortex of champagne, is fully expressed. They
have nothing to do with the war in Burmah,
however; consequently, when the din of that
irrelevant campaign has died away, and Miss
Watson comes forward once more to^ea 1 papers
for her faithless A.N., she seems father like a
extinct volcano. Mrs. Bland Ho , . ,
comic warbler of high moral princip >
item in the performance. Charley Brown roars
gladsomely in a Hebrew capacity, and Mis
Vivienne plays Lady somebody with excellent
effect, apart from her weak voice. Corlesse, Ha y
Norman, and J. Montgomery alll the
other work worth mentioning, and do it very well.

“A Night Out” doesn’t grow in popu-
larity at Melb. Princess’s. For one thing, the
weather is not suited to a thin and frivolous
French farce. People, as a rule, are cold, and
critical, and severely moral in mid winter, which
is the time for problem plays, corned beef, and
any other sort of solid entertainment. Several
austere parents have been heard protesting" that
they took their innocent daughter to A JNight
Out,” and were quite shocked on her account,
but as they forbore from writing to the
papers the “disgust” of these occasional
Podsnaps has not proved a decent adver-
tisement for the show. ‘ A Night Pft
finishes on Friday. It will be supplanted by
“My Friend from India,” an up-to-date trine
that comes straight from London and Hew York.
Geo. Lauri and Miss Juliet Wray join the Poulton-
Stanley company for this occasion.
** * *
Anyone who has not already heard about
the Jubilee matinee, in aid of the Fever Hospital,
will please accept this notice as an exhortation to
rush along to the Princess’s immediately. The
matinee starts at 1.30 this (Thursday) afternoon,
and the programme is appalling in its magnitude.
** * *
A comic opera season, under the manage-
ment of Howard Vernon, is to open at the
Alexandra on Saturday night (12th). The ven-
ture has a promising appearance. Offenbach s
famous opera bouffe, “La Grande Duchesse, is
practically unknown, and therefore as good as a
novelty, to half the population of Melbourne,
whilst" Howard Vernon and Miss Fanny Liddiard
are attractive names. The two old favorites will
be supported by Miss Nellie Young, and three other
principals (warranted new) likewise a cborusof fifty
voices and a full band will assist in this musical
undertaking. For “ loyal ” reasons, or because
the house has been identified with calamity of late
years, the Alexandra is called the Queen’s Theatre
just now, but it will remain the same old Haleck
in spite of the alias. Why try to get rid of a once-
respected title? Simonsen’s first Italian Opera
Company sang for 12 weeks or more to first-class
business at the Alexandra.
** * *
Miss Elsie Adair and her travelling variety
show are staying at the Bijou until Saturday;
next week she will visit Ballarat and Geelong,
and probably return to Melbourne some time
later. It is a small show for a full-sized theatre,
in a city that has Rickards always on the job, but
it presents several neat little features, and brings
a certif. of merit from the King of Siam. The
tawny monarch in question was so deeply im-
pressed by Miss Elsie and Co. that he lent them
an elephant to ride upon, and invited them to
lunch at his royal palace.
** * *
At the Opera House, on Saturday night,
Rickards scored again with the five Kentucky
picanninies, who were duly landed on these
shores, in one parcel, as the 29th instalment of
new artists. The pics, run larger than was
expected. They have not, however, lost the
bloom of youth or the spontaneous impishness that
marks the difference between a colored coon and a
c ilorable imitation.

The much-talked-of Elder (S.A.) Scholarship of Music
dwindles to nothing, as per this extract from a letter
written by the holder in London, Wallage Kennedy
“ Really, unless a fellow has money, or can make money
the scholarship is not worth having. Out of £52 10s. you
have to find board and lodging, your own piano, of
course, all your own music, and travelling expenses, and
have to keep yourself in the vacation of three months;
besides keep up the appearance due to your position.”
Alma Stanley is a divorcee, or rather a divorceree,
having been the attacking party.
S.S. : “Wonderful is the similarity in personal appear-
ance of the American burlesque actress, judging by
samples seen in Melbourne to date. One American
variety artiste’s legs are exactly the same legs as those
flourished by every other American artiste in the eye of
the critic, and the passion for cardinal silk stockings
seems to be national. The contour of the face, the
manner of dressing the hair, the gold-stopped teeth, the
pie-shaped smile, the somewhat plump figure, the side-
long wriggle—these things are identical in every song-
and-dance dona from the land of wooden hams. Judging
by process blocks in Yewnited States journals, and maga-
zines, too, the Dorothy Usner type of face—mind you,
Dorothy was a beauty—is the only one known to Ameri-
can ’alls."
Grattan Riggs’ beauteous pink visage and his volumin-
ous, three-ply smile have been seen hovering about Mel-
bourne theatres again. Since Anson’s departure Grattan
holds undisputed possession of the beauty cake. The
writer once heard the two barracking. “ Never mind,
Riggs,” said Anson, consolingly, “ beauty is only skin
deep.” “Is it, then ?” answered Riggs. “Begobl If I
was you I’d get shkinned.”
Walter Baker, the solid man of Bland Holt’s co., was
smitten suddenly with illness one night last week, and
had to sit amongst the cobwebs and nurse his pain, whilst
Walner Gregory, who has the happiness to be Miss Wat-
son’s hubby, tackled the part, with prompters all over
the shop, and the book up his sleeve. Gregory battled
so pluckily with adversity, and made so good a job of the

Samuel Neriga, of the family of travelling showmen,
has died at Normanton of dengue fever. Out of a popu-
lation of 550, Normanton, at latest, had over 250 cases of
dengue, with 16 deaths.
The Melb. Druids, it seems, were not at all rash in
running their Easter show, at the Exhibition Building,
for a whole week. A grand bazaar was an important item
of the proceedings. All the bazaar profits go to the
Druids. The expenses are deducted from the “ art-
union ” profits, which, according to law, go to the aid of
public charities.

The three Haytors add a very funny little show to their
Girard-ish business at Melbourne Opera House. They give
an imitation of one or two of the best-known cinemato-
graphe subjects, and their jerky, unnatural movements
in the wobbling flashlight are ridiculously reminiscent of
the original pictures.
Dear Bulletin, — Be a Pov. Point par.—it is surprising
to find that 99 per cent, of Melbourne playgoers accepted
the recent Princess’s production of “ Merchant of Venice’’
as the first within 15 years or so. Janet Achurch and
hubby Charrington appeared as Portia and Shylockatthe
Royal, with Bucklaw, Herbert Flemming, and (I think)
Maud Williamson in the cast. The Thespian amateurs,
headed by P. Duggan, wrestled with “ The Merchant” at
the same house not so many years ago, and George Miln
played Shylock, in the trial scene, for his farewell benefit
at the Bijou. The Achurch performance should have
been remembered, anyway, by the fact that Bucklaw
played the Prince of Morocco, a character omitted from
the Potter-Bellew revival. —Melbourne.
Princess Chimay—the awful Yankee heiress who eloped
with a gipsy person a while ago, but soon came back to
her old haunts—was lately engaged to appear as Venus,
and similar nude characters, at a Parisian ’all. The Gay
City prepared to revel in the spectacle of a princess mid
nodings on. Private boxes were sold at 500 francs, and
ordinary seats found eager purchasers at 20 francs. Un-
fortunately, the Prefect of Police put an indirect veto on
the revelation of Princess Clara’s outlines by informing
the management that he would close the hall if a riot
eventuated. The riot, it seemed, was a foregone con-
clusion. A Parisian club, of which Chimay is a member,
had arranged to hurl 100 live rabbits at Venus. “As to
what will be thrown from other quarters, I don’t know,”
said the Prefect. There were such alarming possibilities
in those missiles the policeman didn’t know of that poor
Venus decided not to appear after all. She went home
and had a bogus attack of influenza. Probably the public
didn’t miss a very beautiful show. The lady has been a
mother two or three times, and the Prince didn’t feel his
loss deeply ; ditto the gipsy. Indeed, the gipsy is s.iid to
have whacked her for not realising his expectations.
Dear Bulletin, — It’s not French prudery that relegates
certain histrionic happenings to “ behind’’—it’s the ex-
tension to the general drama of the cast-iron traditions of
French classical tragedy, which bars all violent or even
undignified action in view of the audience. As for
strangling, I once saw “ Othello ” done by a (good) French
company, and not only was Desdemona assassinated out
of sight and Othello prevented at the end from smiting
himself “ thus ! ” —but Cassio wasn’t allowed to get
squiffy, and even the fateful handkerchief was discarded
as being too undignified an item for tragedy. I forget
what took its place, but the whole thing—from the be-
nighted British point of view, of course—was about as
tragic as boiled-mutton-and-turnips !— Alex. M.
Fregoli, the protean star of London Alhambra, will
figure in Williamson and Musgrove’s Christmas produc-
tion, if money can do it.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Attorney-General Want has decided (what The
Bulletin pointed out in its issue of May 1) that there is
nothing in N.S.W. Income-Tax Act to justify the collec-
tion of “arrears” as attempted by the Tax Commis-
sioners. This decision raises three very pertinent
Questions: (1) Who were the two alleged “ eminent
counsel” who said the “arrears” system was legal? (2)
On what clauses in the Act did they base their opinion,
seeing that no one else can find anything in that docu-
ment which gives any semblance of justification? Also
(3) why did the Commissioners waste the publio money in
getting opinions from outside counsel when Attorney-
General Want’s opinion is at the service of the Govern-
ment gratis ? Another point on which Want’s opinion
should be taken, before that eminent lawyer goes
away fishing again, is the trustee swindle. Ac-
cording to the Act every taxpayer is exempt up to £2OO.
But last year, if a person received income as trustee
for another, even if it was only £25 a year, the Commis-
sioners demanded income-tax upon it, whether the recipi-
ent had any other income or not. They took upon them-
selves to abolish the exemption in such cases. Where a
married woman’s sole private income was £25 a year de-
rived from trust funds she was charged 12s. 6d. income-tax.
And yet the law asserts that no one with an income of
less than £2OO a year is taxable. One victim who was
forced to pay this swindle-tax tells The Bulletin that,
after spending time and trouble in various ways, he got
his money refunded from the Treasury a few days ago,
and had to put a 2d. stamp on the receipt. On what
possible clause do the official blunderers justify this
high-handed piracy ?
For the first 11 months of the financial year (to
the end of May) the N.S.W. revenue was £8,433,228 and
expenditure £8,634,357 ; deficit, £201,129. This is
£373,725 better than the situation a month previously,
the deficit at the end of April being £574,854. A great
effort is apparently being made to economise or to shove
things forward into next year or both, the May expendi-
ture being only £519,079 against an average for the pre-
vious 10 months of £811,527. This tendency to shunt
things forward in the last two months of the year is the
worst feature about the Cash Basis system—which is
otherwise wholly commendable. According to Premier
Reid’s estimate, the shortage for the year is to be
£229,000, so he is now a little better than that amount.
By the way, why don’t other Treasurers follow Reid’s
example by publishing monthly returns of expenditure?
Most of them state the revenue monthly, but the expen-
diture is generally a blank.
* * * *
The N.S.W. loan expenditure for the past eleven
months was £1,374,881. It wouldn’t require so very
much of a dead lift in the way of finding new sources of
revenue to meet this amount of publio works expenditure
out of revenue, and put an end to the lamentable piling
up of liabilities. It only wants a strong, brainy man with
a strong majority to make the effort.
« * * *
Victoria has £2,085,776 of 4 per cent, stock fall-
ing due this year, and the desirability of converting it
into 3 per cents, and saving £20,857 annually in interest
is sufficiently obvious. The Government holds £919,000
on account of various trust accounts, and the conversion
of that amount is easy. As regards the rest, which is
also held in Victoria, it is suggested that probably Turner
may arrange, while jubilating in England, to float the
necessary conversion loan with J. Bull, without giving the
Melbourne market a show. If he does, it will be about
time that something serious happened to Turner. What
these provinces badly want is to shift their British in-
debtedness to Australia, not to transfer to Britain the
miserably small proportion of their debt which is held
locally. Moreover, the damphool experiment tried the
other day by Holder, of S.A., only ended by showing that
the London market gave worse returns than the Aus-
tralian one, even apart from the great difference in ex-
penses, which made the Australian figures better and the
British ones worse than they looked.
* * * *
The private enterprise syndicate which wants to
finance Mildura howls persistently outside the Vic.
Government offices, and though Acting-Premier Isaacs
came out last week and told it to go away it still remains
and howls. It proposes that the Government should
build a railway to Mildura, the syndicate giving a
guarantee of £3OOO a year for five years in case of loss.
On the other side, the syndicate is to supply Mildura
with water for irrigation purposes, the money being
raised by the issue of £200,000 worth of debentures, prin-
cipal and interest being guaranteed by the Government.
Summed up, the syndicate proposes that it should
guarantee the Government to the extent of £15,000, and
the Government should guarantee it to the extent of
£27o,ooo—in other words, it wants to start with a capital
of £255,000 supplied by the State and keep the profits.
Think of making an offer like that to a Jewish Acting-
Premier 1

cipated that the year’s expenditure would be £33,000

months are £93,000 more than for the similar period last
year he looks like being badly out on both points.J

The latest re-arrangement of the N.Z. Loan and
Mercantile Agency Co. is a severe knock to everybody
concerned. The iast time the Loan and Mercantile re-
constructed was in ’94. On that occasion it wrote down
its paid-up capital by £240,000, and made calls to the ex-
tent of nearly £Boo,ooo—so far as it was possible to sort
things out. It also fixed up a fearful and wonderful ar-
rangement of preference shares, “ A ” debentures, * B
debentures, and so on, until the final position of things
was something like the following : The first claim on the
assets was £2,013,870 of prior lien debenture stock at 4
per cent., secured by a first mortgage on everything, and
redeemable at a premium of 2J per cent. Next came the
■•A” debenture stook, £1,251,217 at 5 per cent., seoured
by a second mortgage over everything, and redeemable at
" P er cent, premium after the prior-lien crowd was paid
off. Then the “B” debenture stock, £1,043,007 at 5 per
oent., was secured by a third mortgage over all the com-
pany s assets and its soul, and redeemable at 5 per cent,
premium after the prior-lien incubus and the “A” de-
benture encumbrance were liquidated. Then the prefer-
ence shareholders (£456,789) had a fourth claim, and the
fifth and sixth claims on the co. were represented by the
ordinary shareholders with £725,125 of paid-up capital.
This was a horribly tangled but truly British
arrangement (the British financier, in all the reconstruc-
ii reve^e< l ifi prior lien debentures, “A,” “ B,”
"X” and deferred “ZJ” debentures with premiums
on them, also preference and second preference shares,
and ordinary shares, likewise plain shares and shares
with trimmings, frills and insertion in them and cut bias
ol Tk >assemen^er ’ tint it distinctly implied four things :
(1) The Loan and Mercantile was working on 4i millions
?o\ y? rro ' ve< l money and less than 1 \ millions of its own ;
(2) the borrowed money cost nearly 4| per cent, all
round, which was a practically hopeless rate ; (3) every
asset of the co. was hampered by three separate mort-
gages ; and (4) the co. was pledged to pay, in addition to
interest, £165,000 as bonus to the debenture-holders. The
Bulletin, in its issue of 27th January, 1894, said that the
co. was “ bound to come to grief unless it got rid of
some of its burdens,” and this paper’s office Jeremiah was
a true prophet.
Ab the end of 1895 the N. Z. Loan and Mercan-
tile had written off, so far as can be ascertained, £647,000
in all, reckoning capital and reserves. It has altogether
failed to pay interest on the“ B ” debentures, and after
satisfying only the prior lien crowd and the “ A” deben-
ture-holders it showed a loss of £28,000 for 1895 and
£6OOO for 1896. At the end of 1895 the auditors reported
that it was necessary to write off another huge sum to
cover losses, but the directors contradicted this flatly,
and after a severe moral scuffle the auditors retired—pre-
sumably to make wax' for people who wouldn’t say such
unpleasant things. In its issue of 4th January, 1896, The
Bulletin solemnly staked its little pile on the auditors,
and, so far as it knows, it was the only paper which did so.
It is now cabled that the capital will have to be
written down from £1,181,914 to £138,375, and it is also
necessary to write off about £1,500,000 of debentures, so
that £2,500,000 more disappears in one fell swoop. Also,
the interest is to be reduced and readjusted so that the
burden will be £104,000 a year instead of £145,000, unless
the profits make it possible to pay more. Then, by way
of further tangle, £lOO,OOO of preference shares, worth at
present nothing, are to be issued to certain trustees, and
the trustees are seemingly to issue “certificates” to the
nominal value of £1,000,000 to the shattered debenture-
holders and shareholders. In the present state of affairs,
the certificates will have no real value, and are only cum-
bersome foolishness.
The co. in two years made a loss of £34,000
without paying interest on the “B” debentures, and,
provided things get no worse, the saving now effected
should just about enable it to meet its debenture interest.
As for leaving anything to pay dividends, or start a new
reserve, or reduce the huge load of liabilities, or replace
the lost capital, that, on present appearances, seems hope-
less. _ The board announces, as it did before, that the posi-
tion is now absolutely sound. Considering the situation
of the co., with only £138,000 capital, and over £3,000,000 —
even after all the writing down—of borrowed money, on
which a high rate of interest is payable, the position
looks, barring another boom, of which there is no present
sign, more like being absolutely and utterly hopeless.
Further, seeing that it is less than two years since the
last assurance (given after another big writing-down) that
everything was provided for, the writing-off as lost of
2J4 millions—about two-fifths of the entire assets —is a
gruesome commentary on the intelligence or the veracity
of somebody. What the Loan and Mercantile wants now
is to call up £1,000,000 of new capital in cash, not certi-
ficates or brown paper; to pay off that amount of its
costly liabilities; and then to consolidate its “X ” and
“ Y ” and “ K ” debentures, and its first and second and
seventeenth prior lien pawn-ticket, and its first and tenth
and various mortgage certificate permanent debenture
stook into one simple and sane liability at 3 per. cent,
all round.
* * * *
Last week the shares of the A.J.S. Bank
(£7 10s. paid up) were being offered in Sydney at 9s. with
no buyers. All the calls made at the 1893 reconstruction
have been paid, so there is no immediate call liability to
account for this. Also, it is noteworthy that the price is
much lower than it was after the last disastrous balance-
sheet came out showing a loss of £48,200; then the
second re-arrangement and the big reduction of de-
posit-interest (now completed) were still matters of grave
uncertainty, and it was highly probable that if they
didn’t come off the alternative would shortly be liqui-
dation, yet shares were actually sold as high as 17s. 6d.
Apparently the market anticipates either a heavy writing
off, or the calling up, in whole or in part, of the remain-
ing liability of £2 10s. per share, or it believes that,
despite the reduction in interest, the June balance-sheet
will show another heavy loss—in other words, as this
paper suggested when the latest scheme was under con-
sideration—that the saving effected isn’t large enough. Or,
more probably, the market reckons that two or all three
of these things may happen simultaneously.
• * * *
The increase of fire-insurance rates in Mel-
bourne has raised the usual imbecile shriek for the estab-
lishment of a new co. which will charge “ moderate pre-
miums.” A new co., unless it had a run of extraordinary
luck, would have to charge higher premiums than those
now existing; most of them have large accumulations,
and the interest from these keeps them afloat, whereas
the new co. would start without any accumulations at all.
The companies can’t charge more moderate premiums
than they do because the intense competition keeps the
expense rate ruinously high ; and if another co. has to live
out of the business which isn’t sufficient for those already
existing, and there is another office and set of officials and
agents to be maintained, it only means that the expenses
will be Btill more, and rates must ultimately go up still
higher. It is less, not more, competition that is wanted,
and only some legal process for preventing the inrush of
more foreign companies, and weeding out those already
in, can bring that about.

boom shows, among his liabilities, £171,399 to the Do-

tinct idea how things are looking for the end of June.

Reports are going round about the sale of a good-
sized Melbourne enterprise and a very large Sydney one
to English companies. This steady tendency of every
decent Australian enterprise to drift into the hands of J.
Bull is one of the reasons why The Bulletin advocates a
very large and solid absentee-tax.
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
inP' Co (Sydney), reckons that it has a profit for the half-
vear impended of £2228, after allowing £lOOO for depre-
ciation, and a dividend at the rate of 10 per cent, per
annum absorbs £1642. The remainder, with previous
balances, makes £2269 carried forward. The Intercolonial
only owes £12,175 to depositors and all other creditors,
and its success is mainly owing to the fact that it trades
almost entirely with its own funds. Most of the collapsed
land and building companies went down owing to the
burden of a top-heavy mass of deposits.

£lO,OOO reserve-fund, and £29,211 at credit of profit and
loss.
• * * *
The proposal of the United States Government
to gradually call in the “ greenbacks” has created a howl
of joy among the opponents of State banking and State
note issues. The U.S., these people point out, has tried
the experiment of issuing State paper for many years
past, and now decides to give it up as a failure. This
view of the case is either misrepresentation or ignorance,
or both. The worst trouble about the U.S. paper cur-
rency is its woefully mixed character: it includes United
States notes, Treasury notes, gold certificates, silver cer-
tificates, currency certificates (all these are State paper-
money), and also the notes of the national banks, which
are strictly private institutions and number 3676. The
proposal of the Government is merely to gradually with-
draw the United States notes, and thereby make one less
variety of paper-money in existence, and even when they
are withdrawn there will be over £100,000,000 worth of
State paper in circulation. Probably, in course of time,
an attempt will be made to carry the reform further, and
have only one variety of State paper afloat. But the U.S.
Government hasn’t even begun to talk about abolishing
the State note issue.

A huge slice of Tasmanian territory now hangs
to the watch-chain of Richard Chaffey Baker. At the
last moment the £lO,OOO Great Western Gridiron deposit
was cabled from Adelaide to Hobart by that exalted per-
sonage. Baronet Clarke was expected to put up the
amount, but he died a day or two before time was up, and
the bargain was declared off by his relatives. The wily
arch-promoter Palmer then seems to have engineered in
Adelaide, with the above result.

It takes six transit commissioners—at a guinea
per sitting ; chairman, two guineas ; and sometimes they
hold an ordinary meeting for half-an-hour, and a special
meeting just afterwards for 20 minutes or so, and charge
for two sittings—to administer the street traffic of Brisbane
(Q.) metropolitan district. Which is only a pigmy area,
all told. While it takes only one commissioner to ad-
minister all the Q. railways.

Sredwin Smith (S.A.) thinks Govt, bonds are
much better investments than house property, because
there is no trouble about the collection of rents, no fear
of frauds by agents, no risk of depreciaiion in value, and
no anxiety as to the continuity of the tenancy. He,
therefore, does not mind accepting less interest. As he
belongs to the “ Great Conservative Party,” which affirms
that the Kingston Govt, has ruined S.A., his testimony is
valuable.
» * • •
A lady turned up at the B.H. Junction meeting
in Melbourne, and made a diversion ; in an unguarded
moment she confessed her ignorance of mining, and then
the satirical man of the crowd got to work, and the direc-
tors were saved by bluff. But the lady started off with
a question that the directors couldn't face : What was the
cause of the creep ? was the most pertinent question that
could be put at the meeting. The chairman said the
creep was “ a normal subsidence.” The lady’s ignorance
of mining didn’t allow her to follow it up, and nobody
else apparently had the pluck to go to her rescue. She
could only suggest that the manager had too much to do.
Wherein she was mostly right, for he had too much ore-
extraction to do to allow of the timbering and staying
being properly attended to. And for the manager having
too much ore-extraction to do the directors were probably
to blame. To which extent they caused the creep.
Of course, it had to be owned up at the Junction
meeting that the creep was more extensive than the
manager had at first thought.
Block 10’s progress has been better than the div.
figures indicate. Last half-year it paid £20,000, which
was the same as was distributed in the corresponding
half of ’95-6, and twice as much as in the next preceding
half. But the actual profit for the three terms was:
Half-year to March, ’96, £8355; to Sept., ’96, £5740; to
March, ’97, £23,340. The earlier divs. had been paid out
of the reserve piled up while the kaolin “ chimney” was
being coined.
B.H. South pays a June div., and goes on add-
ing to its buddies for the further treatment of its slimes
from the concentrators. The prospecting work under-
ground still shows a very mixed body. The British also
adds some buddies to its concentrating plant.
The increase in the number of buddies, which
has lately been reported as to almost every B.H. sulphide
mine, is of great importance, inasmuch as it allows of a
finer crushing of the ore, and the consequent elimination
of more zinc from the concentrates. Where there is this
fine crushing, there are almost of necessity heavy losses
of silver which flows away in the slime ; and it is to catch
this metal that the buddies are needed.
The first div. in the B.H. British was paid on
the last day in May. The total was £15,000, of which
£6160 Us. was payable in respect of shares on the Aus-
tralian register. Of the 1890 Australian shareholders, 700
are South Australians and 550 Victorians.

Unofficially stated that last month’s crushing of
411 tons from the Beacon mine, N.S.W., formerly called
Taylor’s Reward, returned 1131 oz. gold, but the exaggera-
tion of previous returns makes the acceptance of this
doubtful. Why can’t the 00., English though it be, make
its own announcement, and save all this uncertainty ?
• * * •
Barrass and Conley’s mine, now called the Great
Peak Freehold, is still the wonder of Cobar, N.S.W. The
syndicate having failed to float it on a basis of £200,000,
the owners applied a considerable part of the gold they
had taken out to its active and workmanlike development.
They erected a new 10-head stamp-mill, with engine-power
for 30, built a good dam, started a main shaft, and did
other good work. Not many weeks ago, in driving from
the main shaft to cut the “ brown lode” at 75ft., a forma-
tion composed largely of kaolin, and carrying rich chlo-
rides of silver, was cut. It was soon found good enough
to bag, the brown lode being left alone at this point for
awhile. Over 200 tons of this silver ore have already been
sold, netting over £lO a ton, and 200 tons more are now
being broken. Some 60 yards south they are working a
big auriferous formation, which mills 7 to 10 dwts., and
leaves about the same in the tailings.
Hill End news : “ The plant and 1< aaes of the
Hawkins Hill Co. have been sold in lots. The winding
plant and leases are being purchased by the tributors
working the ground; the rest of the machinery goes
away.” This camp seems even to have broken the heart
of the newspaper man who used to foreshadow for it a
boom every two or three weeks, and allude to the gold
cakes of some thirty years ago.
* « * *
Nundle, N.S.W. : “ Gozley and party have
crushed 250 tons for a yield of about 600 oz. of gold. The ore
was obtained from a reef which varies from one inch to
three feet in thickness.” There is also some variation in
“ about 600 ounces.” Is there no weighbridge in the
neighborhood ?
* * * *
Some interesting figures re the Westralian gold
output are contained in the May number of Clarke and
Co.’s (Adelaide) “ Investor’s Guide.” The gold export for
May (official figures) was 59,1110 z., which is nearly
10,000 oz. higher than that of the previous month, and
brings the total output for the first five months of the
present year to 211,9770 z. This exceeds the entire export
during ’94, and is only 20,0000 z. less than the total pro-
duction during ’95, while the total for ’96 was but
281,2630 z. Thus the operations of the first five months of
the present year produced more than those of the first
nine months of last year. Also, the Westralian output for
last month exceeds the Victorian output for any month
this year save March.

Wired that Bayley’s Reward has got rich gold

708 oz. gold. Another pocket, no doubt.
* * * •
Q. Menzies shares have more rigging than a
clipper ship. If the crushing is good down they go. If
it is bad up they go. Pity some other W.A. mines were
not sold on similar results. They would be worth
millions.
* * * *
Mr. Modest Maryanski, the Polish mining ex-
pert, has returned to Westralia, after a visit to London,
Berlin, and Cripple Creek, U.S. Mr. M. got somewhat
excited on finding telluride ore in the Boulder Main Beef
mine, and cleared off in great haste, allowing the news-
papers to say he was going, not only to restore confidence,
but to bring back heaps of foreign capital. He has
brought back a diamond-drill p'ant “to test the con-
tinuity of the lodes on the field.” He asserts that “ Lon-
don capitalists have had their confidence restored in
Kalgoorlie.” This is news indeed, after the Premier and
other people have just left the province on the restore-
path. If restored, well, where is it? Confidence in the
Great Boulder and the other producers cf Kalgoorlie was
never lost; but the prices of the hundreds of mines sur-
rounding them, and on other fields, continue to fall,
“ cats ” continue to die, there are no new floats, good,
bad, or indifferent, and, look where one will, there is no
sign of restored confidence, cash or boom, unless it be in
the reduction in the labor conditions, by which many
hundreds of men are being thrown out of work in the
interests of the jobbing gang.

• • * •
The prices ruling for shell in Torres Straits are
as good as ever, and production shows no falling off. Yet
business is depressed, and Northerners want to know
why 1 Perhaps the solution lies in the fact that previous
to the Japanese invasion wages went locally into circula-
tion. Last year, however, £B5OO went through the post
to Japan, to say nothing of the amount carried home in
sovereigns by retiring Japs. That’s how the curse works.

* * * *
Holland’s claim at Black Flat, near Melb., has
suspended operations. Only three shafts have been put
down on the alleged goldfield—two by Holland and sons,
and one, across the road, by a syndicate of Richmond
tradesmen. The latter has been finally shut down and
wound-up. They struck a fine reef and brought up 20 or
30 tons—of road metal. The only claim that paid any
sort of return was the one adjoining Holland’s—called the
“ Mountain View,” which kept on crushing all the time.
’Twas a fine reef and went eight pints to the gallon every
time, at 6d. a pint., tailings being re-sold. The “ stuff ”
consisted mostly of malt and hops, with the usual
etceteras, and returned a handsome div. to proprietor
Moylan, who ultimately sold out and built himself a hand-
some house on the opposite corner, overlooking the scene
of his operations.

“ Arrangements made for the sale to an Eng-

Tas., the leaseholder getting £lOOO and a quarter interest

declined the big mine at what has since proved a moderate
price.

7s. 6d. per week; two will turn the handles, while the
others supply water from jam-tins.”

SOCIETY
A recent vice-regal accouchement seems
likely’- to provoke quite a tempest in the medical
teapot of an Australian capital, and the generally-
accepted “economy” of the Excellency in ques-
tion has been debited with another black mark.
Thus : A son and heir was born, the lady being
safely delivered by the accoucheur who has been
medicine man to quite a succession of vice-regal
families. A few weeks later Her Ex. was
stricken, supposedly, with dengue fever—the aris-
tocratic malady < ■ the hour. Another eminent
medico was called in, and the old vice-regal
physician was ignored. Then certain things
became Current talk.
Dr. X. had kept his professional mouth
closed up to this. Then it transpired that the
Excellency had not, as is supposed to be the vogue
of Excellencies, voluntarily forwarded a cheque as
his estimate of the deserved honorarium. But, a
few weeks having elapsed, His Ex. wrote to
Dr. X. requesting him to forward his account.
Dr. X. sent in a bill for (it is said) 25 guineas.
The Governor promptly sent a cheque for that
amount, together with a letter, in which
he is stated to have expressed the opinion
Jiat the charges made bore no fair rela-
DISTRUST THE EXPERT!
“No good asking that cockie for rations ; he was on the road himself
before he settled here!”
tion to the value -of the professional services
rendered, and hinted, if he did not say, that
advantage was being taken of the writer’s posi-
tion as Governor of the province. Dr. X. promptly
z'eturned the cheque with “ some remarks,” and up
to now, so far as is known, the matter stands at
that.
When Dr. Y. was called in to treat Her X.
for alleged dengue fever, Dr. X. seems to have given
his friends explanations as to why he had not
continued to act as the lady’s attendant, and it is
said he intends laying the whole circumstances
before his medical brethren as being in the nature
of an affront to the profession..
* * * *
“Big” Clarke the First did his best to
found a dynasty and solidify the family interest
by entailing the Rupertswood estate, but the Vic-
torian law only allows an entail to be fixed for
two generations, so Baronet Rupert may dissipate
the family pile at his pleasure. This arrangement
is bad for the hereditary title. A hereditary title
is a little less incongruous when the founder of the
family can make reasonably sure, by the entail
system, that a hereditary crust will go along with,
it. Otherwise the pile seldom lasts through half-
a-dozen generations. The time will probably come
when Australia’s only baronet will hold out a
gaunt, long-necked ration-bag to the cook for
tucker, and when the dog that trudges behind
him on the dusty Western track will look no more
baronial than the dog that trudges behind anybody
else. If anyone could draw a true picture of Aus-
tralia’s only baronet’s dog as he will be in 1960 it
might help a little in the good work of blasting
hereditary titles.

importing 33 cases of fireworks to brighten Record
Reign night at Brisbane. Then follows the list,
graciously communicated to the press :
Six batteries various ; eighteen silver tourbillions ; six
mines of jewelled-headed cobras; six batteries, “Flight
of Silver Comets ” ; twelve Jubilee commemoration bat -
teries ; six cracker mines ; three mines forming clouds of
fireflies; two Bin. (diameter) shells, “Poppies in the
Cornfield”; one Bin, shell, “The Lightning Flash and
Thunderbolt Shells”; one Bin. shell, “Great Golden
Cloud, Studded with Emeralds” ; one Bin. shell, “Triple
Japa
And so on. But what’s the betting that the
liberal-minded Gov. passes the bill to the tax-
payer, and doesn’t pay for one jewelled-headed
cobra of the lot ?
♦ * * *
A Victorian dental firm intends celebrating
the Record Reign by presenting 60 sets of teeth to
the “deserving toothless.” The “deserving
aged ” and the “ deserving poor ” have been much
heard of, but the “ deserving toothless ” sounds
novel. There seems a fine opening here for a local
chiropodist to take out 60 corns gratis from the
feet of the deserving corny ; also, if some philan-
thropist would give 100 hot baths to the deserving
dirty, or cut “on the never” the hair of the
deserving hairy, it would bring joy to many a
Numble ’Ome.

A dog-story up to
date : A faithful animal
walks with his master
into the city from South
Melbourne daily, then
catches a green tram at
the Equitable corner, and
rides home in glory in the
gripman’s compartment.
The dog never makes a
mistake in the car, and
when the conductor calls
“Fare, please,” it gives
three wags of its tail as a
canine equivalent for three-
pence.

. The hard-driven escort has a bad time at
Sydney Guvmentouse under the Hampden regime.
The last vice-royalties only used it on large State
occasions, but the present crowd can’t go out to
buy a packet of pms, or to get its hair cut, without
all the pomp and gauds streaming behind. And
on wet days it is a solemn thing to see the escort
trying to form himself into a hollow square outside
a shop while the rain flows off him in sheets, all
because the great functionary is having his solitary
hair briH'iantined inside, or something equally
thrilling is happening. To be a suite on a wet day
is a lugubrious billet.

This is pooh-poohed—the people would have to
contribute. A few doctors want convalescent

The Mayor of Charleville (Q.) called a
meeting for a Saturday night to take steps to
Gorsave the Diamond Jubilee, “up to the
knocker.” Charleville didn’t roll up. The meet-
ing consisted of the Mayor, the Town Clerk, and
a reporter. Tambaroora for drinks. Charleville is
not too beastly loyal. Shove a Saturday night in
one soale, and the Crown in another, and
Charleville takes the night out with its best girl.
* • * *
In King-street, Sydney, last week, a band
played “God Save,” etc., but not a hat came off.
In Sydney, people don’t even doff their hats to a
funeral.
* * * *
The meteorologists agree in believing that
there will be indications of rain yesterday. The
Bulletin’s faith in meteorology—as at present
supplied—therefore grows more vigorous.
A precise and professionally-en-
thusiastic warrant-officer from Sydney
has just spent three weeks drilling the
Broken Hill reservists. The reserves
total at most 18, and the average on
parade has been 11. And against the
cost of this instruction what is there
to show? The reserves are expected
to put in 18 drills a year. No per-
manent instructor being on the spot,
one is supposed to .visit the Barrier
every 12 months and cram the 18
parades into three weeks.
Down South, it is related, an in-
structor once visited a country town
to cram a dozen parades into a fort-
night. The local “good fellows ” got
hold of him on his arrival and kept
him magnificently drunk for 14 days.
They then put him on the train and
farewelled him with mighty cheers.
* * * *
The other day in a Melb. sub-
urb a sulky collided with the kerb, kill-
ing the horse and throwing out the
occupants. The gentleman was des-
perately hurt, but the lady was unin-
jured and bent down over him asking
pityingly, “ What can I do ? ” “ Get
away—quick, for God’s —sake before
cop—pers take your name and ad-
dress, ” gasped the bleeding man. She
fled. Evidently not the right person !
* * * *
People who dine at Northern
hotels often note the curious circum-
stance that, no matter how fat be the
bullocks slaughtered at the abattoirs,
no particle of oily tissue ever appears
on the beef at the table. The expla-
nation is simple. Dripping is the per-
quisite of the Chinese cook, who,
therefore, carefully cuts off every
vestige of fat from the joint and adds it to his
little store in the kitchen barrel.
* * *■ *
By way of a startling novelty, most of the
Australian weekly papers will distribute among
their readers, at Jubilee time, colored portraits of
Queen Victoria. The Bulletin hopes to present
to its constituents something even more wildly
original and interesting—such as a copy of Ham-
let’s soliloquy or the Ten Commandments.
* * * *
New Guinea is beginning to cock its hat
over its right eye. It has imposed a duty of 14s
per gallon on grog, and Samarai has been brought
under the operation of the Towns Police Act. All
it wants now is a women’s league, bloomers, and a
pound of hairpins.
The Maoriland
hangman was recently
sent to gaol for drunk-
enness, bad language,
and “tearin’ me uni-
form.” This plainly
shows that he is an un-
reliable character and
unfit for his position.
No person who is just
about to make his exit
from this world, owing
to strained relations be-
tween himself and the
judge, should be ex-
posed to the possibility
of having a gin-breath
and an ungentlemanly
expression blown into
his ear in his last mo-
ments, and the chance
of having the rope fixed
by a person with a
shaky hand and a
feeble, glassy eye.
Maoriland should at
least keep a sober hang-
man. Moreover, in the
interests of decency, the
appointment of a hang-
lady Beems urgently
called for. When the
law regretfully scrags a
female she should at
least be scragged by
someone of her own
sex, not pawed with
undue familiarity by a
strange man. The last
woman hanged, for in-
stance, was a lady by
education and instinct,
and when the coarse
official male clod inter-
fered with her back hair
to fix the rope it was
probably a severe shock
to her feelings, even
though he was sober
and not using lan-
guage at the time. So
long as the gallows ex-
ists the hanglady is as
essential to civilisation
as the female doctor or
nurse.
A Vic. school-teacher appeals, through the
Argus, for boots for the barefooted children, in
order to “ fill their hearts with gratitude and
loyalty this jubilee year,” and a Salvation officer
thus appeals in Sydney D.T.: —
There are thousands of children who are destitute of
ordinary necessaries of life, and seeing that H.M.’s reign
has been so successful in promoting the happiness of her
subjects, we think it will be a fitting thing to celebrate
the great event of the record reign by feeding the poor
and hungr3 r children.
Loyalty, either to a monarch or hungry children,
evidently leaves no room for logic. And a sense of
humor hasn’t the ghost of a show with a man who
can quote thousands of destitute children as a
proof of the success of the record reign in promot-
ing the happiness of Her Majesty’s subjects.
* * * *
The Jubilee programme for Melbourne is
a foolish-looking collection of small items which pro-
mise to yield about half as much festivity for the
masses as can be obtained during aCup-week. Apart
from illuminations (and they are not expected to
outshine the scattered flare of 10 years ago, when
the average display was one burst of light to every
two and a-half acres of darkness) the programme
offers no excitement for the “ loyal ” citizen ir
Miss ALMA STANLEY.
Photo., Talma.
return for the loss of a day’s work, or more, by
order of Government. At the Exhibition som-
-15,000 school children will assemble for the deadly
purpose of singing “ Gorsave,” unless somebody
intervenes, with a reminder that the infantile
chorus can’t possibly leave room for any audience
worth mentioning. Other historic events arranged
for Jubilee week are a review of troops (Melbourne,
as a rule, hates troops, especially in cold weather),
a promenade of firemen by torchlight, and a pro-
cession of fragments from the local friendly
societies. The remaining joys in store are con-
certs and things, such as happen at any time,
and hospitalities at Government House, which in-
terest very few people who haven’t filed their
schedules and made fat settlements on their wives.

this dreary array of functions, or whatever one
calls them, would at least hold out the induce-
ment to sightseers of a big crowd in the streets—
provided, of course, that the week doesn’t happen
to be horribly wet. But each suburb and outlying
district intends to jubilate on its little own. The
list of public puerilities designed by the contend-
ing intellects of divers mayors and councillors is a
ghastly thing to ponder over. Householders in
one suburb are exhorted to burn a candle apiece
behind the front parlor window, as a token of
devotion to the Queen, ere sallying forth to some
given spot where the German band will play.
There is no end to the scheme of al fresco gaieties
in damp and dismal places. This notice, however,
had better conclude with a brief sketch of
Heidelberg’s fell intent. At Heidelberg, it
seems, councillor A. will commemorate the Jubilee
by supplying a bullock, to be roasted on a spit to
be specially manufactured by councillor 8., at an
immense fire, and subsequently to be distributed
among the villagers. The bonfire, estimated to
consume 20 tons of wood, will occur at Heidelberg

bicycle and pedestrian racers are to run their wild
careers, whilst the school children stand around
the hillsides, devouring buns gratis, and inhaling
pneumonia from the misty atmosphere.

mature funerals that must result, sooner or later,
from one old lady’s longevity, it wishes that the
British crown had gone up the spout before Our
Sovereign Lady was born.

The penetrating powers of the Vic. police
received eloquent testimony at Prahran court,
when the bench remarked that “ some policemen
could get in anywhere if they could smell drink.’
For years the public has regarded the lonely
meanderer of the streets with a pitying eye, but it
"owseems that such sympathy has been wasted.
All that is needed is a balmy breeze of beer or a
waft of whisky, and the copious-footed wanderer
of the night has the open sesame of any pub. on
his bate
N.S.W. Comptroller-of-Prisons Neiten-
stein thinks a prisoner for debt should be made
work for his keep. There’s a lot more sense about
the Dutch plan of making his creditor pay for
him, and thus guaranteeing that he won’t be kept
m quod a moment after it’s discovered that he
can’t square-up. And a better plan still—don’t
put him in !
A recent Bulletin par. (from a lady corres-
pondent, by the way) stated that Melbourne
University students had been satirical at the
HARRY FURNISS.
A HAPPY PAIR.
expense of their venerable Chancellor for putting a
halfpenny in a kerosene-tin towards an Indian
Famine Fund collection, and that subsequently he
amended his jocular indiscretion by donating a
guinea. The secretary of the University Union
begs The Bulletin to contradict the aforesaid
yarn at all points. The Chancellor, who has ever
been most generous according to his means, was
not in the room when the kerosene-tin went round,
nor did he know aught of the collection until after
the money had been forwarded to the Argus.
And, as for chaffing him by means of placards, the
students would never dream of hurling disrespect
at an aged, invalided ruler whom they unanimously
honor. So there, now! The offending lady
romancer may consider her bonnet sat upon, and
her sunshade trampled in the dust.

Moral of recent Melb. divorce-case: Beware
of the scented man.
RODERIC QUINN
Photo., Kerry.
This is an excellent portrait of Rod eric
Quinn, one of the most promising of young Aus-
tralian writers, whose clever and interesting
romance of “ Mostyn Stayne ” has just been pub-
lished by George Robertson and Co. Readers of
The Bulletin have long prized Quinn’s tales and
verses, which link with tender sentiment imagina-
tive power of a high order. And as Roderic
Quinn is still a young man, improving yearly, it
may justly be hoped that he will rise to a very
considerable literary distinction.
* * * *
Hostesses who entertain cycling-callers
find that serving afternoon-tea to them, in dainty
cups of Doulton or Crown Derby, is like casting
pearls before swine. Cyclists usually arrive with
a thirst which no ordinary cup’s contents can
quench and an utter inability to “ drink fair.”

A carpe»ter has been sent up for three
months at Broken Hill for assaulting a girl named
Harris. The assault consisted of kissing the girl
against her will. By the way, what is the chival-
rous thing for a man to do under such circum-
stances ? Should the carpenter express his sorrow
at having kissed the girl, or should he aver that
her charms are so irresistible that had he the
opportunity he would kiss her again ?

When the lonely cop is pad-
ing his beat,
And his soul is somewhat sad,
And his feet
Drag across the pave so drear—
Then he thinks that Heaven’s near
When there comes a breath of beer
Down the street.
And as that fragranoe blows
In its course,
To his well-developed nose,
To its source
Does that lonely cop. prepare
To trace it then and there—■
For they’ll get in anywhere,
Will the force.
No wall is built, ’tis clear,
That oan stop
A policeman when the beer
Starts to pop,
For his cheek, so hard and cool,
Beats the burglar’s little tool,
And the diamond-drill’s a fool
To the cop.
WHEN WILL IT END?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I looked upon the Sorrow that was gnawing at my heart,
And the question that came thrilling dry or night would
not depart:
“When will it end? When will it end? When will it
end ? ” it cried,
And in the corners of my room the chasing echoes died.
Then there came a Voice anon :
“ Hear the answer true, O friend,
This bad thing will never end :
It will simply j ust go on.
“While your pulsing heart is beating, while the blood
flows in your veins,
While the summer ripens cornfields and they fall in golden
grains,
While your sinews lax and tighten, while your lungs
breathe glowing breath,
It will haunt you, it will haunt you, it will haunt you
unto death ! ”
Then I smiled and raised my head :
“ You spake lies to me, O friend,
Saying it would never end,
It will end when I am dead ! ”
Melb.
PAUL MELL.

Bot. : Re counterfeit coins. The popular method of
“ringing” a doubtful coin is effective in most cases, as
counterfeiters usually work with a soft metal, such as
lead or some of its alloys. A good sovereign does not
always “ring,” and may be a legal coin without. But
the cases are extremely infrequent, as the mints “ ring ”
all sovereigns before issue H.E.M. : Not this time
