A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 8, 1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
A popular player, who in private life is a
“ Mrs., was surprised, a few days ago, by seeing
her husband’s name in the passenger-list of an
incoming mail-steamer. She went to meet the
traveller and give him a wifely greeting, and
found, on enquiring about the bearer of the name,
that he was not her husband, but a gentleman of
color. The point of this true little tale is the
vague notion the profesh generally has of its
matrimonial belongings. It strolls casually about
the world, and may or may not meet its better
half at any turning.
Among the floating gossip and chatter at the
Fresh Air League ball in the Town Hall last week,
the women whose names cropped up as often as
anybody’s were two who are dead—Mrs. Carl
Fischer and “ Sappho Smith.” How in previous
years Mrs. Fischer had slaved to make this par-
ticular charity-revel a success, was told again and
again, and what “ Sappho ” had said ©f this one
and that one. It was a little remarkable, because
it shows a noticeable difference between Melbourne
and Sydney memories. Sydney folk have a public
memory, which is lacking in Melb. Hence the
hurry to get up the Clarke statue at once. A day
or two in Melb. is better than a score elsewhere
for consigning the past to cold oblivion. Sydney
remembers its notable people after their death.
Melb. often forgets them while they are still above
ground. It is risky for a Melb. man courting
publicity to live in a remote suburb ; by the time
the train has carried him to his home, the city may
have forgotten him.
The attendance at the ball, which was not so
large as usual, showed the want of an energetic
worker-up; or perhaps the stoutest-hearted
dancers are appalled at the prospect of eternally
waltzing round and round that vast acreage of
floor-space in the interests of charity. There are
so many charity-balls, and it is a long journey
from the northern gallery to the organ, and to
prance the distance backwards and forwards for a
solid four hours is hard work. The intended
decorations were drowned by the deluging rain of
the day before, and improvised ornamentation
was provided by roping healthy young saplings to
balcony pillars and tying up the electric-lights in
red paper-bags which shed a dim glow on all and
mercifully concealed wrinkles. The fancy sets
looked wooden ; ribboned polo implements and
glorified broomsticks with crinkly paper-heads
were clutched affectionately by girls who thus
each made sure of one stick for the evening The
supper-room door was guarded by a stalwart com-
mittee-man who admitted the banqueters in
sections, and once haughtily repelled an aide who
was hungrily searching for provender with his
partner. Govt. House was represented by all the
party, minus the head. Mrs. Ferguson, who
appeared for the first time in the most admired
of all her new gowns, a toney-looking confection
of blue and white, told her friends the exciting
story of the loss and recovery of her diamond
star : she missed it that evening on removing her
cloak at a friend’s dinner-party; a search was
made, and after hope was entirely gone the valu-
able was seen glittering in the gutter.
On board the Union Co’s splendid new steam-
ship Moana last week three huge entertainments
were given. On Friday and Saturdaynights guests
were received by Capt. Carey, who welcomed over a
thousand. The ship was swarmed over by hordes of
sightseers in every style of garb. The full glare
of brightness in the luxurious saloon drove out the
modest persons who came hatted and cloaked, and
they lurked in corners and on deck as if they were
passengers waiting for the ship to start/ One
girl rose to the occasion by taking off her hat
in the cloak-room, borrowing her young man’s
buttonhole-spray and pinning it in her hair.
The upper-deck, where dancing went on, was
a spaeious promenade, and theatre-wraps were
in evidence; one envied person strolled in in a
va,st - sleeved blue-grey garment, with spangly
trimming artistically applied, and much white
fur. A matron newly-returned from ’Ome dis-
played a short pale-blue cape which only Paris
could have produced ; beneath this dream dia-
monds glittered. Singing happened in the saloon,
which was crowded —the gazers above leaning over
the bannisters. One or two women looked like
Venus rising from the foamy deep—a great deal
of Venus and very little foam. The feeding was
a feature of the show —it went on everywhere;
and champagne flowed like water.
The girl who won the bicycle for being “the
most popular person ” at the Australian Fair in
aid of St. Mary’s R.C. Cathedral is a Miss
Hobson. The public paid 6d. a head to record
each vote, so an admirer with a £5 note to spare
might easily dominate the voting. Business was
brisked up during the last days of the Fair (which
will be opened again in Jubilee week) by the
popularity competition. One damsel stuck up all
and sundry with the request that they would put
in sixpence and vote for her; but her artlessness
was thrown away on an unimpressionable male
public, for she didn’t top the poll. Girls and the
clergy, between them, have wheedled out of people
£12,000 for the church, which, as the Rev. Tither-
adge remarks, is insatiable. What other church
could do the same !
Poke bonnets are on view and sale in some of
the fashionable shops for any woman loyal enough
to make herself a guy in honor of the Jubilee by
reviving the head-gear of 60 years ago. Some of
the bonnets are softened with lace and chiffon and
brightened with flowers, but the shape is still the
uncompromising “ Harmy ” scoop. It would be
disastrous for drapers if the example of Her Most
Gracious were copied in the way of fashion, for
that august lady wears white stockings and
eighteen-penny gloves, and the same kind of
ponderous black velvet bonnet, decorated with
white feathers on special occasions, has covered
the royal head for the last quarter-century.
For Jubilee night the arrangement has been
made to allow wheeled traffic, headed by a dray
containing some high-rank civil-servants, to pro-
cesh through the thronged Sydney streets for a
couple of hours to admire the illuminations. A
few miles of punctured gas-pipes to blaze forth in
flame on the night have been already erected in
Pitt-street, but up to date the preparations aren’t
taking anybody’s breath away by their magnifi-
cence. A “suggestion ” —one of many—has been
made that in order to give the public a chance to
get the best view of the illuminated war-ships a
shilling entrance-fee should be charged at Govt.
House gates, to be given to the Consumptive Home
fund.
The Victoria Club weekly meetings are now
established as solemnly-fashionable functions.
Chaperoned by two members as hostesses in their
turn, they prepare a programme, and all the com-
pany drink tea and munch light refreshments, for
which the two hostesses jointly foot the bill. On
Friday last Mrs. Russell-French and Mrs. Chris.
Bennett treated the company. Misses M. Wynn
and R. Reynolds sang, and Miss M. Fitz Stubbs
contributed her recent “Bridal Waltz.’’ The
most novel item of the afternoon was a juggling
exhibition by a child of 12, daughter of the Tivoli
electrician. The juvenile juggler was solemn,
and couldn’t be induced to talk, but her Provo-like
manipulations with bottles, plates, wooden boxes,
etc., were dexterously-clever, after only a few
months’ instruction.
Playing Beethoven is like stirring a fire and
making love. Every man has his own way of
doing it. Experts shook their heads over Law-
rence Phillip’s treatment of “the master” at his
concert last Thursday, but were enthusiastic over
his other efforts. His selection was unconven-
tional —a piano-recital without Liszt and Chopin is
a novelty—and he’s of a school as new as a man
can adopt without shooting ahead into the next
century. Modern composers don’t make that
frank appeal to the emotions on which the old-
fashioned writers of simple melody used to work,
but are all thinkers first and musicians afterwards
and get at the sensuous via the intellectual. Con-
sequently high-class concert-goers of to-day are all
musically literate, or assume to be. Piano-music
has developed much since the days of long ago
when a long-haired genius could bring down the
house with “Home, Sweet Home” with varia-
tions. A new soprano gave signs of a voice worth
cultivating, but used it nervously and crudely.
Lawrence Campbell, E. Strauss, and Burns-
Walker were all at their best and completed a
really fine performance.
At that very High and fashionable church, St.
Mark’s, Darling Point, where the parishioners are
still raging furiously at the Evangelical-minded
Synod nominators imagining a vain thing, a por-
trait of the Rev. Bond, the shepherd whom it is
proposed to import from England, has been hung
in the porch. This is a distinct score for his party,
as the photo, is calculated to win the most evan-
gelical churchwarden over to candles and incense-
burning, and attract the adoring attention of every
girl in the parish, for it shows a young and hand-
some cleric in his early twenties. As the hoped-
for incumbent is now over 40, his “present-day ”
picture might be somewhat of a disenchantment.
The face and form of another cleric, the Rev. A.
R. A. Bartlett, spoken of as a candidate for the
post, have been made familiar to the world by
Phil. May in his celebrated and now scarce
“Parson and Painter.” That the St. Mark’s
stipend of £5OO per annum, with perks for christen-
ing and marrying the local rich, should be regarded
as jhe blue ribbon of the profession shows several
things.
School-children are being presented with soft
metal medals at 3d. each which they hang on their
little pinafores incommemorationof “the 60th year
and longest reign of H.M. Queen Victoria.” This
intelligently-worded badge of loyalty has no re-
marks on Queen Victoria’s other reigns—it doesn’t
even tell how many she has had.
Matriculation exams, are on this week ; several
hundred boys are gathered in the Darlinghurst
Skating Rink, and over 200 girls are biting their
pen-handles thoughtfully in the University
Great Hall. It is a chilly place, and one kindly
schoolmarm has insisted on the girls of her coach-
ing taking rugs in which they sit comfortably
bundled up while they scribble away at their Latin
and physiology.
A few enthusiastic feminine wagerers went out
to Randwick on Saturday to see what they could
do by betting on the nod. The idea that there
was to be no cash-fielding depleted the stand, for
women don’t go to races merely to show their new
frocks. One sportswoman in Sydney wagers sys-
tematically, and keeps a “racing purse” and a
“housekeeping purse,” and both separate—a safe
plan. Contrary to expectation, some bookies
were doing business for cash in the usual way,
and their fair clients were “on it ” every time, in
a rather unusual way.
A Frenchwoman, with her head screwed on the
right way, and much fascination of manner, has
been doing well in Melb. by delivering weekly lec-
tures on her native literature to full and attentive
audiences. In Sydney this winter Madame
Juliette Henry intends to do the same, beginning
the course on the 16th June in Tattersall’s Build-
ings, Hunter-st. Madame has made a name for
herself as a lecturer, and, indeed, claims to have
given the Melb. lecturess the first hints for her
work, and some time ago a smart war of words
raged on the subject.
A Sydney medical man went to Bogan Gate
recently to report to the Govt, about the local
water-supply. The residents have damned a creek
into which they throw everything they don’t
happen to want. A tired-out inhabitant threw
himself in the dam, and a few days afterwards
the townspeople wondered what had become of
Jimmy Smith, whom they eventually fished out
of the water-supply.
Woman suffrage worked badly in a municipal
election in an out-back town where the two candi-
dates for this year’s Mayoralty were rival doctors.
Each was vigorously canvassing and working up
doubtful voters, but the election was generally
looked upon as a safe thing for one candidate
married to the daughter of a widowed property-
holder of the district with many votes at her com-
mand. To make all sure the son-in-law paid up
all back rates himself and drove mother-in-law to
the polling-booth with injunctions to be careful
and scratch out the right name. The old lady,
confused with oft-repeated instructions, elaborately
eradicated her son’s name every time, and trium-
phantly told him so when she came out from the
ballot—whereby the blow was softened for him
before the returns were known, and the rival
practitioner headed the poll by one vote.
A fine Sydney mansion was the scene of a swell
house-warming dinner-party, the other day.
Champagne broke up the drought effectually, and
a select quartette of prominent instrumentalists
were engaged to discourse sweet strains during
feeding-time. At the close of the spread they
were invited to an adjacent apartment, where they
were assured dinner would be served to them.
Two portly retainers laid out a large assortment
of silver, including a massive epergne, monogramed
napkins, etc. Expectations ran high among the
hungry musicians. Then the majordomo entered
with a large dish of—cold tinned herrings ! At
the same time, the assistant seneschal (in dress-
suit) placed upon the table a large decanter of —
water ! And that, barring bread and butter, was
all!
Miss Florence Schmidt, of Rockhampton (Q.),
a lady with a splendid soprano voice, gives a con-
cert at Sydney Town Hall, on Saturday night,
with good local support. Miss Schmidt’s com-
mendable object is to raise funds for a course of
European training, with a view to developing
into a second Melba, or something even greater.
Rockhampton isn’t a good place in which to
grow a great vocalist, but Miss Schmidt has
borne up against its stodgy atmosphere, has
learned four languages, and, with the aid of Signor
Steffani, has cultivated her talents with that
deadly persistence which makes ultimate success
almost a certainty. And the lady who brings
anything good out of Rockhampton deserves all
the encouragement this world can give her.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ The sensational story told the other day
in Melbourne Criminal Court about the “ drunks’
express ” —the midnight train from Melbourne to
Canterbury on wdiich, according to one witness,
females are commonly heard screaming for help
all the way—was probably spoiled by the name
of the prosecutor as much as anything else. She
swore to her thrilling tale of grief and outrage
under the cognomen of Eliza Muddyman, and
when the jurors had made quite sure that her
name was Muddyman, they acquitted the prisoner
straight off. Her only witness was named Foot,
and Foot stated that he never travelled in the
same compartment as a female upon the “ drunks’
express,” because it was “too dangerous.” In
other words, he had a very low opinion of the
ladies who travelled by the midnight drunks’
train and habitually shrieked for help. Probably
he thought that most of them shrieked until they
were paid to stop.
“A pretty Jewish wedding was celebrated in
Melb. on 2nd inst., the parties being Mr. J. H.
Joseph, a leading Perth (W.A.) merchant, and
the charming Miss Florence Jude, of East Melb.
“There was a smart mustering of military at
Lady Holled Smith’s garden fete for the Gordon
‘ Try-to-excel ’ boys. Colonels in grey check
* doubles ’ and sac coats ran dog-shows and raffled
hogs with impressive volatility. ‘Sweet Seven-
teens ’ went round proffering for sale ‘ infantry
brigade cakes ’ tied with blue ribbon in true-lovers’
knot, and pleading the cause of their brother
officers in meltsome accents. The members of the
‘ Lelamine ’ opera co. were billed for an act, and
this drew all the ‘society’ barrackers. The fete
was interesting from many points of view, and
successful. There was one hitch. A performing
dog (a decorated collie) refused to ‘ wag his tail for
his master.’ After many perspiring efforts and
desperate measures on the part of the showman,
the vice-regal party, with a suave apology, left the
ground and hastened into a more congenial atmo-
sphere, although Baby Brassey (who was there,
white-coated and tan-booted) wanted very badly
to stay and see that unhappy quadruped shake its
rudder.
“ Baronet Rupert Clarke threatens to abolish
his Rupertswood battery of “gentlemen” swash-
bucklers. It appears that the pieces of field ar-
tillery with which this body started 15 years ago
are now considered obsolete by the hawty youths
who compose it, and unless Govt, replaces them
with up-to-date guns they intend going on strike
under the direction of their boss. As the Minis-
try declines to spend money, the battery will pro-
bably fall through.
“ P r - Emily Ryder lost one great point in her
ladies only’ lecture, which Evangelist Varley
used to score freely with at all his addresses on
private ’ subjects to young men. No sooner did
the fat ex-butcher take the platform than he would
fix a cold, glittering eye at a far corner of the hall
and raising a sansage-like forefinger he would
call: 1 1 see a lady there in disguise. I must ask
her to leave the hall.’ Of course the whole thing
was only a trick of the trade, for no woman was
there at all, but the interest of the audiSnce was
worked to flashing point nevertheless, and after
Varley had apologised they would crane forward
to catch every word he uttered.

Cards of invitation have been issued for the
Govt. House reception on 23rd inst., the first time
on record that Melb. people have had to produce
invitations at an afternoon reception That
function up to date has been a public struggle in
which all classes have joined in heated endeavors
to grasp the vice-regal hand. It is reported that
three thousand cards have been sent out.

“A Melb. draper affirms that within three
weeks a thousand of his customers left in the mail
steamers for London, many without ‘ settling
up ! ’ and he says he may whistle for the money
they owe him till they recover from the expense of
their European orgy.
“ What will Bart. Clarke’s widow do with
the huge, barrack-like house in East Melbourne
left her by her departed spouse? It is a stupid
house for a hot country —utterly without verandah
shade. In fact, it is a slavish copy of the mansion
style of Grosvenor Place, London. It can’t be let,
it is too big for its owner, and sassiety having
forsaken East Melbourne for Toorak as a place of
residence, it would not fetch much if sold. The
house is very richly furnished inside, but it is
more fit to be a big club-house than a residence.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Artist Furniss and Manager Smythe.
It is remarkable that a dealer in brains like R. S.
Smythe should for six-and-thirty years have
managed all sorts and conditions of professionals,
from philosophers to chorus-singers, yet his course
has always run smoothly. He must indeed be a
man of infinite tact and a marvellous observer of
character. With any of his stars, which differed
so much from one another in glory, never a law-
suit in all those years. Latterly the Much-
Travelled has limited his operations to the higher
class of professional celebrities, as he calls them.
His latest is Harry Furniss, one of the most
amusing of the English Punch artists until
a few years ago, when he took to the
platform as an artist-entertainer k la George
Grossmith with pictures instead of a piano, and
in that rdle all over the United Kingdom, and
more recently in America, he delighted very large
audiences. Furniss must also be a man with
wonderful powers of observation, as his pictures
of English interiors, his splendid “series” of
English Parliamentary caricatures, and his
sketches of American life and character fully
show. He appeared in Melbourne to thoroughly
representative audiences, and there was but one
opinion as to the excellence and amusing nature
of his Pictures of Peoples and Parliament in Two
Hemispheres. He opens at Sydney Theatre Royal
on this Friday night.
Thomas Collins, a Cheshire man, the
pioneer settler of Narrabeen, N.S.W., has died,
aged 94. He had lived on his holding for 60 years.
One Far-Western (N.S.W.) station is keep-
ing its working horses alive during the drought on
chaff which costs £l7 a ton delivered. No wonder
boundary-riders are being mounted on bikes in
the country.
A striking feature of the ’97 drought in the
Far West of N.S.W. is that the stations with a
frontage to the rivers Darling and Murray are the
greatest sufferers, being the most bare of feed.
Their dams and tanks have also suffered most from
drift, dust and sand.
According to Mackay (Q.) Chronicle, that
portion of a recent issue of a Most Respectable
Sydney weekly paper that passed through the
Brisbane P.O. was impounded fora week, opened,
scissored of objectionable advts., and forwarded
in a mutilated condition.
The Bulletin is told that a certain N.S.W.
prisoner committed for trial on a sensational
capital charge has to pass under the gallows each
time he takes exercise—in the yard set apart for
officials during an execution.
Premier Seddon’s loyal boast that, in the
hour of peril, England will find the colonies be-
hind her, is delightful in its diplomatic vagueness.
The colonies, in that event, will certainly, as at
present, be behind England—they will take up a
masterly position about 15,000 miles in the rear.
A caucus meeting of the Q. Labor-party
was to be held on Thursday (when this issue was
published), _to settle the programme for the
coming session, and to choose a sessional leader.
A minority of Labor-members want to depose
Glassey, and Dawson and Turley are expected to
be nominated.
The unprayed-for tornado that accom-
panied the recent heavy rain in Sydney lifted a
suburban Presbyterian Church (the Moderator’s)
bodily from its foundations and knocked it up
against the fence. _ The Presbyterian Church will
now proceed to raise the wind again and shift it
back. By prayer or plate ?
Among Melb. Divorce Court’s recent full
crop of cases was one in which hubby related how
the house (whenever the Tertium Quid was pre-
sent) continually echoed with the sounds of pro-
miscuous kissing. The success of so noisily
demonstrative a lover shows how hopelessly crude
and inartistio women are.
Last week in a Sydney tram a would-be-
young lady wore a hat with a band which dis-
played in the centre the Queen’s head in gold; red,
white and blue flags to left and right; on one side
the date 1837, on the other 1897. Such loyalty in
a middle-aged girl is liable to be mis-interpreted
as proof of how long she has been wearing that hat.
" O Justice,” &c. In N.S.W. a bushman
gets three years for stealing a small tin of baking-
powder, while a policeman, for gratuitously com-
mitting a most brutal assault on a harmless,
broken-down man trying to sleep out in the park,
is let off with six months. Also that policeman is
allowed to select his own gaol, despite the perjury
which he added to his original crime.
Had the training - ship Sobraon been
moored so as to prevent her swinging to the wind,
the chances are that last week’s Sydney tornado
would have sent her and her swarm of imps to the
bottom. As it was, she heeled over till the water
poured in at her lower-deck ports. Had she gone
the rest of the way, the local bad-boy problem
would have been more or less effectually solved
for a year or two, at all events.

trip to Adelaide was the longest he had ever
taken in his life ! But, fortunately for his native
land, he has been to Maitland—and Parramatta.

Rumored that Rider Haggard is to be the
next “ man of letters ” to be “ elevated ” with a
knighthood to the proud level of every draper, or
boot-builder, or tripe-seller who happened to be
Mayor of his particular Little Pedlington when
it went on its face to welcome royalty. Not,
however, that it matters much, after all, about
Haggard, who is only a literary man in the sense
of having had a very thick sale for very thin work.
A Melb. dealer has Ricciotti Garibaldi’s
sword, which Ricciotti had to pawn. On the blade
is inscribed, “ Armee des Vosges,” and on the
handle “Ricciotti Garibaldi.” Yet it is a very
old sword, perhaps 200 years old, and not made in
our era. The guard, round the back of the hand,
is formed of the curved wings, body and legs of an
angel. In one of Ricciotti’s braggadocio Melb.
newspaper articles,- he described how he pinked a
German with it, against a door.
One remarkable feature about the LL.D.-
ing of Australian Premiers by Cambridge Univer-
sity is the fact that their own Australian State (or
nearly State) Universities never seriously thought
their prominent politicians worthy of such notice.
Sydney University has been running since 1852,
and such men as Martin, Dailey, Forster, and
Parkes have as ably, and more picturesquely than
any of the present Premiers, figured right under
its nose without being offered any academical
favor. And the first Provost of Sydney University
was a Cambridge man.
A Bathurst (N.S.W.) resident of 50 years
went out last week, when Mrs. -DeClouett died.
Her husband was a well-known racing man,
remembered far and wide as “Dublin Jack.”
When Ben Hall and gang raided Bathurst one day
in the sixties the first place bailed up was
DeClouett’s, whose racehorse, Pasha, Hall was
anxious to annex ; but Dublin Jack locked the
stable and defied the ’rangers, who were afraid the
solitary old policeman left in charge of the lock-up
would arouse the inhabitants and come upon them.
So they decamped without the horse.

AN.S.W. District Court Judge, under a
sense of slighted merit,.gives it cautiously out
that, unless soon promoted to the Supreme Court
Bench, he will go back to th,e Bar. The present
Justice G. B. Simpson, did that, and gained the
point of his ambition. The late Justice Innes
also stooped to conquer after this fashion.
Dear Bulletin, —The correspondent who
“ thought Chief-Secretary Peacock’s guffaw was a
journalistic myth ” (until he actually heard it)
should turn up his old files of The Bulletin,
which published caricature sketches of the Laugh-
ing Peacock six or seven years ago. The Bulle-
tin didn't invent Peacock’s laugh, but it was the
first advertiser of the dreadful noise, and must be
held largely responsible for the fact that Mr. P.
has turned his racket to notoriety purposes, in-
stead of toning it down.— Binks.
An eminent Liberal politician in England
lately told a Victorian at ’Ome that it was well-
known that Rosebery offered the Governorship of
Victoria to Brassey, because the “ old man ” was
such a terrible bore to party leaders, that they
wanted to get rid of him for a little time. It is
most likely true ; for when Brassey gets a good
listener, he will plant himself up against the wall
and talk, talk, talk, hum-hum, ha-ha, until his
victim positively has to invent an excuse to get
away.
The London cables tell of the reception of
the various Australian Premiers, and of the result
of the Derby race. But never a whisper about
“ Tommy ” Byrnes, Q. Attorney-General, who
bragged to his Warwick constituents that he was
“ going ’Ome as the Second Representative of
Queensland.” Have the English sat upon him as
a deadhead interloper, or mistaken him for
Nelson’s flunkey ? Or did Tomjoe merely go to
England on the cheap because he was briefed by
the Q. N. Bank? Will “ Echo ” please get on the
witness-stand ?
A Conargo man to The Bulletin :
Editor Bulletin,— Be your par. about the Streaky Bay
tragedv, in which you suggest that as “Banjo’s” Clancy
has'not been heard of . . . enquire what sort of per-
son he was last seen with.” I beg to state that “ Clancy
of the Overflow ” gave up droving about four years ago.
Since then he has been opal-mining at White Cliffs. I
know Clancy well, but it is long since he was at the Over-
flow Station. Four of his sons follow his erstwhile occu-
pation of droving : I noticed a par. in a recent Bulletin
about two of them riding bicycles. They are not, how-
ever, the first drovers to use the bike on the road. Some
time before “ Garry ” and “ Will ” Clancy got their wheels
I saw a drover named P. Smith running over the saltbush
on the One Tree Plain between Hay and Booligal on a
Hike. Smith said it was worth two horses, a man, and a
dog in a dry season.

The magic-lantern view of the
Lord Chancellor, shown by Harry
Furniss during his Humors of Parlia-
ment lecture, is considered strikingly
like a member of the Vic. Bar—W.
T. Coldham, the barrister who op-

Squatter Ricketson, one of the
broadest-chested and broadest-acred
of Australian land-owners, tells a
story of “ big ” Clarke, the founder of
the All the people of old-
time experience are aware that the
baronet s father was not overgenerous
in the matter of giving grass to travel-
ling stock. Ricketson, who fre-
quently accompanied his own mobs,
had camped for the night on one of
Clarke’s runs, when the old man
made a wrathful descent upon the in-
truders, and ordered them to move
on. Ricketson promptly grabbed the
founder’s ” nose between two iron
fingers and pulled it, not with in-
human violence, but in a quiet rumin-
ative style, that admitted of no fur-
ther conversation at the moment.
Thenceforth, Ricketson’s stock and
the owner thereof were left severely
alone when they travelled that way.
Barrister William Charles Wentworth took
part in an Equity case in Sydney court, last week
thus reviving interest in an old forensio repufca-
tion. The case concerned the rendering of the
will of the original Wentworth, whose grandson
the present barrister is. When he announced
himself as appearing in the case, a clean-faced
wig-wearer in the rear mumbled—
“ May this, thy last-born infant, then arise
To triad thy heart and greet thy parent eyes,"
distich out of his grandfather’s Cambridge
em on Australia.
As showing how much more honor
free - thought has from the present genera-
tion than from that of 50 years ago : in
1844, Fitzroy, then Governor' of Maoriland,
paid an official visit to the town of Nelson,
where George White (an old man of 60 years]
who formerly held an important appoint-
ment in the House of Commons) was Resident
Magistrate and Government factotum. Fitzroy,
calling privately on White, saw Tom Paine’s
works on his shelves, and, expressing his horror,
insisted on his destroying them or resigning!
White, a man of independent character but penni-
less, absolutely refused to do either, and was at
once dismissed.
Premier Turner, no doubt about it, devotes
his voyage ’Ome to a desperate attempt at getting
rid of his old self in favor of a new and lovely
Turner, whose appearance and deportment shall
“do credit to the province.” The story of this
modest attorney’s metamorphosis on the high seas
is truly painful. He undergoes hours of drill
every day, in company with the Jubilee troops
aboard, not because he suffers from a martial spirit
and a thirst for blood, but merely in the hope that
he will be able to cut a “dignified” figure, and
look like a Sir George, when he gets to London.
One can almost hear the unhappy man appealing
to Mrs. Turner, in the seclusion of their narrow
“ state-room ” —“How are my shoulders shaping ?
Do you notice any improvement about the legs ? ”
The voice of Mrs. T. replying : “ Stick to it,
George, and you’ll soon get a swaggah on you,” is
also audible to the imagination. Turner’s trade-
mark, his queer, crescent-shaped “billygoat,” has
been removed by the ship’s barber, and the im-
provement is said to be surprising. Note the
word “improvement”! It means that Turner
has more or less qualified himself for a tall hat,
frock coat, patent-leather boots, and spats. He
has thrown aside his original identity and degene
rated into a mimic.
From Christchurch (M.L.) Star :
During a banquet at Dunedin, to celebrate the Masonio
jubilee of Bro. Sydney Janies, one of the most respected
citizens of Dunedin, Bro. James disclosed the identity of
the fate Julian Thomas, “ The Vagabond,” concerning
which there has been a good deal of speculation. “ Per-
haps,” he said, “ many of you have heard of and read the
newspaper-writings of Julian Thomas, known as ‘The
Vagabond,’ who died in Melbourne in September last. At
the time of his death much curiosity was excited as to his
identity. This, however, was well-known to three of his
relatives and to other private intimate friends in these
colonies. It was thought best at the time, as he
had lived for a quarter of a century in Victoria, and was
highly honored under his pen-name, not to disturb
that name publicly, but there was no reason why
his true name should not be disclosed by his rela-
tives. As one of those relations, I have disclosed
his identity to my friends, and even to editors of news-
papers privately; but as I have lately been several
times asked if it was true, I now, on this occasion,
being the first time since his decease that I have spoken
publicly, take the opportunity of stating that his true
name was Stanley James, that he was born at Walsall, his
father’s name was Joseph Green James (my brother, a
solicitor), and his mother’s maiden name was Elizabeth
Stanley, of Leamington. My wife and myself have
numerous letters from him in our possession, and his
likeness bears a honored place on my wall. A N.S. Wales
paper stated that he went by the name of Sydney James
on his arrival in that colony. This is evidently intended
for Stanley James.”
Parkes used to relate that in the early
days of N.S.W. Parliament, some of the members
had a small “ school ” where, when debates were
dull or uninteresting, Ananias would reign
supreme, and undressed yarns were at a premium.
At one of these gatherings an altercation occurred
which threatened to end seriously, when Old Jack
THE SUPPRESSION OF CASH BETTING.
Ikey Mo will now field in Sydney Domain.
Robertson entered, and asked the reason of the
noise. One member had called another a liar,
and got the retort that he was a swindler,
and both demanded apology and retractation. “la
that all,” said the amiable John in a relieved tope.
“No use fighting over a blanky difference i of
opinion—you may both be right ! ”
When a leprous Chinaman was being con-
veyed per steamer to an Australian lazarette the
other day, he was boxed with a lot of sheep that
were for consumption by the inmates of the asylum
for the poor.
The joke o’ the canny Scot: “ There’s rtae
use o’ you,” writes one jeering Mac to another in
the columns of a Northern sugar-beet journal,
“usin’ the term ‘bastard’ to the people who
oppose you, hailin’ as you do frae Dumfries.”
Twenty-eight miners have just returned
from New Guinea utterly destitute, and are seek-
ing relief from the Q. Govt. Apropos, the sub-
stance of the following story is taken from Castle-
maine (Vic.) Leader :
Five men—M'Donald, Boyce, Kennedy, Dwyer and Dale
—left for New Guinea in January last. Landing there
“in good health and spirits,” they found the goldfields
peopled by men who were mere skeletons, of cadaverous
appearance, only haK clothed, and in many instances
without boots. It was a common experience to see
men of this description and in this plight, suffering
intensely from the effects of fever, dragging themselves
to their claims, supported by two sticks, forced to work
in order to obtain a little gold to procure the neces-
saries of life. This sort of thing gave the Castlemaine
party a slight shock, and they made up their minds
to go back at once. Some of the New Guinea boats
A PROBLEM IN ASIATICS.
Given the above, required to construct “ one
continent, one nation, and one destiny. ”
however, have got indifferent reputations, and they
fought shy of one ship from fear of shipwreck.
Sure enough, she foundered on the voyage home
The men then went to the first field they had visited, and
a couple of them lodged themselves in an old hut. “ A
functionary called the commissioner, who seemed to take
a special interest in the party,” warned them not to start
work in this place, and after two days the two men in the
hut were delirious with fever. With the commissioner’s
help they took the sick men down to the bay, and went
back to the diggings ; and two days later, when they re
ceived information that five passages had been booked on
the ship Clara Ethel, the other three were down with
fever also. Two of them died before the boat sailed, and
two others on the voyage to Cooktown, which occu-
pied four weeks. The only survivor (Dale) states
that on board the boat he was laid on the hard boards of
the ship’s bottom, and allowed to lie there till he became
fearfully chafed and fly-blown. Hearing that Boyce had
died, he crawled up to the deck to see him buried. The
body was simply bent double, tied with string cords,
and cast overboard, no weight having been attached to
sink it. The body floated, and followed in the wash of
the vessel within sight of the passengers for two whole
days. Presumably as a judgment of Providence, the boat
was wrecked, and the passengers transhipped to one of
the China mails. Dale spent five weeks in the Cooktown
hospital, and then came home. He winds up his story
with a little account of the New Guinea smells (every
country has its own peculiar stink) : There is abundance
of decayed and decaying vegetation, and this, subject as
it is to the excessive moisture and intense heat of the
sun, emits a most fcetid and offensive odor. The very soil
seems to be reeking with impurities, and at times, if a
digger’s pick happen to come in contact with a root’ of a
tree, the stench that arises is often smelt for a chain
distant.

MELBOURNIANA.


MORE MORAL EFFECT.
“ In the hour of peril England will find the colonies behind her.” — PREMIER SEDDON.


POLITICAL
POINTS
According to Ashton, M.P., N.S. Wales
Premier Reid’s net salary is only =£l3oo a year.
After all, politics are mostly wind and dust and
ashes.
The entire N.S.W. Civil Service is being
circularised for funds to oppose the Govt.’s Privy
Council appeal in the case of Adams v. Minister
for Works.
It is understood the Jub—hie—ilee is to
be signalised by such a shower of Knights that
they will come down like a rain of frogs, and
smash the ribs of the umbrella of Republicanism.
Daniels, M.P., himself a sort of patriarchal
grazing-farmeir, prophesies that there will be no
Land Rill passed in Q. Parliament this session,
because the Government wants to go to the country
next time with the war-cry—“A liberal Land
Bill! ”
It is 16 years since Victoria abolished the
Civil Service pension system, yet the outlay under
this head is still .£291,000 a year. And when
Treasurer Turner considers the long list of sturdy
old pensioners who won’t peg-out there is murder
in his eye.
THE HEIGHT OF AFFLUENCE.
SIN FAT (noticing the visitor's rings) : “ Him you
sissetah? Welly rich; all a-same heap
money” To visitor, sweetly : “ You keep-a
pub?”
C. H. Buzacott, who, when editor of Bris-
bane (Q.) Courier, supported the Nelson Govt.,
now that he edits and partially owns Rock-
hampton Record finds that “the Ministry has
become corrupt and inefficient." Not so. The
Ministry always was so; it is Buzzy who has
become open-eyed and candid.
So they will celebrate the Record Automa-
ton by “feeding the poor”—lovely phrase!
Horse-feed, cattle-feed, pig-feed, poor-feed—all on
the same plane. But ’tis a good idea : Loyalty
resides in the belly—Fatman proves it. “ Let me
fill a nation’s stomachs,” says the wise autocrat,
“ and I care not who fills its souls.”
The Tozer (Q.) Govt, continues to accept
lowest tenders for public contracts, with full per-
mission to the contractor to sub-let, to the sub-
contractor to sub-sub-let, and so on, as long as
there is a famishing slave compelled to accept a
chance for half-tucker. This in all departments.
It is accepted as a fact in Melbourne Tory
circles that Duncan Gillies will stand for Toorak
at next election. He had hopes of Barwon, but his
old colleague, Levien, will not make room for him
there. Reid, the member for Toorak, does not
want to stand again, and M'Kinley, the former
member, is such an abject cipher that his opposi-
tion is not to be feared.
Whig : ‘ 1 England is one-third Republican,
two-thirds Royalist. So what is the use of talking
about a Republic—yet ? It would steep the
land in blood and be a Cromwell error. Republics,
like Topsy, must grow. Meanwhile, console our-
selves by looking on Royalty only as a symbol.
We put the clothes on a puppet, and worship—
what ?—ourselves. Play-aoting.”
S.A. Register has analysed the Federal
Convention Hansard of 1218 pp. N.S.W. filled
432£ pp., Yio. 284, S.A. 274, Tas. 111 J, and
W.A. 23J ; the balance was formal matter. Bar-
ton as leader, spoke nearly one-sixth of the whole
volume. Of the 22 representatives who talked
most 16 are lawyers. As to interjections, Barton
is again easily first with 454, Turner, Reid and
Isaacs coming next.
A traveller, who recently visited the New
Hebrides, told the Endeavour Beacon (Cook-
town, Q.) that natives there “are sold like sheep
at .£6 to £8 or <£l3 a head on some of the trading
vessels. A dealer will visit a vessel and offer a
lump sum for the natives aboard, afterwards re-
tailing them out to his customers at an advance of
from £1 to <£3 per head according to demand.”
Apropos of much soulful rot that has been
published _ about the peaceful reign of Queen
v ictoria, it is interesting to note that Britain has
fought 39 wars during the time that peaceful old
lady has occupied the throne, and spent about
£280,000,000 in bloodshed and disembowelment.
Also, the bloated armaments of Britain, which
cost £12,600,000 in the first year of Victoria’s
reign, have kept on bloating till the annual bill is
now over £40,000,000.
Premier Seddon, banqueting at the Lon-
don Institute of Mining Engineers, told the com-
pany that if ever Britain experienced an hour of
peril she would find the colonies behind her. This
“ patriotic note,” says the cable jingo, “ was
greeted with enthusiastic cheering.” What an
utterly unnecessary item of intelligence is that
same “cheering.” As if we didn’t know they
would express their— hie —partially intoxicated
feelings in the usual manner !
In the Central district of S.A. Leg. Coun-
cil, where Labor was beaten last month, only 6438
persons voted out of 14,204 on the roll; in King-
ston’s constituency the proportion was but 488 out
of 1296. The races on Adelaide course (admission
free except to grand-stand) proved too powerful a
rival to the ballot-box —hundreds rushed off to the
course immediately after leaving work. In a less
degree this was true also re the Southern district,
where Labor-member Kirkpatrick lost his seat.
The horse v. the State !
Ex-Governor Onslow, of Maoriland, orating
in London: “My Maoriland experience of the
eight-hours day is that it tends to better work,
more comfort, and greater opportunities, but I
only approve of an eight-hours day when it is
effected by voluntary arrangement.” The Fat
Man’s yearning for “ Freedom of Contract ”
again. The eight-hours system is a good thing,
according to Onslow, yet it shouldn’t be enforced
because of something not explained. Why not
apply the “ voluntary arrangement ” all round ?
Honesty is a good thing, especially as it enables a
British earl to walk the streets without being
stoushed and robbed, but it should only be effected
by voluntary arrangement between the criminal
classes and the person they contemplate robbing.

S.A. Parliament re-opens this week.
Holder meets the Assembly with a larger majority
than his absent chief had in ’96, as the two bye-
elections furnished liberal members in Glynn and
Peake, as against Ash and Harrold, strong Oppo-
sitionists. By the way, Holder’s enemies are
alleged to have a nasty personal scandal in pickle
for him.
The N.S.W. Works Committee definitely
recommends that the new Sydney railway ter-
minus shall be in Hyde Park, at the top of King-
street, so after years of wrestling with the subject
the old, inconvenient, out-of-the-way Red fern
terminus seems doomed at last. Sixteen Govern-
ments have fallen asleep on this subject, but the
Reid Ministry is the first which has seriously
tackled it. Estimated cost of extension, <£850,200.
Viator: “The last Imperial-colonial con-
ference in London broke down through the extra-
ordinary conduct, as the Australian members
thought, of Lord Salisbury, then in his second
Premiership. Brusque to a degree, he went right
in their teeth. For instance, over the New
Hebrides. Berry, as Agent-General, made a
mighty splash. Salisbury as good as told the con-
ference he was going to let France take those
islands, and that the French had a right to them.”.
According to a Johannesburg paper, the
whole of Transvaal Kruger’s fearsome military
preparation is only a vast scheme tor the relief of
the burgher population, which, owing to crass
ignorance, rinderpest, antiquated farming methods,
dislike of hard work, destruction of the waggon
transport industry by railway extension, is practi-
cally bankrupt and face to face with ruin and
starvation. Kruger’s scheme is to form the Boers
into a standing army, on regular wages, and tax
the Uitlanders to pay the expenses.
It is announced that Rupert Clarke will
probably be returned unopposed for the old
baronet’s seat in Vic. Leg. Council. It is only
once in a pink moon or thereabouts that a Vic.
M.L.C. is not returned unopposed. The blank,
not to say blanky, indifference of the people to
their House of 48 Rich Landlords is the most
hopeless feature in the politics of the province.
Nobody has been injured at a Vic. Leg. Council
election, except through cold caught by falling
asleep in an exposed situation, for over 20 years.
Since trial by jury of 12 was established,
English Courts have been always endeavoring to
prevent juries disagreeing. The unanimity of 12
being the cardinal feature, several jurists argue
that when a jury reports a settled disagreement
half-a-dozen or a dozen of the bystanders who
have attended throughout the hearing should be
added to the jury, and such additions should be
kept on supplementing the original body until 12
out of the entire lot would be unanimous either
for conviction or acquittal. This, these jurists
contend, is the essence of trial by jury, and gets
rid of what to all parties is a tedious and stupid
block.

A SKETCH. — By Mr. Caleb Porter, of the
“ Sign of the Cross ” Company.
Gr. Randall, Queensland emigration agent
in London, told an interviewer lately that Queens-
land doesn’t want any readers of Reynolds’
Newspaper to come out here. Randall should
have said the “ Nelson Government ” instead of
“Queensland.”
Premier Reid’s baby, the forthcoming
Sydney Exhibition, will be handed round for the
inspection of English influential during jubilee
week. If Reid obtains Chamberlain’s imprimatur,
it is expected that the necessary sanctions will be
unceremoniously wrung from N.S.W. Parliament.
The forecast of a shrewd Brisbane (Q.)
politician: “ Believe me, one of Tozer’s next
moves will be to say to Bully Palmer, ‘ You must
resign the Presidency of the I,eg. Council ; Nelson
wants it.’ And also believe me that. Tozer will
find a means of inducing the intractable Palmer
to resign. Then—for I don’t think anything
serious, that is, fatal to Tozer, will come of the
Labor-party’s threatened stonewall, or of the
Alleged formation of a ‘ country-party,’ or of the
public overture made by Labor-member Sim to
Bob Philp to lead a ‘ Northern-party ’—then I
say, when Nelson returns and finds the Upper
House presidential chair cushioned expressly for
him, he will resign his portfolio and advise Lam-
mgton to ‘send for Tozer.’ And, mind you, Tozer
is versona grata with Larnington.” But Palmer
will be mighty hard to make resign. Probably
even a majority resolution of the Council asking
him to resign wouldn’t shift him.

A majority of the St. Kilda (Melb.) ceme-
tery trustees have discarded their crematorium
project in response to a howl of protest from 500
property-owners, many of whom dwell a mile or
two distant from the contemplated furnace. Yet
it seems that the vigorous whip-round for signa-
tures agin’ the crematorium was treated with con-
tumely by hundreds of old St. Kilda residents
who, because they live near a cemetery and are
deeply versed in boneyard lore, would like to have
a handy means of getting burned to ashes when
they die. Personal vanity and utter foolishness
are the only opponents of a furnace at St. Kilda.
Foolishness, inasmuch as the novelty would soon
have tended to increase the value of property in
its neighborhood.
A cremation society has sprung up in
Adelaide, but the promoters are meeting with
opposition from property-owners in the vicinity of
Rosebery township, where they want to pitch their
little oven These people already have a ceme-
tery in their midst, and object to a double ghoul.
N.S.W. Government offered £5O for the
discovery of the body of Lesagh, one of the sup-
posed Mountain Mystery victims. Lesagh turned
up alive, but, in searching for him, parties found
the body of Burgess, a genuine victim. Last week
the Govt, compromised with £35 down. How can
hair-splitting paltriness cf this sort be defended?
The contract morally was: £5O on finding the
body of a murdered man.

mHE FRIENDS of the late Mr. PATRICK NOLAN
are respectfully informed that his funeral took
place YESTERDAY (Wednesday), and NOT TO-DAY,
as previously advertised.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post freec or 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
Stock Report. —Pitt, Son and Badgery,
Ltd., report heavy supplies of cattle and sheep for
the month. With the latter, much inconvenience
has been caused through want of sufficient class-
ing and yarding room. Our ample drafting yards
have enabled our experts to handle all consign-
ments carefully and punctually. We have sold
through the yards during the month 4092 cattle,
and 79894 sheep and lambs. Calf and pig sales at
the City yards have also been extensive.*
A Popular Verdict.
Frossard’s Cavour Cigars are unequalled any-
where. They are a delicious smoke; mild,
fragrant, aromatic and medically recommended as
being free from nicotine. They are absolutely
uninjurious, and retain all the fragrance of the
fine leaf employed.*
The Australian Cycle and Motor Co. Ltd.
has somewhere approaching a million ladies’
cycles, and will work more for the wholesale than
the retail business. At the same time, the retail
buyer will receive every care and attention. There
are Simpson and American Humber racers to
mount all the cash men ; pneumatic brakes, wood-
guards and rims, and other sundries by the cwt.
The plain chain is on hand for its lovers, and the
Simpson chain for those whose ambition has been
fired by that chain’s wonderful records.*
Usury is one of the few profitable and
popular walks of Melb. commercial life now-a-
days, and, judging by the frequent press revela-
tions on the subject, is apparently so conspicuous
as to constitute a new blotch on the already much-
besmirched fair fame of the Southern city. The
abolition of one branch of the evil is contemplated
by Howden and Co. (Melb.), who announce else-
where their willingness to act as agents in the
sale of life-policies, reversions, &c. So that he
who is determined to sell his birthright may have
some certainty of receiving a reasonable equiva-
lent for same.*
Quite a Sydney attraction is the exhibition
of Wm. Cameron and Bros.’ tobacco in the hand-
somely decorated window of Meredith Bros., Pitt
and Moore-streets, Sydney. The names, “ Royal
Delight” and “Two Seas,’’ are most artistically
inscribed upon the plate-glass, while the tobacco
exhibition itself is the finest ever seen in Sydney.
Gold Bars, Royal Delight, Two Seas in plugs,
tins, glass jars, &o. ; Coronet Navy Cut, cigarette
tobacco, and every grade of color from Dark
Raven to sun-cured g;old leaf, are here excellently
displayed. The exhibition will raise Wm. Cameron
and Bros.’popular tobaccos still higher in public
favor.*
Harrington and Co. Ltd., 66 King-street,
Sydney, embodies the science of photography up
to date. The Editorial Dept, compresses Aus-
tralian photographic life, and the world’s photo-
graphic publications into The Photographic!
Journal ; whilst the Trade Dept, stands a model
with its shop, factories, storerooms, showrooms
for the most modern cameras, plate-charging and
developing rooms, laboratories, expert assistance,
&c., &c. The exhibition of 500 selected back-
grounds—instead of the expected dozen —indexes
the firm’s stock resources. One secret chamber,
perfectly contrived, manufactures the Invicta
sensitised albuinenised paper —the class standard.
Other heads are studio stands, all photographic
appliances, complete repairing department, print-
ing frames, photographic cabinetwork. No vessel
leaves Sydney harbor for Queensland, Westralia,
Maoriland or the Islands without a consignment
from Harrington and Co.*
The disfavor with which Butler’s captors
were welcomed by the authorities has made it
almost impossible for Sydney reporters to extract
news from the police. When the loss of the
Taiyuan gold was reported, no member of the
local force could be induced to loosen his tongue
on the subject, and visits to the departmental
heads were only productive of arguments on the
debateable question of newspaper ethics. For
years the papers have been accustomed to obtain
information which should obviously have been
held secret, and the sudden shut-up of the police is
a direct admission that someone must have under-
gone a bad quarter-of-an-hour.

ihe fullest faith seemed shining in your eves •
And on your lips, half-parted maiden wise ’
Dawned one rich smile ; and on your cheeks k glow
Of soft emotion witchingly did show.
Sweeter than all of these, your subtle sighs
a l loVe ' gladness - Thus the smiling
Rained radiance on one flattered fool below.
O admirable actress ! Was it, sweet
Practice made perfect this your deep deceit ’
Or did you simply revel in one lie,
Craving success, naught dreading but defeat
And deem such sport with hearts full-womanly

Wellington (M.L.) city councillors have
decreed that no lady is to stay more than half-an-
hour in the municipal swimming-baths. Hitherto
the sweet creatures who got in first stayed so
long doing their back-hair after they came out of
the water that the second and third batch never
got a fair show—by the time it arrived night was at
hand, and the water was cold, and it was so dark
that it could not see to fix up its hair at all. The
awkward question now is how to enforce the new
rule. The caretaker is a male person, so he can’t
rush in and capture the lady who is overdue with-
out outraging the respectability of .the establish-
ment. He can’t even stand on a chair and put
his head over the wall to remonstrate with her.
All he can do is to beseech the late female through
the keyhole to hurry up, and as he won't know
which one it is, and as a lady with her back-hair
in her mouth and a pair of curling-tongs in her
hand isn’t a responsible being, he will probably not
even evoke an answer. The only feasible idea
seems to be to fix each dressing-room with a
spring floor which will throw the occupant right
over the fence when her time is up, and throw her
bonnet and her boots after her.
A roasting midsummer day, and not a
breath of air in the hot sandpits where 125 of
“ Creer’s lambs ” (the Sydney “ unemployed ” en-
gaged on relief-work) are shovelling sand into tip-
trucks. Up on the bank the ganger cursed the
heat and looked longingly at the distant smoke-
draped city ; down in the pits the “ unemployed ”
blasphemed, and sweated, and fainted. Among
the sweating, steaming men the ganger suddenly
caught sight of one in a flannelette shirt and white
tie—evidently a fellow who had “ seen better
days.” The white-tie man was nearly exhausted;
his stroke was spasmodic, his cracking lips were
drawn back from his dust-grimed teeth ; he reeled
at every throw, and his shovel was never more
than half-full. The ganger came along just above
, n n i g ! ared d ° WI l at him, and the white-tie man

‘ • pay, mate !is that a shovel or a porridge

ridge-spoon you are not fit for your billet; but
perhaps they fed you with it when you were young,
as it is just about large enough for your mouth.”
Then the ganger blew up.

SYDNEY, N.S.W.


SPORTING NOTIONS .
Sydney sports of the past--foot-racing, boat-pull-
tng, boxing—not to mention dog and cock fighting. And
now the turf looks like going the same way. Fine subject
for a sporting Gibbon—the Decline and Fall of Sydney
Sport.
• * * •
The present N.S.W. anti-betting crusade was
started some time ago, when a couple of obscure persons,
probably because they did not care to swell the club’s
gate-money, got fined for betting outside the fence. Then
the “place” definition became a regular farce.
* * * *
Bookmaker Alf. Josephs on the recent crusade
against Sydney betting “ Well, the bookmakers of Syd-
ney have brought it all upon themselves. They have
made the pace so warm by the pernicious shop betting
that the authorities were bound to try and stop betting
altogether. Why, in no city in the world would such a
state of things be permitted for a day. In all your princi-
pal thoroughfares shop betting is in full swing, and wagers
are taken for a sixpence or a shilling. This, in my opinion,
is depreciating bookmaking.
Bookmaker Westbrook thus voices an old truth:
—“ I was on the London Stock Exchange before I came to
Australia, and I have also laid the odds on every race-
course of note in England, and I can say without hesita-
tion that I have seen more men ruined in one day by
gambling in mining stocks than I have seen go under in
10 years of my turf experience.”
Bookmaker Humphrey Oxenham on the betting
situation :—“At present there is racing on, in and around
Sydney, about five days a week all the year round. In
former days there were only two or three days’ racing in
the year at Randwick, Tattersall’s Club meeting at Christ-
mas, and a couple of meetings at the Hawkesbury, which
were looked on as more of picnic meetings than anything
else. Then it was a holiday to go to races. Now it has
become a business. Then horseowners took their strings
all round the country, and the provincial towns were
greatly benefited by the healthy meetings which used to
take place periodically. If races were held in Sydney
only about once a fortnight, I think much of the evil
would be done away with.”
• • • •
The death of Mr. I. J. Josephson, better-
known as Mr. “Ikey” Josephson, removes another
landmark of old Sydney. Mr. Josephson was ever
fond of the briny, liis yachts, Pert and Emma, being
successful racers a lifetime ago, and, up to the time that
his illness was brought about by garrotters, some three
years since, he never missed a morning dip in the briny,
winter or summer. “ Ikey ” was a small man, but full of
pluck and “devil” as a soldier-ant, and, when 70 years
or more of age, his dapper little figure, with pipe in mouth
and hat hanging rakishly on his left ear, could be seen any
day at the Oxford Hotel, playing even then a good game
of billiards.
THE LATE “ IKEY” JOSEPHSON.
During the betting-debate in N.S.W. Assembly,
Messrs. Crick and Hassall held a brief for the Australian
Jockey Club. “The A.J.C. members did not make a
shilling out of racing, they raced for sport,” said Mr.
Crick. Maybe, but he forgot to remark that many mem-
bers of the A.J.C. had a queer way of showing it by in-
sisting, under threat of “scratch,” upon having their
pound of flesh when a Tattersall’s sweep was knocking
about. As regards the conduct of the sport, it. is
notorious that the only racing body about Sydney which
acts fearlessly and without favor as regards “stiff” run-
ning is the Rosehill Racing Club. Reversals of form that
would paralyse a five-days-and-two-nights-a-week pony-
follower occur at Randwick meetings unnoticed, so long
as the offender is influential or a big-entry man ; but woe-
betide the countryman who brings a horse to Sydney and
tries any funny business at Randwick. It is not so long
since Rosehill Club tackled an influential A.J.C. man and
warned him, his horse, and all concerned, off their course
for allegedly running “ stiff.” This little affair, no doubt,
escaped Mr. Crick’s memory but now that he is reminded
of it, he will perhaps move the adjournment of the
House to state that he knows of at least one A.J.C. man
who runs for “ splosh ” as well as sport.
* * * *
A recent shindy in N.S.W. Assembly arose
because the two horseowners attacked were originally
more sinned against than sinning, and one of them was
son-in-law of the M.L.A. who took up the cudgels.
Racing is supposed to improve the- breed of
horses—a plea that does not apply to the pony branch, for
its tendency is to reduce size. But the War Office authori-
ties think Australian horses inferior to those from Argen-
tina, where racing is comparatively unknown. By the
way, does racing tend to improve the breed of men ?
* * * *
With his presumedly most dangerous opponent,
Vesuvian, amiss, the Irish-bred colt Galtee More (Kendal
—Morganette) easily won the English Derby from his old
opponent Velasquez. The Oaks was won by Limasol
(Poulet —Queen of Cyprus), bred and owned by the
veteran Newmarket trainer Tom Jennings. As in the
Derby, Lord Rosebery had to put up with second place,
his flying filly, Chelandry, being unable to repeat her One
Thousand Guineas victory.
• * * *
The result of the Derby recalls the opening
lines of a ballad by Charles Kickham, Fenian poet and
novelist
“ My name is Patrick Sheehan and my years are thirty-
four,
Tipperary is my dwelling-place, not far from Galtee
More.”
The honor of winning the Derby, for which
wealthy parvenus like North and Hirsch, as well as
men like Bentinck, were willing to sacrifice tens of thou-
sands, wasthrustupon John Gubbins, ownerof GalteeMore,
rather than sought by him. Never much of a horsey man,
he was disposed, on inheriting the property of his brother,
Capt. Gubbins, a genuine sportsman, to auction off the
stud, but was dissuaded by friends and relatives. It is re-
corded of Capt. Gubbins, the founder of the stud which
produced this year’s Derby-winner, that he once went
into a cavalry charge armed with a black-thorn. As his
native place on the borders of Limerick and Tipperary
was the main convincing ground of the “ Two-year-olds
and “ Three-year-olds ” —lrish factions which waged a long
and bloody feud over a dispute about the age of a heifer
it is probable that he regarded the “ bit av a shtick” as a
formidable weapon, as well he might.
Galtee More, though Irish bred and Irish
owned, was trained at Beckhampton in Wilts, a once
famous coursing-ground, and the site of some interesting
Druidical remains.
* * * *
Adelaide Racing Club put .£41,934 through the
totalisator at its Birthday meeting, which means £3150
in commission. Never before was racing so healthy in
S.A.
* * * *
The Newminster horse, Beverley, who has often
dropped his backers in the hole, ran a real good race at
Bendigo, last week, under the influence of half a bottle
of whisky. The Newminsters incline to roguishness, un-
doubtedly. Newman has many a time disgraced himself
in public, and the great Newhaven —well, if he isn’t a
rogue, why has his “form” sometimes varied two or
three stone within a week?
* * * *
The V.R.C. Grand Nat. Hurdle Race will be a
very open affair if Don John and other novices take
kindly to the sticks and are brought out to battle on the
day.
* * • *
The two-year-old racing in Australia this season
leaves Aurum in the position of facile princeps, with Fleet
Admiral, Keera, The Hypnotist, Patriot, Amberite, Kilt,
and the sprinting wonder, Key, high up. Unless some
“ dark ” wonder is sprung on the racing world next season,
the abovementioned youngsters should cut up the leading
classic events among them. The Carbine colt, Amberite,
has been judiciously treated during his two-year-old
career, and promises to be a three-year-old of more than
ordinary merit.
* * * *
The two-year-old Kilt (Lochiel —Mantilla), who
is not far removed from the best of the season, has been
bought by the Westralian owner of Le Var, with the
hopes of winning W.A. Derby for the second year in
succession.
* * * *
The oft-scratched and mysterious Aurea was
not allowed to give her multitudinous backers a run in
Exeter Stakes at Randwick on Saturday. Perhaps when
the public are off her she will get home all right in Rose-
hill Cup next week.
• • * •
As there is a big sweep on Rosehill Cup, run
next Saturday, no less than §0 horses have been “ put
together ”by the handicapper. True Blue, Aurea, Pre-
caution, Moorefield, Milkmaid, Survivor and Rebel Queen
cannot complain of the imposts allotted them, and, per-
chance, one or more of the plaoed will be found included
in this septet.
• * * •
Under the auspices of the A. J.C. racing, with
its natural concomitant betting, went on just as merrily
at Randwick last Saturday as if the crusade against the
“bookies” was as dead as Peter the Hermit. In the
Hurdle Race the favorite, Aeolus, fell and broke his neck,
a serious loss to Mr. John Mayo, and the uncertain Cornet
decisively beat Favor. Two-year-olds again showed their
superiority to the other aged animals by running first
and second in the Exeter Stakes, The Tola being lucky in
winning from Toxteth, who was much interfered with.
Miss Nora (Nordenfeldt— Lady Nora) won the Ellesmere
Stakes for Mr. S. Hordern, but as the punting public are
“ not taking” Hordern’s colors at present, she started at
Bto 1. Vanitas was again backed, and finished down the
course, whilst the unlucky Kallatina had to put up with
second place. True Blue appeared all right and was
heavily supported, but could get no nearer than fourth.
Overjumping himself lost Gladstone the Steeplechase,
and the very useful Sovereign landed the shekels for his
party in most convincing style.
An apparently startling reversal of form, for
which, as the A. J.C. has taken no public notice of it, there
is, no doubt, a good explanation, was afforded by the
running of Sovereign at Randwick on the last two Satur-
days. At Tattersalls Club-meeting on May 29th, Sovereign
started in the Welter Handicap at 10 to 1, absolutely
friendless, and ran nowhere. Last Saturday, in the High
Weight handicap, Sovereign went like wild fire from 8 to 1
to 3 to 1, at the last moment, and duly landed the good
thing.
* * ♦ *
Five horses ran a dead-heat for first in Too-
woomba (Q.) Short’s Handicap. A handicapping record.
• * m *
The best game so far this season in Sydney
senior football was played on Saturday last between the
Randwicks and Wentworths. The game was devoid of
brutal roughing, and even when the umpire erred in
awarding Randwick a goal which it never earned, Went-
worth took its gruel good-humoredly. At half-time the
game stood, Randwick 7, Wentworth 3 points, but the
latter pulled up, and but for the umpire’s mistake the
game should have ended a draw. Pirates played Wallaroo
on the Agricultural Ground, the play being pretty equal
during first half, Pirates 3, Wallaroo nil, but the Pirates
took a lead in the second, eventually winning by 14 to 3.
University played Paddington a most stubborn game on
the former’s Oval. No score up to half-time ; and it was
not until just before finishing that Shortland got over
rather luckily, and scored 3 for the University.
* * * •
Saturday’s football match between Collingwood
and Fitzroy was reminiscent of the games of five years
ago, when all Melb. used to roll up with its lunch in its
hat to get a good seat. The surrounding bank was
crowded, and, as the game was one of the good old blood-
and-hair sort, the spectators barracked as in days of yore.
in the expectation of a free stoushing-niatch. The games
between the clubs of these adjoining Sdburb3 al „^. 3 t the
exciting-for the ’Royites and the Woorlsies are about the
only Melb. clubs which have managed
clientele of barrackers. Saturday’s match recalled toe
good old times of the early nineties, when the ;
thick with half-bricks and the umpires used to escape i
false beards. After some real live play, which should do a
heap to galvanise the moribund game, the Woodaes
by 5 goals 7 behinds (37 points) to the Royites 2 goals
9 behinds (21 points). + ,
The modified system of electorate football,
adopted in Adelaide this season, has partia J
public interest. So far, South Adelaide, Port Adelaide
and Norwood are level for top place.
* * *

* * * *
News that the Vic. rifle-team in England put up
a fine average score at their first practice is full of inter-
est. Practice, in shooting or billiards, is not a reliable
guide to achievements “on the pinch” when a gazing
crowd holds its breath and the silence can be felt by tne
performer ; but up to now “ nerve” has been re . oa ° ,led a
strong point with Australians abroad, a few cnoae “. cdl '
lapses notwithstanding. The Vic. team, anyway, finds no
difficulty as regards the light.
* » *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen is suffering from
swelled head, and has started to break up what promised
to be a vast association. Armidale wanted a carnival on
June 22, but the League refused permission, as it is hoia-
ing a meeting in Sydney on the same day. As well might
the A.J.C. refuse to sanction meetings throughout tne
country on, say Queen’s Birthday, as they hold a meeting
on that day. The Northern districts now talk secession
from the League and the formation of a body of their
own. „ *
* * * *
Rupanyup (Vic.) rifle club some time since
challenged ten men a-side, at 400, 500, and 600 yards, in
England, Canada and India. Six teams responded, and
the shooting duly came off. Rupanyup was beaten by a
Scotch and two Indian teams, and in turn beat three
Indian teams. Wind and weather conditions may have
been so dissimilar, however, that it is hard to gauge the
respective merits of the teams.
Elias Laycock, whose portrait, as he appeared
in ’BO, is here given, has lost his situation as ranger at the
N.S.W. National Park, and his old-time friends are giving
a variety entertainment at the Opera House, Sydney, on
his behalf. Laycock went to England in ’BO and won the
ELIAS LAYCOCK
rich Hop Bitters sculling contest, winning four successive
races before going down to the then invincible Hanlan
early in ’Bl. The blue-eyed Laycock, with his golden
Saxon beard, was a fine figure of a man in those days, and
a great favorite with the Thames watersiders, who
christened him “The Bushranger.’

Probably finding that “retired champions ” do
not “draw” so well, Fitzsimmons is said to be reconsider-
ing his determination against another match. Anyhow,
Corbett will have to wait, as he kept Jackson and Fitz!
waiting, and may be put off with the excuse, “Fight
someone else, to prove you’re in my class.”

A ’Frisco club offers £2600 for a match between
Choynski and Fitzsimmons. What an ass Fitz. must be
if he does not gather in trifles of this sort before his sun
sets !

Bill Slavin and Wolff Bendoff, the two greatest
mugs who ever put a glove on in Australia, were recently
haggling over a match in London. Failing a match, Ben-
doff talked Peter Jackson. Great Scott!
* * * *
The Mrs. Fitzsimmons who distinguished her-
self at the recent fight by her advice to hubby as to the
best means of “stiffening” Corbett, is not the Sydney
girl who followed him to share his fortunes across the
Atlantic. Like many other great men when they rise in
the world, Fitz. got tired of the “old woman” who
washed and mended for him in his early days, and soon
wanted a more stylish consort. So he cast his eye on the
buxom and shapely Rose Julien, of the same variety troupe
Mrs. Fitz. No. 1 divorced him—he had previously failed
to divorce her, naming as co-respondent his present
brother-in-law and chief barracker, Martin Julien f

England and America are each claiming Fitz-
simmons as its own since his defeat of Corbett. Australia
and Maoriland had as big a share in making the <>reat
fighter. History records that “ Kildare was in 1497 re-
moved from the Deputyship, which was in time vested in
Walter Fitzsimons, Archbishop of Dublin, a declared
enemy of the Geraldines.” Fitz’s Irish descent is un-
doubted. Even his birth would not make him English
for the Cornish, like the Welsh, are more Celtic than the
Scottish Highlanders. e
England likes to claim the dog on top Like
Mace (the gipsy), Fitzsimmons, the Cornish-born son of
an Irish policeman, is hailed as a champion specimen of
Anglo-Saxon manhood.

The scratch men in a recent Tas. road-race were
curiously blocked. The course crossed the railway line at
one point, and as the rear men came up the radv^ ay a Kat ®?
were closed-the train was approaching. Thus about
were lost, and the blocked ones were out of the
hunt. , , . •

Dr. Youll, Melbourne’s ancient coroner, says
that if cyclists continue the road-racing game, his salary
must be increased. Cyclists have no more. business to
road-race on public highways than equestrians.

E. LENTHALL OLDFIELD, Principal.
Parson Edgar, when before Vic. Royal
Commission on old-age pensions the other day,
gave some instances—out of scores known to him
—of persons who had been sent to gaol for no
crime but poverty. An old man was found by a
lady-missionary in. an empty room, foodless and
paralysed. He was taken to all the hospitals and
was refused admission by all. The police then
took charge of him, and he was committed to gaol.
A few weeks afterwards he died in the gaol hos-
pital. Several highly-respectable women had also
been sent to gaol, having been convicted of desti-
tution. Incidents of this kind are of almost every-
day occurrence in Melbourne.
“Railway-Man ” to The Bulletin :
In reply to M'Gowen, M.P., Premier Reid stated that
the train accident at Redfern on Ist Feb. “ took place
in consequence of the driver of a train leaving the plat-
form whilst the signal worked from the station-box was at
danger against him.” According to which on N.S. Wales
railways there is no check on the driver’s reading of the
starting-signals. If that individual goes suddenly color-
blind, as men sometimes do, or, as in the case in question,
simply makes a mistake, a whole train-load may go to
Sheol because there is no check on the driver’s liability to
human error. A driver has to pick his signal out of a
dozen or two others, all about one or two hundred yards
away, and take his chance of promotion or Hades on the
result. And yet we have anti-gambling laws. The officer
in charge of the platform, or whoever starts the.train,
and also the guard, should act as a check on the driver
and share the responsibility. In Maoriland they do.
. Also the starting-signal ought to have a duplicate indi-
cator in a safe place on the platform, where starting-
officer and guard can see it, without having to jostle and
walk half-a-mile down a crowded platform to see the
signal while attending to about a dozen other things.
As a set-off to a well-advertised case in
which an adopted daughter caused trouble betwec n
her fond “father” and his wife, a Melbourne
husband seeks to divorce his wife on account of
her alleged unmaternal affection for an adopted
son. The lady being childless and, therefore,
unfitted for nursery management, decided to
satisfy her yearnings by adopting a boy of 16, who
had come safely through the measles, whooping
cough and other infant troubles, and would be
quite capable of appreciating a mother’s love at its
proper value. He was a new-chum youth from
Dr. Barnardo’s notorious Home, and seemingly he
gave complete satisfaction to both parents during
the first few months of his soniiood. But,
one sad day, the husband, so he avers, found
Madam seated upon their new boy’s knee, after
which time the boy ceased to give satisfaction to
his male parent by adoption. “ Mother ” clung to
him, however, and when she left for England two
years ago her boy, it is averred, sailed to the same
country in another ship. The divorce petition,
which will come to court again in September, is
based upon allegations of adultery between mother
and “ child ” previous to their departure from Mel-
bourne. This story, as far as it has gone, tends to
prove that an adopted son, who happens to be
full-grown ere his mother takes him to her arms,
amounts to much the same thing as the common
or garden sort of lodger.

March 7th, 1895.
To Herr Rassmussbn,
Proprietor of the Alfaline Remedies.
Dear Sir,—lt gives me great pleasure to inform you that
I have derived great benefit from your celebrated Alfaline
Remedies during my exploring expeditions throughout

Mr. Button writes later:— “The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”
