Specialists Fail and Doctors are
Baffled.
terrible condition of a botany
BAY GIRL.
WAS POSITIVELY WASTING AWAY.
GALLOPING CONSUMPTION FEARED
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS WERE
TRIED, WITH THE RESULTS
GIVEN BELOW.
Miss G. Harris, of Botany Bay, to a special
reporter who had been dispatched to interview
her, said :
“ Until within the last two or three years I was
always in the best of health, and I fear that what
occasioned my illness was a cold I contracted in
going from Parramatta to Ashfield. I commenced
quite suddenly, as it seemed to me, to suffer from
indigestion and constipation, which were my chief
ailments. I had a pallid appearance, and invari-
ably a tired weary feeling, and also suffered
horrible torments from racking headaches. I
went to several doctors in Parramatta, but none
of them did me any good. I also consulted a
specialist with a like result. I then tried a large
amount of homoeopathic medicine, but it appeared
to me to be a sheer waste of money. I then went
in for a much-vaunted and largely-advertised
liquid specific, which had not the slighted effect
upon me ; in fact, I threw the greater portion of
it away in disgust.
“ I was now positively wasting away, and feared
I was going off into a galloping consumption.
“One afternoon, towards the beginning of
January of this year (1897), on glancing through
the columns of the Evening News, an advertise-
ment headed ‘ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People ’ caught my eye, and I read through it with
interest, as I recognised in two or three accounts
of where cures had been effected the cases were
almost identical with my own. Anyhow, I deter-
mined to get some as a forlorn hope, with w. at
results I will tell you.”
“Did you experience any relief after the first
box, Miss Harris ? ”
“ I can hardly say I did, but something induced
me to go on with them, and I thought, at any rate,
I ought to give them a fair trial. After the second
box I felt decidedly better, so naturally I went on
with them. My appetite improved wonderfully.
My mother will tell you that prior to taking the
Pink Pills I did not eat enough to keep a baby
alive, and it distressed her beyond measure to see
my poor appetite. It pained me so much to swal-
low that I could only exist on slops, milk and
arrowroot being my chief nutriment.”
“You, of course, continued with the Pills all
the time, Miss Harris?”
“ Oh, certainly ; for I was feeling better day by
day, and I might say, without exaggeration in the
slightest, after each dose.”
“You certainly do not look like an invalid now,
Miss Harris.”
“I do not feel like one either. All this good
has been effected by that simple remedy, ‘Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills,’ which I think no household
should be without. I have already persuaded
many of my friends to purchase them ; it has been
an easy task, for they see how well I am looking,
and how different I am in health and spirits, and
I suppose they think that the proof of the pudding
lies in the eating. Of course I tell them all that
it is Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and they alone,
that have made me feel as I do, and look as I am.”
At this stage Mrs. Harris interposed : “It is
certainly quite true, all that my daughter has
stated. She is ever so much better, and looks like
herself once more. I cannot say too much in
praise of the marvellous medicine that has restored
her to health and strength.”
The gratifying results following the use of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills in the case of Miss Harris
prove that they are unequalled as a blood-purifier
and nerve-tonic. In the case of young girls who
are pale or sallow, listless, troubled with a palpita-
tion or fluttering of the heart, weak and easily
tired, no time should be lost in taking a course of
Dr. Williams’Pink Pills, which will speedily enrich
the blood and bring a rosy glow of health to the
cheeks. They are a specific for peculiar
to females, such as suppressions, irregularities, and
all forms of weakness. These Pills are genuine
only with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People, and are sold by chemists and by
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company, Queen’s Place,
Sydney, at 2s. 9d. a box, or six for 14s. 9d. They
invigorate the system after overwork, worry and
indiscretions of living.*

CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY

Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure, is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.’’

‘ The doctor sees all the weakness of
mankind, the lawyer all the wickedness, the
theologian all the stupidity.”—Schopenhauer.
Q. Police - Commissioner Parry Okeden
tells some queer stories about Q. beche-de-
mer skippers. It seems these joyous ruffians
are m the habit of inducing, by presents,
Oarpentarian niggers to come aboard their
craft. Once aboard they are whipped off to beche-
fle-mer stations on the Barrier and elsewhere,
the men being compelled (if they ever wish
to get back) to collect trepang, while the gins are
located in some joyous outlying islet to afford
a slight solace to the pirate in his hours of
ease. The sequel often consists of a sudden attack
on the kidnappers and a wild heaving overboard,
or else in a few desperate aboriginals essaying a
wild journey homewards in a fragile dingy.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defeet cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection-
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
At Melb. Criminal Court, on May 17, a
little girl of seven years was a witness for the
prosecution. Crown Prosecutor Walsh, Q.C.,
straightway put to this mite the theological ques-
tion : “ What do you mean by taking an oath ? ”
The mite replied : “ Kissing the Bible.” Then up
spake Holroyd, J. : “What is the Bible, my
girl ? ” and the poor little mite began to cry.
Again up spake the Judge : “ Don’t they teach
you these things in Sunday-school ? And don’t
they teach you what the Bible is? ” And the mite
cried even more. As bishops and Jews and Brad-
laughs and lots of other folk have not yet answered
the above questions, the putting of them to a
7-year-old girl savors of problem plays and
insanity.
A Vic. case of that rare disease, acromegaly,
in which the face-bones thicken even to lion-head
proportions, the lower jaw enlarges hugely, the
hands become spade-shaped, the fingers sausage-
like, and the feet the size and shape of a comic
policeman’s, is reported in the last Intercolonial
Medical Journal. This queer disease induces
abnormal sleepiness, like the “ sleeping sickness ”
of West Africa, and the patient can go to sleep
anywhere, anyhow, any time. The slightest exer-
tion also makes the sufferer sweat horribly. This,
too, is the disease which produces giants by exces-
sively lengthening their long bones, and at the
same time so weakens them that when the giant
is dead and post-mortemed and his bones are on
view, a small child’s handling snaps them as easliy
as a lolly-stick and crushes them like damp bread.
Writes “Ponte ” :
Dear Bulletin, —By what authority does the Sulphide
Corporation (Ashcroft process) pour the refuse from its
smelting works into Lake Macquarie, or, to be exact, into
Cockle Creek, a mile from its entrance into the lake ? The
idea is being assiduously spread that the directors are far
too long-headed to have sunk so much money without
making provision for such an obvious necessity as the
disposal of refuse, and assuring themselves against pos-
sible interference. As far as I know, however, no Enab-
ling Bill has been passed. The destruction of Lake
Macquarie as a fish-producing centre would be little short
of a national calamity, for over 10,000 baskets of fish—
approximately 350 tons —are sent away annually, In
Europe, experience has proved that smelting-works exer-
cise a highly deleterious influence upon the adjacent
waters, whether running or tidal; and practical men
assert that, if the pollution of the lake with chemicals in
solution be persisted in, its waters will soon be utterly
void of animal life. So far, the|only move in the matter
is a request from the Fisheries Commissioner for samples
of the water for analysis.
“ Drunks ” of the U.S. army, run in after
the usual pay-day spree (says Modern Medicine)
are treated as cases of alcoholic poisoning,
stomach-pumped right away, and have their
works washed out with warm 2 per cent, soda
solution. Thereafter, the delinquent gets a big
bowl of well-peppered warm beef extract, and is
allowed an hour’s rest; then he goes straight on
duty as good and well as L. M. Isitt or any other
waterspout. Certainly a counterblast to the Aus-
tralian police system of treating an unknown
drunk or apoplectic, which chiefly consists of
banging him on the head with a pair of handcuffs
or a handy footpath, and leaving him foodless in a
cold cell from Saturday afternoon, say, to Monday
morning. Then, when he dies, the symptoms are
“ plain drunk, yer Washup.”

Kangaroo versus Thistle .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dave Ambrose and I were mates on the Ash-
burton, but we had a big row and “divided
up. I started carrying. Dave took the whole
of my first load ; stuck a board outside his tent
“storekeeper, &o.”—the et cetera, of course, being
whisky, although there was a pub. on the field—
and struck out on a business line. His tent grew
with his trade, and just as he began to get things
going right there came a rival to him in the shape
(and not a bad shape, either) of a fine Australian
woman of thirty. And she was a widow, with a
little girl of three or four years old. Dave cursed
her.
Three days before Christmas, Australia fired the
first shot. It struck Dave’s eyes as he opened his
calico door a little after sunrise. There on a
placard at the door of the widow’s “store ” in
huge letters, he read :
“ Given away on Xmas to everybody, one tinned
plum-dun and a drink.
Dave took down a whisky advertisement, and
turning the back, printed “ All hands and the
cook; two drinks and a tin of fruit on Xmas.”
This he signed, and cutting from another adver-
tisement a thistle he pasted it above his name.
And, not to be outdone, the widow cut from an
old Bulletin the picture of a kangaroo and nailed
it to the mast, notwithstanding that the artist
had given the animal a round podgy face and an
eyeglass ! And she added to the duff a pipe—
Dave added tobacco. She added a penknife—
Dave gave a belt pouch. The diggers entered
into the spirit of the fight, and, could a little
orange have been introduced—Lord ! it would
have been glorious. Unfortunately, they were
both Presbyterians. One thing which annoyed
the widow greatly was that she could hardly keep
her little girl from Dave’s store ; for little Aggie
-yqr Agsin, as they called her—had taken a great
liking to her mother’s enemy.
Christmas Eve saw a crowd in front of the
widow’s door intent on a second placard (the
first was completely filled up) notifying that “ On
Xmas, and in addition to the things mentioned on
the other notice, anyone hard-up would receive a
week’s rations free!”
On this appearing Dave took down his notice
altogether, and there was a cheer for the kangaroo;
but in a quarter-of-an-hour a fresh notice appeared
on his door :—“ Everything free in my store on
Xmas and drinks on New Year’s Day.” “ Every-
thing free, Xmas and New Year,” replied the
widow. Then darkness fell on the field, and the
widow and Dave rested, and wiped their gore-
stained blades.
That night the widow prayed long and fer-
vently that Dave might wake no more ; while he
lay and smoked and thought what a pity it was
such a fine, big, plump, healthy woman— and a
pretty—should be at enmity with him. He was
fighting women and children ! Never mind; it
would be unnatural and inhuman to give in. And
poor, wee Agsin ! However, “Sufficient unto the
day,” muttered Dave, and put out his pipe and
slept.
When the rival stores opened on Christmas
morning there were few people about, despite the
enticmg placards. A buggy stood at the door of
tne pub.. Some half-dozen men had got it to visit)
a leader just discovered about ten miles out. The
norses had done nothing for five or six weeks, and
were a bit restless. But the men were in no
hurry It was Christmas, and there must be
another drink.”
As Dave Ambrose gazed abroad he saw little
Agsm toddle from the widow’s door, and, without
hesitation, shape her course for himself. When
about half-way over, however, the widow ap-
peared at the door and coo-eed for the youngster,
iv.-! undecided which way to go, sat down to
think it over—right in the dust of the track. The
widow sang out louder, and at the same time a
great roar from the pub. sent the horses into their
collars, and almost immediately they were in a
gallop, straight along the track. They were 30
yards from little Agsin when Dave was nearly 20.
u OU 7u d° it ? A dozen people held their
breath. He caught her, and with a rush the horses
passed.
Out of the dust and into his store went David,
a ' n one arm , an d the widow at his back,
through the store and into his own tent he ran,
a ud placed the little one on his bunk. Through
the store after him came the widow, and even the
tent couldn’t block her. Down on her knees
beside her girl she dropped, and smothered her
with kisses. Down on his knees dropped Dave,
and his right arm went round the widow’s waist
(his left was broken). He kissed Agsin, and he
kissed the widow. The widow—blinded, perhaps,
with tears—kissed away at random. And there,
sitting on the floor, they settled their bitter feud.
Christmas day was a free day at the stores. On
New Year’s Day the widow’s place was closed, and
three weeks later—although his arm was hardly
right—there were two bunks in David’s 12 x 14
tent; and one of them was Agsin’s.
D.B.G.
Boneyard badinage. A widow employed
as a grave-decorator at St. Kilda (Melb.) Ceme-
tery advertised lodging for Christian young men,
whereupon a monumental-mason and rival grave-
decorator asked the lone, lorn lady “whether she
intended to feed her young Christians on resurrec-
tion-pie.” This is one item in about three acres
of law proceedings.
Q. meat-inspectors may condemn beasts as
unfit to be slaughtered for human food, but
they have no power to destroy such diseased
beasts. Nobody knows which branch of which
department has such power. So condemned
beasts are simply turned back into a paddock, and
quietly cut up into “prime pasture beef” when
the inspector’s back is travelling away from the
slaughter-yards.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Fragrance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rain was beating against the slabs with the
deafening monotony of flood-time when the Crown
Lands ranger awoke in Sis Dempsey’s hut.
For the moment he could not locate himself.
The position he was lying in, the light in the door-
way, the slow ticking of the clock in the next
room, suggested an early turning-in at his own
home. Stretching himself wearily his legs lunged
off the makeshift couch into space.
Disillusioned, he got up in a vile temper with
Sis Dempsey. Not content with neglecting her
land and defying the legal conditions generally,
she must arrive just when he was comfortable for
the night, rousing him from a deep sleep and
forcing a long wet ride to Brogo upon him ! Her
offence seemed more heinous now than when he
had lighted on her tenantless hut some hours ago.
He was still fuming when the light came nearer,
glimmering through the chinks ; there was a swish
of wet skirts; and Sis Dempsey, holding a small tin
lamp in her hand, stood in the doorway.
The ranger and the girl looked at one another
and away again. There was neither screaming,
apologising, nor any attempt at greeting.
“By Jove, Miss Dempsey,” the ranger said,
looking pointedly round the cobwebby-dusty room.
“ I can’t strike your day at home. It beats me, I
cheerfully admit.”
He glanced carelessly again at her when she did
not reply; this time the misery of her condition
caught his eye.
. She was literally drenched. The water was fall-
mg in little pools at her feet. Her old habit clung
to her limbs, accentuating her sharp hip-bones and
sharper elbows. Her hat, “out of time and out
of space,” dripped steadily, and her fringe had
uncurled and was standing out like horns.
An exclamation escaped the ranger.
“You’re wringing wet!” he said. “Your
mother must be mad to send you here to-night.”
She put. the light on the table in an uncertain
way, and in a tremulous fashion put her hands to
her hair and before her face.
Standing silently there, with her face covered,
she suddenly burst into tears.
At that moment the ranger was far from looking
or feeling the hard nut he was considered. He
was conscience-stricken. The poor girl, unstrung
by a long ride in a heavy storm and by his in-
opportune appearance, met an effective sneer
stolidly, but broke down utterly at a rare faint
touch of kindliness !
He led her to a seat, made her drink some brandy
from his flask, and sat on the same box until her
sobs became quieter. At intervals he spoke sooth-
ingly and reassuringly. “You must cheer up,
Sis,” he said at last. “ I know it is confoundedly
hard for girls to live on their land with their
homes within riding distance. Yes, I heard you
were here the wet week. Listen ! This time I
won’t be hard on you. I’m off now to Brogo.
To-morrow afternoon I inspect this place. To-
night—we will agree to forget. I look to you to
manage things better in the future, though. I sup-
pose you’re pretty dull here,” he broke off, looking
round the bare, smoke-darkened room. He had
been reading of the intense desire of bush girls for
culture. For himself, he had seen no indication
of this yearning. Yet it might be that this awk-
ward, sensitive creature was consumed with lofty
aspirations and sublime dreams.
“ Have you any books ? ” he asked, abruptly.
“ No ; mother hates to see us readin’.”
“ Does she ? Well, you can read here. I have
some books at Brogo that I will bring you over.
Now, I’m off. My horse is in the shed. You saw
it there, I suppose ? ”
In the rain, stumbling his sleepy horse out of
the shed, with pouring blackness all round, the
ranger was inclined to call himself a fool, but he
changed his mind when he saw Sis on the veran-
dah, shading the flickering light from the wind,
and called out a cheery good-night instead.
When he was through the slip-rails Sis went in
and put the lamp down.
“ That devil of a Micky’s pawing done me a
good turn,” she said. “The smell of tobacco on
my hands made me cry.”
Q.
M. E. LLOYD.
Paradise.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said Adam to himself, “ I see
That Paradise is very good,
While Eve is free, and I am free,
To wander each where either would.”
But Eve reflected, “I can see
(Though Paradise is very good)
That Adam’s free—but, as for me,
I wouldn’t wander if I could ! ”
When Eden’s voice breathed low and sweet
(Ah ! Paradise was very good)
She gently led his foolish feet
Until without the gates they stood.
Oh, hideous world of fire and ice !
She called him hubby—said he would
Eind matrimony rather nice
(But Paradise was very good).
J. HAY.
The latest X-ray story has its point deli-
cately wrapped in cotton-wool by the Lancet,
which sets forth that while a six-foot fluorescent
screen was being experimented with in mixed
company, a lady happened to get into the line of
light, and, “as dress-materials are very transpar-
ent to the rays,” she was suddenly projected full-
length upon, the screen so apparently in the
“altogether” that “an embarrassment was
created almost beyond the power of words to
express.”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ;5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

A Song of Fighters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve a toast I want to offer,
And we’ll drink it to the lees —
A toast to some who’ve stayed at home
And some who’ve crossed the seas,
To some who’ve fought and fallen,
And to some who’ve fought and won,
To some who still are fighting
And whose battle isn’t done :
“ To those who make, to those who take,
Or lift a livelihood,
To the men who face the music
When the music isn't good / ”
You will find them in the city,
With half-hid out-elbows air,
Still going, sternly smiling,
Though the good luck isn’t there,
With the ink upon their coat-seams,
And a blacking-polished hat,
But a fixed determination
That they’ll play off their own bat.
“ To the men who fight and fear not,
Be they in or out the Hood,
To the men who face the music
When the music isn't good / ”
You will find them on the out tracks
With a solemn gritty grin,
Pecksniff’s alms and tracts refusing,
Though the belly aches within.
You will find them on world fringes
With the unknown for a goal,
With a huge belief in courage
And a wee belief in soul.
Where there’s work that wants the doing,
There they’ve always fought and stood :
“ To the men ivho face the music
When the music isn’t good / ”
And when it comes to dying,
As they lived, so do they die,
With their backs to old Earth Mother
And their faces to the sky,
Unscared by fools or sages,
To the world’s clouts dourly dumb,
Dying game and dying biting,
All unscared by what may come.
“ To the battlers of the ages,
Who with back to wall have stood,
To the men who face the music
When the music isn't good 1 ”
STEELE GREY.
The Bulletin notes with interest that
there is one real Oriental potentate left. The
Sultan of Zanzibar’s bandmaster had reason to
complain of his musicians—expecting some slight
punishment to be awarded ; but the Sultan ordered
three of them to be blinded, one to have his tongue
split, and another his wrist broken. The conduct
of the rest is now unexceptionable.
The Seventh Commandment.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A WOMAN was riding quickly across a rough, half-
cleared paddock. She was seated sideways on a
man’s saddle, and kept her balance with the ease
born of custom. Here and there the bracken was
knee-deep, and the ground was strewn with logs
and stumps. On two sides stretched heavy forest;
on the other two, the country swept away in
gentle undulations, heavily wooded, till it merged
with a low line of hills into the blue horizoif.
About the woman the few hundred acres of clear-
ing looked raw and unsightly—new fences, black-
ened trees and logs, stretches of land littered with
felled trees and scrub, drying for a burn. Every-
where the first desolation of clearing. Plights of
cockatoos passed shrieking from one dead tree to
another. Away near the creek bushy peppermint
saplings caught from the setting sun brilliant tints
of red and green on their new shoots.
The horse stopped abruptly at the slip-rails.
His rider pulled them down without dismounting,
and urged him into a brisk canter along the wind-
ing track, which lay in heavy shadow for a mile or
so, and then opened out into clearer country,
where tall, straight gums were wreathed for their
seventy or eighty feet of height with new bushy
sprouts, looking in the uncertain light like a
huge fantastic hop-garden. Here and there, in
sudden hollows, the air was almost cold, and
strong with the scent of hazel and dogwood.
* » * *
Suddenly there was the sound of rapid hoofs ap-
proaching. The woman pulled up, and her hand
went quickly to her hair to arrange a straggling
lock, thence to her neck to straighten her tie, and
so with quick feminine touches to belt and skirt.
Her heart was throbbing fast, her eyes were
bright, but her thin lips and square chin were set
firmly.
Round theturn of the road appeared a man, who
drew up hastily as he saw her, and so close that
their hands met instantly.
“ I got your letter yesterday,” she said.
He smiled, and gazed into her face. “ It is two
months since I saw you, but you are coming away
with me now.”
She drew her hand away from him. “ No, I am
not coming.”
“ Not coming ? Mary ! ”
“I would have written to you,” she went on,
“ but I had not your address. But I am not going
with you.”
“ Not going ! You mean not this moment, but
in a day or two ”
“I don’t mean that; I mean that I am never
going away with you.”
“ Never ! Good God ! what have I worked and
waited for; what have I thought of night and
day, but you, always you. Of your coming to me,
of our going away to forget all the past, and begin
life again.”
“Ah!” she drew her breath sharply, “ don t.
I must not think of that; it is all over now.”
He looked at her in silence, and she met his
eyes, and saw a stern look which she knew.
“ Everything is changed now—l cannot go
away. Alick was struck by a falling tree a day
or two after you left, and he’ll never be able to
walk again. The doctor says he will grow even
more helpless as the years go by. ” Her tone was
even and dull.
“ And you—you are to stay and nurse him and
work for him ;is that what you mean? „
She nodded. “ What else could Ido ?
“ Couldn’t he be got into a hospital or
“And leave his wife free to elope with another
man,” she interrupted, with a jarring laugh.
• * » *
He laid his hand on her sleeve. “He has beaten
and kicked you ; drunk or sober he ill-used you.
What duty do you owe him ? You love me, an
I could make you happy. Come, dear .
She looked away from him into the wide,
darkening landscape. “No, I will not com
there is only me to look after him. lam y g
and strong, and whatever you may say, it is y
dl “'lt is not,” he cried, passionately ; “ you love
me, and gave me your promise. Did he ever sho
you any consideration ? Were you better treate
than his wretched dog?” , .
“ Hush ! ” she interrupted, sharply. Isn tit
hard enough without all that? You love me,
don’t make it harder. lam not going to see you
again. You must go away. I know what I have
to do, and I will do it. I have thought of you
she went on, slowly; “but you are a man, and
things will right themselves to you.
“ Possibly,” he returned, roughly.
A crescent moon shone in the amethyst sky, a
big star below showed faintly in a golden setting ;
the whole earth had grown dim, and there was a
subdued, comfortable twitter from innumerable
little birds in the scrub, and far away the distant,
deep bellow of a lonely bull.
He leant towards her and took her hand. i-
can’t let you do it, Mary. You are mine, and 1
will not let you go back to that brute.”
Her fingers tightened on his. “I am going to do
it. It is hard, but there is nothing else to be
done. Who but I can look after him, feed him,
nurse him ? ”
* * * *
He realised, entirely against his will, that she
was in earnest, that no pleading could turn her.
It was brought home to him in the slow, steady
tones of her voice, in her whole quiet manner, in
her steadfast eyes. As her fingers lay in his, he
felt a wild impulse to lift her from the saddle and
carry her away, making her his, and freeing her
from her hateful bondage.
“ How will you live ? ” There was a faint hope
that she would let him assist her—that he might
come and work for her.
She sighed: “ That is the easiest part. lam as
strong as a horse. Our land is good, and nearly
all cleared. Out-door work I have always liked,
and one thinks less in the open air. In the night
time one is too tired to care what happens—a
broken heart doesn’t matter. A fair allowance of
sleep is what one longs for most.”
He looked at her half wondering. He would
tempt her no further, and yet there was the re-
membrance of their last meeting, when she had
clung to him sobbing. Passion burnt in his veins,
and desire struggled with purer love.
“ Go, then !” he said, thickly. “ I understand
you; but I love you, and, my God! it is only
because I love you that I let you go.” Then he
kissed her cheek. “I love you, and you are the
best woman on God’s earth. May He help you !
I can’t; I’d only hurt you.”
She bent and kissed the hand that was tight on
her wrist. He drew it away hastily and roughly.
“Don’t! Go quickly. I understand you, but I
love you.”
Her breath came in a thick sob; then she turned
hastily, and soon the sound of her horse’s hoofs
was lost on the dusty track.
S.G.G.
Sunday.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s one day in the week! The rest don’t
count:
Mere padding for the space from day to day.
One day I live. Betwixt the days I dream
Of things she said—and meant, but did not say;
Of sweetest truths by trembling kisses told ;
Of clinging arms, soft eyes half-shy, half bold ;
Of all that happened on the last bright day—
Our walk beside the river—o’er the hills ;
Our good-byes at the nightfall—then the ride
Across the plain—to Loneliness that kills.
Ah, sweet —there soon will be no need to say
Good Night!
Then will each dear dead week count seven days'
delight.
Vic.
L.M D.
H. “An Anglican clergyman informs me
that the Church Army is positively billed for Aus-
tralia, after the Lambeth Conference this year.
It will shake up the Salvationists a bit, though a
palpable imitation —merely the thing washed,
shaved, and put into clean linen. It is, compara-
tively, an Argus to an Age.”
Parkes used to tell a story about the insane
time when the N.S.W. Soudan Contingent was
arming in hot haste and laying in a stock of hair-
restorer, jam, tracts, grog, and other necessaries
for the field of gore. One morning, when the war-
fever was at its height, a lot of influential Sydney
citizens were ascending the stairs to the Colonial
Secretary’s office to see him about the construc-
tion of a certain railway, when suddenly a great
din was heard above, and the Minister came rush-
ing down in wild excitement. Waving the visitors
aside, he fairly shrieked: “ It’s no use coming
here. I can’t possibly see you now. I can’t see
anybody or do anything until we have rescued the
dear old mother country from this awful danger !’
The Port Douglas (Q.) P.M. endeavored to
coerce a Kanaka lady, over 60 years of age, to go
back to her lawful husband, whom she had de-
serted for a young buck of about 20. The P.M.
made out an “ Order,” and then put the official
stamp in every clear space available on the paper,
it being a legend that Kanakas gauge the value of
an official document by the number of stamps that
it bears. But the device didn’t work this time.
Lucy was too enamoured of her 20 - year - old
to care a pin for all the Governments and stamps
on the planet. . . Previous to this, Lucy had
asked the P.M. for a divorce, on the ground that
she wanted “ nuther feller—young feller.” Being
refused, she “ eloped.” Then the old man, Soondi,
waited on the P.M. and said he was willing to let
20-year-old have Lucy, provided he (Soondi) was
paid .£4O by the dusky Paris.
